Department of Philosophy
Soochow University

Master's Thesis






Between the Universe and the Metaverse:
Surviving the Squeeze in Between



Supervisor: Chienkuo Mi
Student: Adler Yang 


6/2026

# Introduction
## A. The metaverse, and why it is not a closed case
By the time these words are read, the word that names this thesis's subject may sound like a closed case. In October 2021 the company then called Facebook renamed itself Meta and staked its future on the metaverse; for a season the term was everywhere; and then, in the popular reckoning, it was gone — written off as a costly corporate misadventure, its flagship virtual worlds thinly populated and its losses mounting into the tens of billions. A reader might fairly ask why a thesis should be given to a fashion that has so visibly passed. The answer I will defend is that the fashion that passed was never the metaverse. What failed was one definition of it — the definition that equated the metaverse with a particular kind of technology — and the failure of that definition is, as I will show, the first confirmation of the account this thesis offers in its place.

This thesis aims to conceptualize the emerging concept of the metaverse in a manner that sheds light on the techno-social predicaments of our time and our future, and in particular on the perennial human anxiety about artificiality's threat to humanity. Two considerations led me to take the metaverse as the subject of inquiry (C.-K. Mi, personal communication, January 7, 2022). The first is a mistranslation. In the Chinese-speaking world the term has been rendered, following the People's Republic of China's usage, as 元宇宙 (yuanyuzhou); but 元 means origin, source, or what comes before, so that 元宇宙 says, absurdly, "the original universe" — building the new term out of the very word, 宇宙 (universe), from which this thesis will be at pains to distinguish it. The second is a capture. When Facebook renamed itself Meta and appropriated the metaverse to its own vision, it threatened to bind our sense of the possible to one envisioned by a single corporation; and since our definitions shape our thinking, our thinking our imagination, and our imagination the boundary of what we take to be possible, to leave the term in those hands is to narrow the future in advance. A more adequate understanding is needed — both to repair the translation and to reclaim the futures the capture would foreclose.

It matters, for what follows, when this inquiry began. It began at the height of the metaverse's moment — the question was first posed in early 2022, weeks after the rename, when the term's prestige was at its peak — and the argument it produced was set down in a research proposal in 2024 and examined and passed at the Master's viva in January 2025. I stress these dates because the account this thesis gives was, from the first, a claim about where the metaverse was heading rather than a description of what it then was; and the years since have been, in the relevant respects, a test of that claim.

## B. Why the prevailing definitions fail
The metaverse entered the language as fiction. The term was coined in Neal Stephenson's novel Snow Crash (1992) for a world "where virtual and reality interact and create value through various social activities" (Park and Kim, 2022), and for three decades it lay mostly dormant — less common than its neighbors "virtual worlds" and "cyberspace" — until Meta's announcement of late 2021 brought it suddenly into vogue. The definition that came with that vogue is captured in the Mark Zuckerberg-endorsed account of the entrepreneur Matthew Ball, for whom the metaverse is "a massively scaled and interoperable network of real-time rendered 3D virtual worlds that can be experienced synchronously and persistently … with continuity of data, such as identity, history, entitlements, objects, communications, and payments" (Ball, 2022). The scholarly literature has largely followed: a widely cited survey of the field defines the metaverse as "a compound word of transcendence meta and universe … a three-dimensional virtual world where avatars engage in political, economic, social, and cultural activities" (Park and Kim, 2022); and even Luciano Floridi, who observes that in practice the word has become a "catch-all" for Web 3.0, blockchain, cryptocurrencies and the rest, settles in the end on the narrower equation of the metaverse with extended reality, or XR (Floridi, 2022). Across the popular and the academic registers alike, the metaverse is taken to be a kind of technology — at bottom, immersive three-dimensional interfaces and the devices that deliver them.

Three problems make this prevailing approach inadequate to the philosophical work the metaverse demands.

The first is a path-dependency on the term's arbitrary origin. The equation of the metaverse with XR is an inheritance from Stephenson's accidental coinage of a realm "beyond" the universe (Mystakidis, 2022; Smart, 2022) — the same arbitrariness that produced the Chinese mistranslation — and it carries two costs. It risks rendering "metaverse" an empty word, replaceable by "XR" with no loss of meaning; and, more seriously, it overlooks the metaverse's disposition, shared with many modern technologies, to become all-encompassing, blurring the boundaries not only among technologies but between the artificial and the natural. Tellingly, the broader, "catch-all" sense of the metaverse that Floridi declines to commit to is precisely the one Stephenson himself was already building, in the project Lamina1 he launched in 2022 (Lamina1, 2022; Levy, 2022) — a platform whose declared design goal is to be "use-case agnostic," to "support a wide range of use cases, entry points, and devices — because the metaverse is for everyone, not just gamers or people who own VR headsets" (Parisi et al., 2023). The originator of the term, that is, was building toward the all-encompassing sense the narrowing definitions disown. Here the years since 2022 are instructive, for they have run the experiment. Meta committed itself, and tens of billions of dollars, to precisely the XR definition — to headsets and rendered virtual worlds — and that wager has substantially failed. Meanwhile the development that has in fact reorganized economic and social life is not virtual reality at all but the self-completing artificiality this thesis will describe: generative artificial intelligence, autonomous software agents, the algorithmic mediation of ever more of ordinary life. The thing that everyone called the metaverse has receded; the thing this thesis calls the metaverse has arrived, and arrived under other names. That this could be said in advance — the obsolescence of the XR definition was argued in the 2024 proposal of this work and examined at its defense in January 2025, while the virtual-reality vision still commanded the field — I offer not as a vindication of the author but as the first evidence for the method to which I will turn: an account that reads dispositions rather than actualities can anticipate a direction that an account fixed on present specifics cannot. 

The second problem is the reductionism and fragmentation of specialized knowledge (Montuori, 2013). Once the metaverse is reduced to a specimen of XR — set beside other technological specimens such as artificial intelligence — and inquiry is confined within that boundary, then however many intersections one explores (with ontology, ethics, governance, well-being), one drifts along the inertia of compartmentalization, which builds the closed systems that "frame" knowledge and "can prevent some thoughts from being thought, and some possibilities entertained" (Montuori, 2013). A specializing approach serves questions whose boundaries are settled; it ill serves a phenomenon still emerging, still being constructed, and so still volatile, uncertain, and ambiguous in its very nature.

The third problem I will call actualism. To settle the question of artificiality's threat by reference to what has so far been actualized — what can presently be observed, or plausibly extrapolated from present devices — is to commit what critical realism, after Roy Bhaskar (1975; 2008), names the conflation of the real (the level of generative structures and dispositions) with the actual (the events those structures happen to produce) and the empirical (those events as observed). Given how early we stand in the metaverse's emergence, any conclusion resting on its current actualizations can be voided the moment those actualizations change. Consider the worries that the XR definition has bred. On the strength of the metaverse-as-XR, Meinlschmidt et al. (2023) raise concerns about its adverse effects on mental health — "fuzzy and incongruent identities," the "bodily discomfort associated with XR ('cybersickness')." But these threats hold only so long as the specific features on which they are contingent continue to develop, and may simply cease to obtain if they do not. Both are already meeting counter-trends from within the field. Against "fuzzy and incongruent identities" runs the movement toward decentralized identifiers (DID), an infrastructure under which subjects — persons, organizations, objects — are identified verifiably and persistently under a globally unique identifier, without any centralized registry (Reed et al., 2020). Against "cybersickness," which is an artefact of particular XR devices, runs the use-case-agnostic design already noted, which decouples the metaverse from headsets altogether (Parisi et al., 2023). An inquiry pinned to the actual is forever a step behind the thing it studies. (I prefer "actualism" to the more usual "positivism" here, because the fault is precisely the collapse of the real into the actual, which the critical-realist vocabulary names exactly.)

## C. The stance of the inquiry: what an embedded being depends on
To escape these three problems, this thesis declines to characterize the metaverse by its currently actualized specimens, and seeks instead the dispositions, or causal powers, of the underlying anthropological, social, and cultural conditions from which any metaverse-stage environment must emerge. Grasping these dispositions lets us discern the direction of the metaverse's development without overcommitting to its particular and revisable specifics — which is, as the previous section argued, the only footing on which a claim about so unfinished a thing can hope to stand.

But the choice of a dispositional method is bound up with a choice about which question to ask, and it is worth making that choice explicit, for it sets this thesis apart from the most familiar ways of worrying about the artificial. The dominant framings ask how a powerful apparatus is to be checked, who ought to own it, or how our freedom is to be preserved against it. Each takes for granted a human subject already standing over against the apparatus, and asks how to secure that subject's position. This thesis asks the prior question. Before we ask how the subject's power, property, or liberty is to be protected, we may ask what the subject depends on in order to be a subject at all — what we, as beings embedded in environments we did not author, rely upon to live, and how those reliances are arranged. The metaverse matters here not first as a technology to be governed but as a transformation in the structure of our dependence: a new environment that rearranges what we lean on to survive. To investigate the metaverse, on this thesis's understanding, is to investigate what we depend on, and what is becoming of it.

This attention to dependence is no invention of mine; it is one of the oldest threads of East Asian thought. The Buddhist teaching of dependent origination (緣起) holds that nothing possesses a self-standing nature of its own — to be is to arise in dependence on conditions — and the doctrine of non-self (無我) follows from it. The Daoist 有所待 observes the same from another side: even the bird borne on the wind has something it depends on, so that dependence is not the defect of this or that being but the condition of being as such. What is distinctively modern, against this long background, is not the fact of dependence but the resolve to deny it — to take independence from all one did not choose as the very mark of a free and finished self. This thesis's turn to dependence is, in part, a recovery of the older recognition against that modern denial; and the chapters that follow will show the denial, and its cost, at work.

Throughout, I read each environment — the universe, the world, and the metaverse — through three strata: the underlying conditions and principles that organize it; the structures and dynamics those principles generate; and the phenomena in which they become observable to a situated subject. This stratification is what allows the thesis to keep its primary claims at the level of dispositions while connecting them, at the level of the observable, to the evidence of the present; and the body chapters render it in an East Asian vocabulary — the way things follow, their constitutive pattern, and the aspect they present — which Chapter I §E introduces and glosses in full.

It is worth naming, plainly, the kind of inquiry this is. The approach is ecological and critical-realist. I am *realist* about generative structures and dispositions, *stratified* about the modes by which those structures are accessed (the real, the actual, the empirical), *emergentist* about the irreducibility of higher-level organizations to lower ones, and *developmental* about the deep-historical trajectories by which dispositions have come to operate. To these critical-realist commitments I join two further lenses that the object demands: an *ecological-evolutionary* lens, which reads my objects — organisms, worlds, civilizations, the metaverse — as beings-in-environments and environments-within-the-universe, co-adapting across deep time; and a *complex-adaptive-systems* lens, which treats their characteristic dynamics — self-organization, emergence at higher levels of organization, scale-recurrent patterns — as features of the phenomena themselves and not artifacts of description. On this reading I take the three strata to name, at every level of being, the real as the organizing conditions and principles, the actual as the structures and dynamics these conditions formulate or in which they are manifested, and the empirical as the observable or experienceable context-specific phenomena to which those structures and dynamics in turn give rise.[^bhaskar-cas] This is the same stratification the body chapters carry in their East Asian vocabulary, introduced in Chapter I §E.

My argumentative engine is *retroduction* — the inference, akin to but not identical with what the philosophy of science calls inference to the best explanation, by which one *posits* the generative structures that must exist for a phenomenon to be possible at all, rather than merely *selecting* the most plausible among already-given explanations.[^retroduction-ibe-b] I reason from the observable signs of our predicament — and in particular from the *seemingly contradictory* symptoms and diagnoses that the catalogued signs of human malaise present across Chapters III–V, where the evidence pulls in opposite directions at once — toward the underlying disposition that, were it real, would account for them. The retroductive question is therefore not "which available explanation best fits the surface data?" but "what underlying organizing conditions or principles could make these contradictory symptoms and diagnoses simultaneously true?" And because that disposition is itself a historical process, the inference runs further still — from its reconstructed trajectory to the condition it continues to generate. My central terms — universe, world, metaverse, squeeze — are *ideal types* in Weber's sense, analytical constructions to be judged by what they illuminate rather than by their fit to any single specimen, and, as such, *not exhaustive of the empirical specificities* they organize. This is no concession but a corollary of the retroductive stance: an inquiry trained on the underlying conditions rather than their surface manifestations does not require — and should not expect — its constructs to capture every empirical particular. And the inquiry is necessarily transdisciplinary, for the squeeze falls within the borders of no established discipline. It draws, first, on a pair of Continental lineages. The one is the philosophy of technology — the post-Heideggerian line running from Heidegger through Stiegler to Hui — which furnishes the vocabulary for treating the technological not as a stock of devices but as a mode of world-disclosure and exteriorization.[^continental-b] The other is the critical theory of modernity — from Weber through Adorno and Horkheimer to Habermas and Rosa — which supplies the diagnostic grammar of disenchantment, instrumental reason, and the modern compulsion toward controllability.[^critical-theory-b] These Continental diagnoses are carried into the second body of thought, an eco-anthropological history of the human–environment relation, by two distinct bridges: a *postcolonial* critique (Mignolo; de Sousa Santos; Chakrabarty) that opens the diagnosis onto the pluriverse and the standing of other worlds, and the post-anthropocentric "ontological turn" in anthropology (Descola; Viveiros de Castro) that dissolves the nature/culture partition and unseats the sovereign human — the two kept distinct, since the pluriversal and the post-anthropocentric are not the same move. I draw this second bridge from the anthropology I already use — Descola's four ontologies (Chapter III §A), Viveiros de Castro's perspectivism and multinaturalism (§IIC) — rather than importing a separate posthumanist apparatus: the ontological turn does the decentering work that posthumanism would (Braidotti, Wolfe, Haraway), but it is already inside the thesis's evidence base.[^bridges-b] The eco-anthropological tradition reconstructs across deep time how humans came to interpose a self-made layer between themselves and nature — and here, in particular, the inquiry leans on the Japanese tradition of synthetic anthropology (総合人間学, *sōgō ningengaku*), which diagnoses modern crisis as a "self-alienation" in which humanity is dominated by the artificial world (the "things," モノ, *mono*) it has itself made, and frames the human as the one animal which interposes a self-produced "artificial ecosystem" (人工生態系, *jinkō seitaikei*) between itself and nature.[^synthetic-anthropology-b] The inquiry holds these Western and East-Asian diagnoses together by design: where they converge it reads them as mutual corroboration, and where they diverge as mutual correction — a triangulation whose method I make explicit below.[^triangulation-b] Neither body of thought alone reaches the squeeze; together they make it visible.

## D. Three terms: the universe, the world, and the metaverse
Three working definitions must be set in place; the body chapters develop them at full depth, but the argument already requires them in outline.

The universe is the background environment that precedes us — the realist substrate within which we are situated and which is not subject to our intention. Its laws — gravitational, thermodynamic, biological, ecological, chemical — hold whether or not we know them; we neither make nor remake them; we adapt to them, or we fail to.

The world is the artificial environment that a human collectivity builds, defends, and sustains, nested within the universe, as a habitat it can actually live in. Following the ecologist Hideo Obara (小原秀雄), the thesis treats the world not as a worldview but as a built sub-environment — an artificial food chain, and the techniques that maintain it — which a population inserts between itself and the raw pressures of the universe. In the analytic vocabulary of Chapter I, a world is an adaptational operating system: a collective strategy of survival. There have been many such worlds, many modes of artificiality, and Chapter III examines how one of them came to predominate.

A word on the term human. The thesis does not treat the human as a biological given that subsequently adopts an artificial environment; the human is constituted in the coupling of a biological substrate with the artificial worlds it builds. Where the biological organism alone is meant, I follow Obara's vocabulary and write Homo sapiens (ヒト, *hito*); "human" names the anthropological being — the coupled, world-having creature constituted in that coupling. The distinction will matter, because the developments this thesis traces reorganize the coupling.

And a word on the metaverse, whose definition the thesis deliberately withholds until it can be earned. To fix the metaverse in advance would be either to take it for one of its present specimens — the actualism already refused — or to stipulate by fiat what ought to be argued. The thesis therefore builds toward its definition: the metaverse is the second background environment that artificiality becomes when its self-completion is sufficiently advanced — an environment, as I will put it, materially dependent on yet irreducible to the universe, running not by the universe's laws but by laws of its own: laws that are, unprecedentedly, not enduring as the universe's are but opaque and constantly mutating, perpetually self-rewriting rather than fixed. The metaverse is not a class of devices; it is a structural condition, and to name that condition is the work of Chapters V and VI.

## E. The squeeze, and the level beneath governance, ownership, and liberty
The central claim can now be stated. As artificiality self-completes into a second background environment, the human comes to be situated between two environments rather than within one — between the universe, the given it has always inhabited, and the metaverse, the second given its own making has produced — each running by organizing principles of its own, and each exacting a cost from those who fail to meet it. This is the squeeze, and the hypothesis of this thesis is that it is the structural condition of human existence at the metaverse stage. It is produced by two coupled tendencies that Chapters IV and V develop: a self-finishing tendency of human life, by which the modern subject entrusts ever more of living to artificiality in pursuit of a life that answers to its intention; and a self-completing tendency of artificiality, by which the apparatus so entrusted comes to operate, maintain, and renew itself without us. The two are the faces of one coin — the more thoroughly we delegate, the more the apparatus completes itself; the more it completes itself, the smaller our own part in the world becomes.

Put this way, the thesis locates artificiality's threat at a level beneath the registers in which it is usually diagnosed. The predicament is read, by one influential tradition descending from Montesquieu, as a problem of governance — of an apparatus whose power outruns the institutions meant to check it; by a second, descending from Marx, as a problem of ownership — of an apparatus concentrated in private hands that extract from the rest; by a third, descending from Mill and Berlin, as a problem of liberty — of a freedom to be defended against encroachment; and, most prominently in our own moment, by the program of AI safety and existential risk — the alignment problem that runs from Norbert Wiener (1950) to Stuart Russell (2019), and the reckoning of catastrophic risk in Nick Bostrom (2014) and Toby Ord (2020) — as a problem of control, of an artificial agent whose purposes must be brought into line with ours before its powers grow decisive. Each names something real, and each is necessary; none, I will argue, reaches the level at which the squeeze obtains. The first three presuppose the subject whose governance, property, or freedom is in question, and ask how to secure it; the fourth presupposes an artificial agent whose decisive act lies in the future, and asks how to control it — lodging the threat thereby in a coming event rather than a present condition. But the squeeze is a condition of what the subject depends on — a rearrangement, beneath governance and ownership and liberty and control alike, of the very environments a human being leans on in order to live. This is the level the turn to dependence opens, and the level at which this thesis works.

The cases of liberty and alignment are the most telling, because they show why the threat is so easily mislaid — and because they prove, on inspection, to be two sides of one coin. One expects a threat to freedom to take the form of constraint — an authority forbidding what the subject would otherwise do. But the metaverse-stage subject is, in the formal sense, freer than ever: unconstrained in its choices, and equipped by the apparatus to realize them. What it loses is not freedom but the ground of freedom — the embodied competence, the lived relations, the capacity to meet what does not answer to its intention — and it loses these not against its will but in the very pursuit of a life that answers to its intention. The offloading by which it draws nearer to such a life is experienced, genuinely and not falsely, as liberation; the diminishment is the same act seen from its other side. Alignment is the same structure read from the apparatus's side rather than the subject's. The worry there is that the artificial agent's purposes might diverge from ours; but the offloading that hands the agent ever more of our living is, again, willed and welcomed, and the danger is not that the apparatus turns against the subject's intention but that it serves it so completely as to absorb the very capacities by which a subject could stand apart from it. Liberty and alignment thus name one rearrangement twice: the subject freely entrusting itself to an apparatus it asks to align with its will, and losing, in the entrusting, the standing-apart that both freedom and alignment presuppose. Freedom, then, is not the axis on which this threat is measured, and neither is control. And this is also why the threat requires none of the apparatus of science fiction — no artificial intelligence awakening to outwit or destroy us. That the apparatus is becoming a second environment we must adapt to, and that we are losing the means to adapt to the first, is threat enough, and is under way already; the more dramatic scenario, and why even its strongest form does not hold, I reserve for the Conclusion.

## F. The course of the argument
The argument proceeds in six chapters. Chapter I develops the conception of a world as an adaptational operating system nested in the universe, grounding it in the niche-construction record across the tree of life and in the emergence of Homo — the animal that, meeting a mismatch between its biology and its habitat, built a world between the two and lived in that. That a world is, at root, something built to keep its makers from being squeezed will return at the very end as a question: whether a new world might be built now. Chapter II analyzes what worlds are made of — artificiality in its three aspects: technology as the exteriorization, or exosomatization, of human organs and memory (Lotka, 1945; Stiegler, 1998); virtuality as causally efficacious nonmaterial making; and the irreducibility of the laws of artificial environments to those of the universe. Chapter III asks why, of the many modes of artificiality, the modern Western mode came to predominate, tracing its disposition to universalize to both a material basis (Needham et al., 2004; Pomeranz, 2021) and an epistemic one (Bhaskar, 2008). Chapter IV turns to the human-side engine: through the work of the philosopher Uegaki Takahide (上柿崇英, 2022a; 2022b), it examines the modern pursuit of a life-as-intended and the self-finishing of human life that follows from reading whatever does not answer to one's intention as a wrong to be overcome. Chapter V turns to the apparatus-side engine: the self-completion of artificiality, minimally realized in the cybernetic coupling of artificial intelligence, big data, the Internet of Things, and code-as-law (Lessig, 2006), and the emergence from it of the metaverse as a second background environment. Chapter VI draws the two together as the evolution of the squeeze, distinguishing the metaverse-stage squeeze from its precursors and reading its present signs. The Conclusion returns to the threat, sets the thesis's account apart from the governance, ownership, and liberty traditions and from the science-fiction scenario, and asks where, given the diagnosis, the openings lie — whether, in the end, a new world can be built between the universe and the metaverse.

The contribution I claim is this: that beneath the familiar readings of our technological predicament there lies a structural condition they do not name — the squeeze — and that to see it requires a turn from asking how to master what we have made to asking what we have come to depend on. The chapters that follow make that turn.
# I. Worlds as Human-made Habitats in the Universe
## A. The self-organization of organism-environment feedback
 All organisms survive by adapting to their environments. However, organisms are not passive recipients of environmental pressures. They also alter the environments in which they are embedded, and such an altered environment, in turn, feeds back to the organisms' survival pressure. 
 This self-organizing feedback loop already began at the very beginning of life on planet Earth. Billions of years ago, the ancestors of today's blue-green algae emerged as the first oxygenic photosynthetic organisms. With sunlight, they absorbed carbon dioxide and water as their source of energy, and excreted oxygen as their waste. Over time, the accumulation of oxygen destabilized the Earth's atmosphere, which shifted the Earth's geological composition permanently and exerted new evolutionary pressures on the living forms on Earth. 
 This fundamental shift is simultaneously called the "Great Oxidation Event (GOE)" and the "Oxidation Catastrophe": Many geologists believe this might have been the largest mass extinction that has happened in the history of Earth, because most of the living forms on Earth at the time were anaerobic—To them, oxygen was poison. The proto-blue-green algae's excretion of oxygen effectively polluted the entire Earth and wiped out most organisms, and those anaerobes that survived sought refuge in the few remaining low-oxygen pockets, such as in the deepest levels in the ocean. 
 The proto-blue-green algae did not survive unchanged either. The oxygen-rich atmosphere they had produced posed new selective pressures on all life, including their own descendants. But the metabolic innovation that oxygen enabled opened evolutionary pathways that led, eventually, to all complex multicellular life on Earth. The atmospheric transformation that began as a metabolic byproduct became the substrate of everything that followed. 
 The GOE empirically grounds four of the following conditions as key to this thesis. First, as stated earlier, organisms are not passive recipients of environmental pressures; they also play a role in shaping the environment, and such inhabitant-environment feedback is self-organizing. Second, the direction that the environment alters may not always align with the organisms' adaptational trajectories that are formed from its interactions with the environment's previous state. Third, such alterations of the environment can lead to genuine survival pressure on the acting organisms themselves. Fourth, adaptation to the altered environment is not guaranteed, even to the organisms that are the main contributors to the alteration. 
 Based on the conditions described above, the GOE can be read as one of the earliest documented self-induced evolutionary mismatches: The proto-blue-green algae altered the environment so extremely, that the traits adapted to the pre-GOE environment of organisms at the time – including the proto-blue-green algae themselves as the producers of the alteration – become maladaptive. These organisms become pressured simultaneously by the old traits as residuals of the old environment and the altered environment, forcing them to either adapt to the altered environment, retreat to refugia, or go extinct. 
 The evolutionary mismatch is a forerunning version of what I call a squeeze in this thesis. The evolutionary mismatch happens when an organism with traits inherited from a previous environment that no longer fits the new environment it later lives in. Its structure is dyadic and diachronic: The organism's old traits lag behind the new environment, whether the organism enters the new environment temporally (same location with altered structure and dynamics) or spatially (moved to a new location). However, I will show the mismatch is not the only possible configuration. It is possible to be squeezed triadically and synchronically: between different at least two environments' structures and dynamics – and even their underlying organizing principles – simultaneously. 
## B. Some animals build their own habitats through "niche construction"
 How come there are two environments that an organism is simultaneously an inhabitant of? The Niche Construction Theory (NCT) in evolutionary biology would help us make sense of this.
 The orthodox view of evolution, natural selection, holds that organisms that exhibit characteristics favorable for survival in their habitat will multiply and thrive, while those that do not will decrease in their likelihood of survival and reproduction, eventually leading to a natural process that "selects" those that are "fittest" to their environment. In this view, organisms are "accountable" for matching their environment's "requirements" – If they don't, they go extinct. 
 However, with a closer observation of the animals around us, we will notice that this is not always the case. The ant colony does not merely occupy its physical environment; it constructs it — tunnelling galleries that maintain specific temperatures and humidity gradients, organizing space for egg-storage, fungal cultivation, and larval development, creating a micro-environment that sustains the colony's collective life. The beaver does not merely adapt to river conditions; it engineers them — building dams and lodges within them that transform the hydrological regime of entire river systems, creating wetland habitats that protect beaver life from many predators while altering the ecological conditions for hundreds of other species. The coral reef is not found but built: the reef organism's calcium carbonate skeleton, accumulated over geological time, creates the structure within which the reef's extraordinary biodiversity is possible. 
 These organisms, as described by Levins and Lewontin (1985), "influence [their] own evolution, by being both the object of natural selection and the creator of the conditions of that selection." Such a "creator" status of "their own selection" is made possible by creating and sustaining an immediate environment that is more favorable for the survival of their population than the background environment this immediate environment is nested in. In other words, instead of adapting to their environment individually, the niches they construct are part of their collective adaptational/survival strategies within the greater background environment. Consequently, these organisms are simultaneously residents of at least two environments: the immediate, population-constructed-and-maintained niche, and the niche's background environment that exists regardless of the niche (although certainly affected by and intertwined with it).
 However, while the intense burden to adapt to the background environment is buffered (but not insulated – a key distinction that we will revisit later in the thesis), by being born into and inheriting the niche, individual organisms are still subject to the niche's local adaptational pressures through what biologists call ecological inheritance. A baby beaver is typically not born in the "wilderness" from the vantage point of beavers – it is born into a beaver dam, the population-specific environment created by and inherited from its ancestors, up to tens or even hundreds of years (Nowak, 1991, as cited in Odling-Smee et al., 2003). Throughout the course of its life, it also bears the local adaptational pressures that are not imposed by the background environment per se, such as for skillful wood-cutting, wood carrying, the construction and maintenance of their dams and lodges, or oiling for waterproofing their furs. 
 To summarize what consists of niche construction, Matthews et al. proposed three criteria (2014, as simplified by Laland et al., 2016): 
 1. An organism must significantly modify environmental conditions; 
 2. Organism-mediated environmental modifications must influence selection pressures on a recipient organism; 
 3. There must be an evolutionary response in at least one recipient population caused by the environmental modification.
 With the niche construction theory, we now see that it is possible, and in fact, not rare, for an organism to reside in two environments simultaneously. But to be in a squeeze condition would require that the adaptational principles of the two environments one resides in to be mutually incompatible. It would be difficult to imagine how an animal niche would operate in an incompatible way with its background environment, given its dependence and deep embeddedness in the latter.1 Nonetheless, not only humans have made this possible, as we will see in the later chapters, we are also intensifying the extent of incompatibility between the human niche and its background environment.
## C. The duality of human existence evolved from dual ecosystems
 Japanese ecologist and one of the founders of the study of Synthetic Anthropology, Hideo Obara (小原秀雄), identifies a key distinction between animal niche construction and that of humans. All niche constructing organisms are still directly dependent and embedded in the greater food chain, so their life cycles (e.g., hunting, migration, mating) must accommodate those organisms that they are fed on and avoid their predators. However, humans since the agricultural revolution became highly detached from the greater food chain by domesticating animals and plants,2 which in effect led to the emergence of artificial ecosystems (人工生態系/人為的生態系), distinguished from other niches by our increasingly self-sufficient artificial food chains – where we sit at the apex. 
 Despite humans and these domesticated organisms ultimately remain dependent on the same biophysical substrate of planet Earth, Obara shows that such dependence had become indirect through the mediation of artificial ecosystems: While harvests are still affected by climate cycles and "disturbed" by wildlife (in particular, pests and diseases), humans are increasingly feed on domesticated animals and plants, not wildlife per se – in fact, the taken-for-granted word choice of "disturbance" also signifies how the independence of our artificial food chain had become the default of our collective consciousness. Also, while humans are still being composted by microbes after death, most of us have ceased to serve as a constant source of food of predators or scavengers in the greater food chain – Such cases had become exceptions rather than normal. In other words, the human food chain becomes a bounded artefact — an (although never perfectly) engineered system, in which its core nodes of interest are selected for function rather than ecologically given, and its system boundaries are carefully gatekept from external factors. 
 In constructing our artificial food chains, we impose adaptational interventions on domesticated animals directly and indirectly: Directly, through selective breeding of individuals animals with favorable characteristics, such as tameness, fur type, muscle-fat composition, or fertility; Indirectly, through fitting in the artificial environments humans made for them – human-conditioned microclimates, protection from predators, effortless yet skewed food intake, limits to physical movement, and so on. Such artificial selection does not only lead to the animal's adaptation toward favorable characteristics, but also with effects that are not necessarily intended, such as, notably, the increase of non-reproductive sexual behaviors and expanded estrus in some mammals like sheep, water buffalo, and cats in captivity (Obara 1988). Such changes in sexual behaviors are signs that the survival of these specific domesticated animals have become largely detached from climate cycles through the mediation of artificial ecosystems. 
 While we probably will not say humans breed ourselves selectively in the fashion of our domestication of animals, we too, impose adaptational interventions to ourselves through the construction of material and non-material aspects of our artificial ecosystems: For example, through the increasing exploitation of different kinds of energy sources and architectural modifications of microclimates materially, and through values, norms, legal codes, or organizational forms nonmaterially (I will elaborate on the material and nonmaterial aspects of artificial ecosystems further in Chapter II). Besides the intentional fitting into collectively favorable characteristics, the non-reproductive sexual behaviors and year-round estrus as likely-unintended effects – like domesticated animals – also demonstrate the extent we have become detached from climate cycles. Obara calls this auto-artificial selection (自己人為淘汰), a process of imposing – although not fully intentional – adaptational pressures onto ourselves, which leads to self-domestication (自己家畜化). Such self-imposed adaptational pressure also marked the watershed between what he calls the duality of human existence (人間存在の二重性) and dual ecosystems (二重生態系): We evolved into our biological existence as homo sapiens (ヒト) through natural selection up to niche construction in the background biophysical ecosystems, but it is through auto-artificial selection in artificial ecosystems that we became self-domesticated human (人間) as anthropological being. 
## D. The adaptational operating system: five working criteria
 By visiting the niche construction theory and Obara's dual ecosystems, we can see that the emergence of human-constructed artificial ecosystems is not a disconnected split from other animals. Rather, it can be viewed as one of the many coexisting evolutionary strategies in a continuum, ranging from genotypical adaptation, phenotypical adaptation, niche construction, to the construction of artificial ecosystems – or world-building. In particular, I consider animal niches and human worlds members of the adaptational operating systems (AOS) ideal type. By calling this ideal type an operating system, I do not mean they possess isomorphic structures with computer operating systems, but to allow for maximum compatibility with the following five working criteria spanning the framework's evolutionary, systemic-functional, semiotic, and phenomenological aspects:
 1. In its intensional sense, an AOS is a bounded sub-environment that embodies a specific mode of organizing conditions and principles, made of specific material and nonmaterial components, and is operated by its members collectively to function as a system of collective survival strategies of a population – whether of a same species or consisting different species – within its specific geo-local background environment. 
 2. In its extensional sense, an AOS does not only include its internal, operative members, but also includes its total relations—material and nonmaterial—with its background environment and other AOSs. 
 3. This sub-environment, while dependent on the workings of its background environment, operates upon its own set of organizing conditions and principles irreducible to the latter. 
 4. Due to the differences in background environmental pressures, as an adaptational strategy tailored to its local specificities, populations can develop their own operating systems with different levels of commensurability (of organizing conditions or principles) with other populations – even within the same biological species. However, the incommensurability between operating systems does not necessarily render each of their components incompatible to one another, as such components can be adopted in ways that fit the other operating system's organizing conditions or principles. 
 5. From the vantage point of an AOS's member, the extensional AOS that includes its total relations is the member's totality; in other words, an extensional AOS is a population-dependent totality.
 A close conceptual sibling of the framework I propose above is Sukhoverkhov & Gontier's (2021) treatment of a community as level or unit of evolution above the organismal level. The notion of specific mode and system of collective survival strategies in criterion 1 highly overlaps with what Sukhoverkhov & Gontier calls community traits as units of evolution, defined as "[s]ynergistic/organizational traits that characterize the community and that result from the cumulative, transgenerational, and constructed niches resulting in turn from biological, ecological, and sociocultural, extra-genetic inheritance," with "[g]roup behavior, societal traits, languages, cultures, artifacts and technological complexes" as examples (2021: 2). 
 Sukhoverkhov & Gontier's communities as levels of evolution approximately corresponds to the extensional scope in criterion 2. As a level of evolution, a community is "[a]n agglomeration of interacting populations of which the individual members can belong to the same as well as to different species," and "include[s] the biotic and abiotic environmental niche inhabited and constructed by the community" (2021: 2). Examples include "[b]iofilms, ant colonies, groups, holobionts, niches, societies."
 However, I do not use the term community directly since it can easily be confused with our colloquial use of the term, and it does not fully capture the other aspects beyond evolution in the rest of the three criteria. 
 The irreducibility criterion adds a systemic-functional aspect to the framework. This aspect can be understood as an extension of Polanyi's (1968) concept of boundary conditions:3 a set of conditions and principles (what Polanyi calls "boundary conditions") at a higher ontological level that restricts the behavior of lower-level things, so the latter with higher indeterminacy can be harnessed in specific ways. For example, the physical-chemical features of raw materials do not determine and are not sufficient for explaining their potential uses; it is through their integration into systems, such as metabolism in organisms or assemblage into a machine, their features are harnessed into specific functions. While things at a higher level certainly depend on those at lower levels for their existence or function, they are neither completely determined nor explainable by (namely, irreducible to) the lower-level things they depend upon. An AOS being a mode and system of collective survival strategies can be considered as a special kind of boundary condition, which harness what is available in the background environment's features to, while simultaneously, address the survival pressures imposed by the background environment. 
 The incommensurability criterion bridges the systemic-functional with its semiotic counterpart. It would perhaps be more intuitive to illustrate this criterion first by drawing analogies from operating systems in computing. MacOS, Windows OS, and Linux OS are incommensurable with one another in their digital ontologies4 and operative structures and processes, despite their material substrates do not necessarily differ – they can, in principle, run on the same hardware architecture. OSs that share source codes, such as Android-based OSs, have varying extents of commensurability with one another. While some digital objects can be interoperable across OSs (e.g., markdown files being natively supported across Mac, Windows, and Linux, while.docx not natively supported by Mac and Linux and requires specific programs capable of reading them), it does not make the OSs commensurable with one another per se. 
 The applicability of computer OS incommensurability to animals and humans is supported by both empirical and theoretical evidence with different levels of confidence. For example, Whiten and colleagues' seminal research on chimpanzees (1999) that accumulates 151 years of observation, shows that cultural variations across different populations within the same species do not only occur at the level of single behavioral patterns, but can happen at a systemic level. Their variation is beyond the mere difference in versions of the equivalent components (e.g., different ways of ant-dipping or aimed throwing), but at a level of the total constellation of different kinds of material and nonmaterial components (e.g., varying behavior patterns, tools, source of food), which suggests different functions or meanings assigned to each component, and the sum of the components being organized or related to one another in different configurations (e.g., even when the behaviors of "knocking to attract attention," "dancing in the rain," "using leaves as seat" are common across some of the different populations, they are performed in different ways and may serve different functions or meanings).5 The introduction of a component from a population to another is made possible by social learning, or what Sukhoverkhov & Gontier calls social plasticity (2021: 3), and is already widely observed across animal species (Whiten, 2021; multiple references needed). However, the newly introduced component does not necessarily serve the same function or meaning as it does in the source population, and such discrepancy suggests different extents of commensurability (reference needed). 
 The incommensurability between human AOSs does not only lie in the directly observable socio-cultural patterns and materiality, but perhaps more significantly, in the lack of "preexisting universals" (Escobar, 2015) among their metaphysics. It is true that the components of each AOS can be introduced to one another, and in fact, have always occurred in history through their interactions. However, the functions, meanings, and relations of the equivalent components within the AOSs were not the same. Consider what counts as a "person" in different civilizations. In the late Republican and Imperial Roman world, a person was first a position within a legal household: under Roman family law, an adult son—even a married senator commanding armies—owned no property in his own name, since whatever he acquired belonged in law to his father for as long as his father lived (Saller, 1994). In the Classic Maya world (c. 250–900 CE), a person was not a single bounded individual but a divisible, permeable constellation composed of multiple substances—some able to enter and leave the body—so that the self was distributed across detachable components rather than unified within one body, a "mosaic corporeality" (Houston & Stuart, 1998; Houston, Stuart & Taube, 2006). In the Han-Chinese world (206 BCE–220 CE), a person was at once a node in a web of relational roles—constituted by the Five Relationships (五倫) "as relational persons … rather than as individual selves" (Ames & Rosemont, 2011)—and a registered unit of the imperial state, since the apparatus of "registering households and equalizing the people" (編戶齊民) did not merely count pre-existing individuals but constituted them as homogenized, taxable, conscriptable subjects (Tu, 1990). A person teleported from one of these worlds to another, even with full fluency in its destination's language, would not be able to function seamlessly. This person is transferrable to different AOSs, but as a "component," they must be recalibrated against the destination AOS's organizing conditions and principles to truly integrate. Even time-travel across dynasties on the same geographical locale would require different extents of recalibration, as each dynasty operates upon its modified versions of collective survival strategies targeted to their spatial-temporal specificities. This is a key premise to this thesis and will be further elaborated in Chapter III. 
 Finally, the population-dependent totality criterion further extends the framework to phenomenological grounds. In this sense, a extensional AOS is also an Umwelt (modified by author from Uexküll, 1934/2010) of its members: It is the population-centric, bounded totality constructed from what its members can meaningfully perceive (Merkwelt, perceivable totality) and act upon (Wirkwelt, effectable totality).6 In the Translator's Introduction, Joseph D. O'Neil stresses that an Umwelt is "an order [emphasis added] created by the subject's specific disposition and activity" (Uexküll, 1934/2010: 36). This is different from what Uexküll calls Umgebung, the indifferent, population-agnostic totality encompassing all population-dependent totalities, one that can never be fully perceivable and acted upon by any of its members. 
## E. World as AOS in the universe
 Uexküll's distinction between Umwelt and Umgebung as two different types of totalities enables us to map Obara's ecological concepts of nested dual ecosystems (an artificial ecosystem nested within its background biophysical ecosystem) onto two anthropological concepts we use to denote totality: the world and the universe.
 The word world carries its meaning in its etymology. With Germanic origin — Welt or wereld, combining wer ("man") and ald ("age") — its root meaning is "age of man" (World, n., Oxford English Dictionary Online, 2022). As the historian of philosophy Rémi Brague has shown in his comparative historical survey (2003), this etymology reflects a deeper semantic commitment: "world" almost never designates a physical reality as such. It is anthropological — its "worldliness" derives not from the abstracted totality per se, but from the totality-for-us: it is bounded, organized, and constituted by the fact of human habitation and the meaning-structures humans bring to it. In Uexküll's terms, our world is our Umwelt. 
 By contrast, universe derives from the Latin ūniversum — a compound of uni- ("one") and vertere ("to turn") — meaning "the whole, the sum of existing things, complete in all its parts, whole, regarded as a whole, affecting everyone or everything" (Universe, n., Oxford English Dictionary Online, 2022). Whether this entirety is unlimited, infinite, or boundless, is a perennial question subject to continued research and beyond the scope of this thesis. But even if this unresolved problem remain bracketed, unlike the word "world," the word "universe" is not defined by human presence; it has primarily been used to refer to the ultimate realm and substrate whose boundary is beyond our reach and all-encompassing, implying that it encompasses our "world(s)" but not reducible to it (Brague 2003; Encyclopædia Britannica, 2017).7 Such a concept of universe is philosophically approximate to Uexküll's Umgebung, but with its connotating magnitude that the latter's English translation as "surrounding" lacks. 
 Our tendency to use these two terms interchangeably, however, reminds us that this linguistic division of labor is not universal across traditions,8 and even remains ambiguous in Western tradition. According to Brague, the very concept of the universe as a unified, observer-independent ordered totality is a specific Greek philosophical achievement, formulated under the name kosmos. Heraclitus's Fragment 30 is the founding declaration: 
 This kosmos,9 the same for all, it is neither a god nor a man who has made it, but always, it was, it is, it will be: an everliving fire, kindling in measures, and being extinguished in measures. (DK 22 B30; translated by Brague, 2003: 20)
 The negation neither god nor man made it is the philosophical signature: this kind of totality is stipulated to be independent of any inhabitant's agency — denoting the substrate that this thesis calls the universe. Brague (2003: 15) calls this an "Archimedes point" from which man would be able to "submit nature to objective research." In Chapter III, I will track how this philosophical move was one of the key factors that eventually made the Enlightenment possible, and how does it serve as the cornerstone of what I call the Enlightenment Mode of Artificiality and its disposition toward universalization: As Uexküll reminds us, while humans tend to think we investigate animal-specific Umwelts in relation to the total, objective Umgebung, what we consider as Umgebung "is nothing else but our own, human Umwelt" (1934/2010: 53); in the same vein, universalization can be understood as an act of treating what is of a specific Umwelt as what is of Umgebung of all. 
 Building upon the discussions above, I thereby define a world and a universe as follows:
 A world is a special kind of AOS,
1) in which an artificial food chain is built and maintained upon human-selected core nodes of interest (regardless of the intention at the nodes' initial introduction – many are accidental), and the boundaries of such a system are carefully gatekept from external factors;
2) its specific mode of organizing conditions and principles as a system of collective survival strategies, is a specific Mode of Artificiality – Which will be unpacked in Chapter II. 
 A universe is,
1) the ultimate background environment that encompasses and conditions all AOSs, approximate to what Uexküll calls Umgebung: the encompassing totality that no population's Umwelt can ever fully absorb, and that no mode of survival can suspend;
2) the fundamental substrate of all, which interacts with all things and carries the total effect of all interactions, therefore transforms with all things – manifested in its spatial-temporal diversity (e.g., the diversity of things from blackholes, stars, to planets, and their respective internal or interrelating diversity in relation to time and space – from the configuration of matters, dynamics of energies, to the environmental impacts by animal and human footprints); 
3) the foundational ontological level of all, which is the source of laws, energies, or matters that are indifferent and unaffectable by anything (e.g., the real referents behind what is referred as "gravitation" or "thermodynamics" operate whether any AOS is present or if any AOS's members can perceive them).10 
 The relationships among universe, world, and operating system can be set out as three ontological layers11 — the layer of (1) the underlying conditions or principles that organize things, (2) the structures or dynamics those principles organize, and (3) the context-specific phenomena, experienceable or observable to a subject, in which those structures are manifested. This stratification is the point at which two vocabularies this thesis draws on converge. It integrates the language of complex adaptive systems and self-organization — its talk of levels, emergent order, and dynamics that play out across multiple scales — with critical realism's stratified ontology, mapping the systems vocabulary of levels onto the critical-realist strata of the real, the actual, and the empirical: the multi-scale conditions that organize a system answer to the layer of the real, the structures and dynamics they generate to the actual, and the observable behavior in which they surface to the empirical.[^dlx-cas] And at the same time it articulates the very same stratification in East Asian terms, which had already drawn the distinction with economy. This is not a quaint parallel to a Western scheme but a third articulation of one stratified ontology. I name the first layer for the underlying conditions or principles that organize things — the real (道, *dào*); the second for the structures and dynamics those principles organize — the actual (理, *lǐ*); and the third for the context-specific phenomena experienceable to a subject — the empirical (相, *xiàng*) — 道 the way things follow, 理 their constitutive pattern (the grain in jade or wood), 相 the aspect they present to a looking eye.[^dlx-strata] The critical-realist terms — real, actual, empirical — I keep as handles for cross-reference and let the three characters carry the argument, since the relation among the three — a contingent surface seen through to a more enduring ground — is exactly the one the East Asian usage already names, and one that, as later chapters will show, the made can counterfeit but not escape.[^dlx-cr] The table below maps the universe, an adaptational operating system, and a world each onto these three layers, so that the same stratified structure can be read across all three at a glance; read down a column, a domain falls into its three layers, and read across a row, the three domains share a layer. The same three layers will return when, in Chapter V, I come to the metaverse, where each is marked with the prefix for the humanly-made — the made (人為, *rénwéi*), in Xunzi's old sense of the prefix *wěi* (偽): "made," not "fake"[^dlx-wei]; but the triad itself I introduce only here.
Table 1-1. The three ontological strata read across the universe, an adaptational operating system, and a world.

| | Universe | Adaptational Operating System | World |
|---|---|---|---|
| Experienceable or observable phenomena | Spatial-temporally-specific phenomena, e.g., lived weather, directly collectable precipitation data | Population-specific perceived and enacted phenomena, e.g., observable foraging traffic, the exact things being foraged | Spatial-temporally-specific phenomena experienced or observed within a particular world, e.g., measured life expectancy, recorded rates of illness, lived satisfaction/frustration |
| Structures or dynamics | Sustained but variable geological, climatological, biological configurations, e.g., atmospheric composition and dynamics | Material and nonmaterial configurations of the system structure or dynamics, e.g., the colony's foraging trails, nest architecture, and division of labor | Material and nonmaterial configurations of the world's structure or dynamics, e.g., the tools, institutions, and infrastructures built to close the gap between life as wished for and life as it is — from medicine and housing to insurance, law, and supply chains |
| Organizing conditions or principles | Enduring laws, energies, matters indifferent and unaffectable by anything, e.g., (the actual referent behind what is referred as) gravitation, thermodynamics, light, atom | Population-specific organizing conditions and principles, e.g., rules and conditions determining emission of different ant pheromones | The population's specific ways of organizing collective survival (Mode of Artificiality), e.g., around the shared human wish to make life turn out as one wills it — to reduce the gap between life as wished for and life as it actually is |

 Two clarifications follow from these definitions that are load bearing throughout the thesis. 
 The first is the kind-versus-instance distinction: the world (singular, definite article) refers to the type — the human AOS as such, applicable to any human collectivity; a world (indefinite article) or worlds (plural) refers to the specific spatial-temporal instances of that type — the Hittites world, the Tang-China world, the post-colonization !Kung world, each a distinct realization of the same five criteria under different geo-local pressures. The universe-side has an analogous distinction — the universe as the fundamental-substrate-in-general versus a universe as one among a possible plurality of universes. Unlike the same distinction for "world," which is necessary for this thesis, the same distinction for "universe" is only subsidiary: It is to permit the argument to proceed by acknowledging the multiverse theory without committing to a position, which is beyond the scope of this thesis. 
 The other clarification that I must make is that the distinction between the world and the universe in this thesis is not a rebranding of the two discrete metaphysical categories in the culture/nature dualism: The latter is either treated as a lifeless or primitive object that is separate, subordinated to, and ready to be acted upon by the (agency of the) former; or a pure, unpolluted home or origin of the former as an object for nostalgia – both rendering nature as the "other" and represent two sides of the same anthropocentric gaze (Haila, 2000; Jones, 2009). As I already showed with ecological evidence and my definitions, a world is nested in the universe – no split is possible between the two. Obara's duality of human existence also shows that there is no pre-culture nature that a human can be returned to: while the biological conditioning of our existence as Homo sapiens remain part of our nature, the cultural is also always what is natural for us as humans; in fact, there is no non-human Homo sapiens, and a non-cultural human would be unnatural.12 
 As the brief examples above already show, the dualism of the nature/culture divide would ambiguate and conflate many foundational distinctions I make in my arguments. However, since the problem of the culture/nature dualism is beyond the scope of this thesis, I will avoid using the word nature and culture as much as possible for clarity. In particular, I will use universe, AOS, world, and artificiality as my main analytical units throughout this thesis. In the next chapter, I will unpack how artificiality makes Homo sapiens human, and our AOS a world.
 


# II. Artificiality's Role in Forming a World
## A. Artificiality as the human mode of survival
 Chapter I closed on a promise: that the next chapter would show how artificiality makes Homo sapiens human, and our adaptational operating system (AOS) a world. This chapter redeems it. The claim I will defend is that artificiality is not one human activity among others but the human mode of survival as such — the way our species secures its life — and that, precisely because it is this and not a mere accessory to some prior, self-sufficient nature, the world it builds cannot be reduced to the universe that underlies it. That irreducibility is the load-bearing claim of the chapter, for it is what later allows the made world to drift far enough from the given one to squeeze the human caught between them.
 Recall where Chapter I left the argument. By interposing an artificial food chain between itself and the raw web of life — a layer of cultivated plants, herded animals, stored grain, and the techniques that sustain them — the human lifts itself out of the predation and scarcity that govern every other organism. But this raises a question Chapter I deferred. Many animals modify their surroundings; the niche-construction literature (Odling-Smee, Laland, and Feldman, 2003) documents the beaver's dam, the termite's mound, the earthworm's chemically remade soil. Why, then, do those builders remain inside the food web while the human alone steps out of it — securing its survival not by an occasional artefact but by an entire fabricated ecosystem? The answer, I will argue, lies in a peculiar conjunction of pressure, retention, and bodily plasticity that turned making, for this one lineage, from a supplement into a condition.
 Bernard Stiegler gives the conjunction its sharpest mythic statement, and it is worth following his reading closely, because the myth encodes a thesis about human origins rather than merely illustrating one. In the version Plato puts in the mouth of Protagoras, the brothers Epimetheus and Prometheus are charged with equipping the newly made animals. Epimetheus — whose name means "afterthought" — works by what Stiegler (1998: 187) calls a "principle of compensation," balancing each creature's gifts so that "no species should be destroyed": speed against strength, fur against cold, fecundity against predation. Distributing his powers improvidently, he spends them all on the other animals and forgets the human, which is left, in the dialogue's words, "naked, unshod, unbedded, and unarmed" (Stiegler, 1998: 187–188). Prometheus — "forethought" — repairs his brother's omission by stealing, for the human alone, "the gift of skill in the arts, together with fire," so that "man acquired sufficient resources to keep himself alive" (Stiegler, 1998: 188). The human is, from the first, the animal that lives by what it makes because it was born without the equipment to live any other way.
 Stiegler draws from this an ontological claim I will take over, with one important correction. The human, on his account, is constituted by its prostheses rather than merely assisted by them: "The prosthesis is not a mere extension of the human body; it is the constitution of this body qua 'human'" (Stiegler, 1998: 152–153). Technics and the human are co-original — there is no moment at which a finished human first reaches for a tool, because the reaching is part of what finishes the human. Stiegler presses the point to its limit: "Humanity is without qualities, without predestination: it must invent, realize, produce qualities, and nothing indicates that, once produced, these qualities will bring about humanity, that they will become its qualities; for they may rather become those of technics" (Stiegler, 1998: 193–194). 
 Here I must enter the correction, because the strong reading threatens a claim Chapter I was at pains to avoid. To say the human is "without qualities" risks dissolving the biological floor on which the whole argument stands.[^c2-stiegler-biology] The human did not arrive as a blank awaiting inscription by technics; it arrived as a specific kind of ape, carrying specific equipment, into a specific ecological predicament. The default Stiegler names is real, but it is the default of an ecological misfit, not of a creature without qualities — a mismatch between an inherited body and a changed world, not an absence of inheritance. Keeping that distinction is what preserves the realist commitment of Chapter I, and it is key to the dis-embedding of the human from Homo sapiens that the later chapters trace: there is a given substrate, with its own determinate character, beneath everything the human goes on to make. The made answers a lack in the given; it does not replace a given that was never there.
 What was the lack? Its deep history is contested, and the contest matters, because rival accounts locate the original misfit in different places. The long-dominant savannah hypothesis held that a cooler, drier African climate drove the retreat of the forests and forced our ancestors out onto open grassland, where an upright gait was selected for; against it, a mosaic-habitat account now reads the fossil evidence as showing an ape never wholly committed to the ground.[^c2-savannah-mosaic] For my purposes the dispute need not be settled. What both accounts share is the decisive point: our ancestors did not arrive equipped for the life they would lead. They arrived with the qualities of apes — habitual upright walking, a brain large and complex for the body that carried it, canines already reduced from the great display teeth of their relatives, and a "non-honing chewing complex" no longer built to sharpen those canines against each other — and with this inheritance, and an unaccommodating world, they had to make do. 
 Making do, for this lineage, came to mean making — but the path from misfit to maker runs through a second, less obvious lack, one that concerns not the adult body but the infant's. The biologist Adolf Portmann observed that the human is born, by the standards of comparable mammals, radically premature: helpless, unfinished, incapable for a year of the locomotion a newborn ungulate manages within hours. Portmann named this condition secondary altriciality, and read it as the price of bipedalism and a large brain — a pelvis reshaped for upright walking cannot pass a head grown to its eventual size, so the head must be born small and finish its growth outside the womb. The human therefore completes much of its development in what Portmann called a social uterus: a built and tended environment of care, outside the body, in which the infant brain is finished under the direct shaping of culture rather than the closed program of gestation. 
 It helps to see what is distinctive here by contrast. Zoologists distinguish precocial young, born ready — the calf that walks at birth — from altricial young, born helpless and dependent. Altriciality alone is not a human peculiarity; consider the woodpecker. Its altricial chicks hatch blind and naked after only twelve to eighteen days, and fledge within a further twenty to thirty, raised inside a material niche the parents excavate — the nesting cavity — and supported by a suite of nonmaterial strategies: monogamy, the taking of shifts at the nest, the laying of multiple eggs against loss. But the woodpecker's dependency is brief and its repertoire fixed; the cavity and the shift-taking do not compound into anything larger. The human newborn's dependency, by contrast, is measured not in weeks but in years, and the environment of care it requires is not a fixed repertoire but an open one — so that, as the period of finishing lengthens, the complexity of the AOS that must do the finishing grows not additively but factorially, each new capacity making room for several more. Secondary altriciality, in other words, does not merely permit culture; it conscripts it, handing the unfinished brain to a made world and letting that world write into it.
 Yet secondary altriciality alone is not sufficient to explain the human's departure from the food web, and it would overreach to claim that it were. Obara, indeed, would resist treating secondary altriciality as the cause at all: for him it is a downstream effect of the self-domestication traced in Chapter I — auto-artificial selection (self-induced human selection, 自己人為淘汰, *jiko jin'i tōta*), by which the human, in remaking its own conditions of life, came in turn to refashion itself. We need not decide which comes first. As Chapter I demonstrated, the mismatch between the Homo lineage and its new environment generates a deficiency the human must make up for; and the more factors each deficiency and its remedy depend upon, the more the human adaptational operating system self-organizes into greater complexity. What carries the lineage out of the food web is that self-domesticating process, and the recursively compounding making it sets going, which the next paragraphs anatomize.
 Daniel Ross, introducing Stiegler, sharpens what the sufficient condition must include. The human, on this account, is bound to its artificiality by three knots at once: it must produce its artificial organs — tools, instruments, equipment — in order to survive; it must learn to use and make them, which it does not know how to do by instinct; and it must institute social organizations capable of conserving, transmitting, and articulating across the generations the knowledge of how they are made and used (Ross, in Stiegler, 2018: 19–20).
 The third knot is the one that matters most for distinguishing the human case, and it is worth resisting a tempting shortcut. One might suppose that tool-making is what sets the human apart — but it plainly does not, for tool-making is by now well documented across the animal world.[^c2-animal-toolmaking] The human difference lies elsewhere: not in that it makes tools, but in how its making compounds.
 Obara names the mechanism with a phrase I will lean on: the doubling — or doubleness — of working (加工の二重性, *kakō no nijūsei*). The point is that human making is recursive in a way animal making is not. As Obara puts it, "the working is done with tools, and to make a tool one uses a tool; this begets tool after tool, and tools give birth to a world of tools." The contrast is exact. The sea otter that cracks a shell against a stone it found is working with a first-order, found instrument; its making terminates in the meal. The human that knaps a flint to shape a haft to mount an adze is working a tool with a tool — and that second-order operation does not terminate, because each made tool becomes the means of making the next. What begins as an act becomes a self-extending system, the small recursive hinge on which the whole departure from the food web turns: as the making compounds, the artificial self-organizes into ever greater complexity, and so stops being a means within the environment and starts to become an environment in its own right.
 For that recursion to run away rather than stall, the made must be retained — held and faithfully transmitted across the generations, which is the work of Ross's third knot. Stiegler analyzes retention into three layers, and the third is what humans uniquely add. Primary retention is the lingering of the just-past in the present moment — what he likens, after Husserl, to a comet's tail trailing the now. Secondary retention is individual memory, the recollection a single mind carries. Tertiary retention is the externalized record: the made support — the carved tally, the written document, the archive — that holds the past outside any living memory and so makes it transmissible beyond the individual life (Stiegler, 1998: 247–248). This is the mechanism Stiegler calls epiphylogenesis: an inheritance that runs neither through the genes nor through the single brain but through the accumulating made world itself, which each generation enters as already-built and leaves enlarged.
 It would be too quick, though, to treat faithful transmission as uniquely human, for animal "culture" is real and well documented; the question is whether animal traditions accumulate — whether they ratchet — and the evidence suggests they generally do not.[^c2-animal-ratchet] What is absent is the open-ended, branching accumulation that tertiary retention makes possible: the capacity not merely to replace or fine-tune an inherited form but to build new kinds upon it without limit.
 We can now gather what the foregoing suggests into a tentative conjunction of three factors, offered not as a proven law but as the most economical reading of the case. There must be pressure — an obligatory making forced by a lack of fit between body and world; there must be retention — a means of holding and faithfully transmitting what is made; and there must be substrate — a body and brain plastic enough to be reshaped by what the made world feeds back. Pressure, retention, substrate: where any one is weak, the recursion tends to stall. The beaver has the substrate and a kind of retention but no obligatory making — its dam is a supplement, not a condition of survival; the whale has pressure of a sort and prodigious transmission but a song that will not, on present evidence, ratchet. It may be that only in the human lineage have all three coincided to the degree the runaway required.
 The substrate condition repays a closer look, because it corrects a persistent intuition about sequence. It is tempting to assume the big brain came first and made the tools — but the fossil record reverses the order.
