Australopithecus walked upright and handled stones while still carrying a brain scarcely larger than a chimpanzee's; encephalization, the dramatic growth of the brain, came afterward. Obara draws the moral plainly: it was "through the interaction of hand and eye" in the sustained use of tools that the brain enlarged, so that "the tool is the core of humanization" — humanization (人間化, *ningenka*) meaning the socio-cultural making-human, not the biological becoming-human (hominization, ヒト化, *hito-ka*) (Obara & Hani, 1995: 29). The brain is, in part, a product of the made world it then went on to extend — which is precisely why the substrate condition explains why the human could embark on runaway artifice, but not, by itself, why it did.
 That gap — between could and did — is filled by contingency, and it is worth marking the modesty this imposes on the argument. The conjunction of pressure, retention, and plastic substrate made runaway artifice possible; it did not make it fated. The same favourable conditions coincide in several lineages that never embarked on anything like the human trajectory.[^c2-contingency] The human took a rare fork; it was not bound to. Keeping this contingency in view guards the account against the teleological reading it must avoid: artificiality is the human's mode of survival as a matter of natural history, not of destiny.
 One question remains for this section: why a food chain in particular? Why does the human's obligatory artifice take, as its founding form, the fabrication of an entire alimentary order — provisioning giving way to cultivation, cultivation to domestication — until the species feeds itself wholly from within an artificial ecosystem? The answer follows from the character of the original lack. The misfit was, at root, alimentary: an ape under nutritional pressure in a world that no longer fed it as it had fed its forest-dwelling ancestors. Obligatory artifice, directed from the first flake toward the problem of food, naturally elaborates along the alimentary axis before any other. The artificial food chain is the human's native object precisely because the founding misfit was a hunger — and it is the construction of that chain, more than any other single achievement, that lifts the human clear of the web of life and converts its AOS into a world.
## B. Naming the made: why "artificiality," not "technology"
 If the made is to carry the weight this argument places on it, it needs a name — and the obvious candidate, technology, will not serve, for reasons worth stating carefully rather than by fiat. In its now-dominant usage, technology names a means to an end: a neutral instrument, indifferent to the purposes it serves, awaiting good or bad use by its wielder. The Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy registers the common view in just these terms — technology as "a neutral instrument" that is good or bad only in "the use to which it is put," or as "merely applied science." The conception descends from Heidegger's diagnosis of the "current" understanding, which he summarizes as the twofold commonplace that technology is "a means to an end" and "a human activity" (Heidegger, 1977: 4–5) — the "instrumental and anthropological" definition he sets out to question (Heidegger, 1977: 4–5).
 The trouble is that this means-end framing, smuggled in with the word, distorts most of what I need the name to cover. Call religion a "technology" and one has already presumed it to be a device for producing some effect — when religion, on its own self-understanding, is a matter of revelation or devotion, not an instrument calibrated to an end. Call science a "technology" and one imports the same presumption — when science, whatever its applications, is in the first instance a truth-seeking practice answerable to the world, not a tool answerable to a user. To be sure, there are influential inversions of the neutral-instrument picture: Foucault's "technologies of the self," which treat ethical self-formation as a kind of craft, and Yuk Hui's "cosmotechnics," which embeds technical activity within a moral and cosmological order. These are defensible, even illuminating, redescriptions — but they earn their force precisely by straining against the word's ordinary means-end sense, and I do not want a term whose every use must first fight its own connotations.
 Stiegler makes the same complaint from the other side. In ordinary use, he observes, "in general, technics designates… [the] restricted and specified domain of tools, of instruments, if not only machines" — an understanding he calls "inadequate," "already restrictive and derived" (Stiegler, 1998: 93–94). Against this narrowing he restores the older, wider sense: "Technics (tekhne) designates… all the domains of skill," so that "all human action… is after a fashion tekhne" (Stiegler, 1998: 93). It is this breadth I require — but rather than reclaim the contested word technics against its modern drift, I will adopt a term that wears the breadth on its face.
 That term is artificiality. By it I name not a domain of objects but an origin and a mode of being: the condition of being humanly made, instituted, and maintained, as against being given. Artificiality, so defined, does not pick out the tools-and-machines sense of "technology" at all; it picks out everything the human produces to secure its life — the field and the city, the law and the rite, the word and the institution, the science and the creed. On this usage, science, ethics, and religion are not technologies (they are not instruments calibrated to ends) but kinds of artificiality (they are humanly made and maintained). The word is chosen with its root in view: artificial descends from the Latin ars, skill or craft, and facere, to make, so that the artificial is, etymologically, simply that which is brought about by human making, as against that which arises of itself.
 Two independent traditions converge on just this breadth, and the convergence is what licenses treating it as a natural category rather than a stipulation. Stiegler's wide technē, already cited, "designates first and foremost all the domains of skill" — he names "politeness," "cooking," "rhetoric," "poetry," finding "something of poetry and rhetoric in all language" (Stiegler, 1998: 93). From an entirely different direction, Obara marks the same field in Japanese orthography, writing the made as *mono* in katakana (the made, モノ) — distinguishing it from the everyday *mono* (もの) of ordinary things — to gather everything humanly produced, the abstract no less than the concrete: religion, ideology, and music alongside the axe and the granary. Two thinkers working in different languages and traditions, reaching independently for a category this wide, suggest the category is found rather than imposed.
 To see that the boundary between given and made is itself drawn differently by different civilizations — and so that no single drawing can be taken for granted — it helps to set the Western vocabulary beside the Chinese. The most instructive term is Xunzi's artifice (*wěi*, 偽). In the "Human Nature Is Bad" chapter he writes that human nature is foul and what is good in it is artifice ("人之性惡，其善者偽也") — and glosses the term: that in a person which can be learned and brought to completion through effort is called *wěi* ("可學而能、可事而成之在人者謂之偽"). The renderings are Paul Goldin's: foul (*è*, 惡) not as "evil" in any Augustinian sense but as the raw, undisciplined impulse, and *wěi* as artifice; while nature (*xìng*, 性) names not a moral essence but the inborn, the biological given one is simply born with, an etymon Goldin traces to "genus, stock" and to birth or life (*shēng*, 生) (Goldin, 2026). The Tang commentator Yang Liang spells out the graph itself ("偽字人傍為"): the character *wěi* is person (*rén*, 人) beside doing (*wéi*, 為) — the made is, literally, the person-done. And the etymon of *wěi* is telling — it carries the root sense "to forge" (Goldin, 2026), the very sense this thesis will later give the metaverse's forged Dao (*wěi dào*, 偽道). The tradition thus names the made not by opposition to a reified "nature" but as the artifice human effort lays over a raw biological given, the human completing what the given leaves unfinished.
 There is an irony in both vocabularies worth pausing on, because it bears directly on my choice of term. The Latin *artificialis* curdled, over time, toward the pejorative — "artificial" came to mean fake, contrived, insincere. The Chinese *wěi* (偽) curdled the same way: the *Shuowen jiezi* defines it flatly as deceit, taking 人 as sense and 爲 as sound ("偽，詐也。从人爲聲"). Both traditions, that is, let the word for the made slide into the word for the false — as though to make were already to falsify. Recovering *wěi* in Xunzi's neutral, even honorific sense is exactly the operation this thesis performs on "artificial": reclaiming, for the made, a name that ordinary usage has soured.
 One caution must be entered before going further, lest the comparison claim more than it should. I take from Xunzi a name and a structural insight — that a civilization can mark the made (*wěi*) without running it through the Nature/Man divide, without opposing it to a reified nature — not his substantive doctrine. Xunzi does not for that reason collapse the nature/artifice (*xìng*/*wěi*, 性/偽) distinction; he insists on it, writing in the "Discourse on Ritual" that nature is the raw material of the beginning while *wěi* is the flourishing of pattern and order ("性者，本始材朴也；偽者，文理隆盛也"), and in the "Rectifying Names" chapter that the deliberate activity arising when the mind reflects and acts is called *wěi* ("心慮而能為之動謂之偽"). His distinction is between the inborn and the wrought, not between a self-standing Nature and a Man set over against it. (English renderings of *wěi* vary tellingly — Hutton gives "deliberate effort," Knoblock "conscious exertion," Goldin "artifice"; I follow Goldin's renderings — "artifice" for *wěi*, "foul" for *è* — for the reasons given above.) The point of the cross-traditional detour is not to enlist Xunzi as an ally in doctrine but to show, by counter-example, that the Western partition of given from made is one contingent settlement among possible others — and therefore that adopting "artificiality" as a term is itself an artificial act, to be performed deliberately rather than inherited unexamined.
 The Greek vocabulary that the modern term inherits drew the line where the Chinese did not, and the seam runs along the contrast between the grown and the made. Phusis — "nature" — derives from the Indo-European root *bʰeh₂u-, "to grow, bring forth"; in Aristotle's canonical formulation (Physics II.1, 192b8) what is by phusis has the principle of its motion within itself. Against it stands technē — "craft, art" — from *teks-, "to fabricate," the root that surfaces in τέκτων, the carpenter or builder: the made as what has the principle of its coming-to-be in another, namely its maker. The Greeks add a third, nomos — "law, custom, convention" — from νέμω, "to distribute, apportion" (Indo-European *nem-): what holds not by nature but by human positing. (These Indo-European reconstructions follow Watkins, 2011; for φύω in laryngeal notation cf. Beekes, 2010 — the full grade *bʰeh₂u- given here is a variant of the root also cited in its zero grade *bʰuh₂-.)
 Now the comparative point can be made precisely, and it is subtler than a simple presence-or-absence of concepts. Yuk Hui states the asymmetry directly: "the Chinese have no equivalents of the categories that the Greeks called techne and physis" (Hui, 2016: 33–34). But the right inference is not that Chinese thought lacked a concept of the made; it is that it grouped the field differently. Rémi Brague helps locate the Greek grouping. What the Greeks set on one side, he observes, is "that which has its principle in the human activity of fabrication or estimation, i.e., the artificial (tekhnē) and the conventional (nomos)," and on the other "that which grows by itself… the natural" (Brague, 2003: 14). The decisive move here is the grouping, not an identity: technē and nomos are bracketed together — the fabricated and the agreed, the made object and the made norm — and jointly opposed to phusis, the self-arising. The Western partition is thus two-against-one: the made and the conventional on one side, the grown on the other. Chinese thought, organizing the field around the way (*dào*, 道) and the vessel (*qì*, 器) — the ordering principle and the particular instrument, the vessel being the made-as-instrument situated *within* a cosmic-moral order rather than set over against nature — simply does not run the same seam. This is an early instance of the world–universe embeddedness that, I will argue in the coming chapters, is the default condition of most civilizations: the made nested within the given rather than opposed to it. I dwell on this not to adjudicate which carving is correct but to expose the contingency: in choosing "artificiality," I deliberately take the broad bracket — technē-and-nomos together, the whole field of the humanly made and instituted — as my object, rather than the narrow, machine-bound "technology" that the same tradition's later drift bequeathed.
 
## C. Why the made is irreducible to the material: virtuality
 While it might be easy to picture how tangible material technologies have extensively reshaped our worlds — from cave- and stone-based tribes to AI-powered "smart cities" — artificiality is not always manifested in material forms. The nonmaterial forms of artificiality are indispensable to the development of their material counterparts, and the two lead dialectically to one another. Artificially created nonmaterial things — gods, money, laws — have the power to direct our thoughts and behaviours, and even to elicit emotions or biological reflexes such as the fight-or-flight response. Without these nonmaterial things, or nonmaterial aspects, that facilitate the interactions and dynamics among a system's components, a world — "an artificial environment nested in the universe for human survival" — would not be possible at all, for its material things would remain mere aggregates rather than being integrated into functioning systems.
 With this in view the central claim of the section can be stated without hedging. The world the human builds is irreducible to the material that underlies it. I do not mean that the world floats free of matter — every artefact is some arrangement of matter and obeys matter's laws. I mean that what an artefact *is*, and what it can *do*, cannot be read off from the matter that composes it: a made thing has properties and exercises powers that its material substrate neither possesses nor explains, and a world built of such things is therefore a stratum of reality with laws of its own, not derivable from the physics beneath it. The argument turns on identifying the precise feature of the made that its material cannot supply.
 That feature is what I will call virtuality. The made works, very often, through something that is causally efficacious yet nowhere material — a status, a rule, a meaning, a relation that does real work in the world without being any of the matter through which it works. I call this causally efficacious nonmaterial aspect of artificiality virtuality. Its medium is the sign: virtuality is the *semiotic* dimension of the made — the work done by what a thing *means* rather than by the matter it is made of. Two qualifications fix the term against predictable misreadings. First, virtuality is an *aspect* of artificiality, not a separate category of thing: every virtuality is an artificiality, but not every artificiality is virtual. A wall compels chiefly by its sheer mass rather than by what it means, and is thus artificial without being, in the relevant sense, virtual — though even a wall is rarely mute, since its mass and its meaning (a boundary, a prohibition, a frontier) typically work together. Second, and against the word's everyday connotations, virtuality is not unreality. It is the opposite: the dimension in which the made does its most consequential work. The authority in an office, the obligation in a promise, the value in money, the boundary in a border — each is fully effective and nowhere locatable in the matter that bears it, present in that matter only in the way a melody is "in" the air that carries it, which is to say not reducible to that air at all.
 Because "virtual" is a worked-over word, I must mark my sense off from the conceptions already in circulation, keeping from each what is sound and declining what would mislead. The first and most entrenched is what I will call the *simulationist* view — the view that a virtual *x* is a mere "as-if" copy of a real *x*, possessing certain characteristics or features of the simulated real thing yet not being completely real, in the sense Baudrillard gives the term. This view can be traced back to C. S. Peirce's definition (1902): "a virtual *x* (where *x* is a common noun) is something, not an *x*, which has the efficiency (*virtus*) of an *x*" (emphasis added). My usage carries forward the "efficiency (*virtus*)" aspect of Peirce's definition — the sense descending from the Latin etymon *virtus* (virtue) and *virtualis* (power or potency) — but I find the "as if" aspect of his definition unhelpful. As Jean Baudrillard shows (1994), many artificial things have no original or real counterpart to represent whatsoever. Seeing a virtual *x* as a simulation of a real *x* — as a copy whose reality-status is parasitic on some original it stands in for — overlooks the fact that many virtual things are novel: a virtual *x* may be original in its own right. The money in the banknote is not a stand-in for some realer money elsewhere — it *is* money, fully and without remainder. Without a radical departure from the simulationist view, we cannot make sense of an emerging future in which we are increasingly incapable of finding the real that is supposedly represented or simulated by the virtual.
 The simulationism just described is, however, only one of three positions on the reality of the virtual that are easily run together, and it will help to be exact about which I am rejecting by setting all three side by side. The first is *fictionalism*: the view that virtual objects are mere fictions, no more real than the characters in a novel — efficacious only as we pretend, with no causal standing of their own. The second is the *as-if simulationism* I have just traced to Peirce's "as if" and Baudrillard's copy-without-an-original: the virtual is a stand-in for the real without being it, possessing the features of the real thing yet falling short of its reality, and so not really efficacious in its own right. The third is *virtual realism* (or *simulation realism*): the view that virtual objects are *real* — that they exist, exercise causal powers, and are no illusion. The thesis rejects fictionalism outright; its quarrel over *reality-status* — virtuality is really efficacious, not a derived stand-in — is a quarrel with the *Baudrillardian as-if*, not with realism about the virtual. On that decisive question the thesis stands with David Chalmers, whose view I take up at length below. Chalmers is a *simulationist*, but of the realist, anti-fictionalist kind: he holds that the virtual is computer-based *and* fully real, the opposite of a fiction. He is not, in any usable sense, an "anti-simulationist"; what he is against is *fictionalism*, and so am I. My departure from Baudrillard is therefore a departure in Chalmers's direction on the reality of the virtual. From Baudrillard I keep only the precise distinction between *dissimulating* — masking a reality that is there — and *simulating* — generating a "reality" with no original behind it; what I decline is his terminal, epochal framing, in which the virtual names a late historical condition, an "integral reality" that has finally displaced the real (Baudrillard, [VERIFY citekey] — *The Intelligence of Evil; or, The Lucidity Pact*, on *la réalité intégrale*). Virtuality, in my account, is not a stage that modernity reaches; it is a *trans-historical genus*, as old as money, law, and rite, of which the digital is only the latest and most concentrated species. (My one quarrel with Chalmers, set out below, is not over the reality of the virtual at all but over what *makes* a thing virtual — the computational-versus-semiotic axis.)
 That this is no eccentric usage is best seen by recovering two thinkers in whose work the very same conception is already at home — and who supply the *reasons* for it that a bare etymology cannot. The sociologist Anthony Giddens holds that social structures exist *virtually*: on his analysis, structures are rules and resources that have no continuous material existence of their own but exist only as instantiated in social practice and as memory traces in the minds of agents (Giddens, 1984). Here "virtual" plainly does not mean an "unreal or not-as-real representation or simulation" of something else that is truly real; it marks, rather, that social structures of the kind Giddens analyses are, as he analyses them, human-dependent and do not exist materially *per se* — which is exactly the sense I require. I take this as a feature of social structures as Giddens describes them in the present human dispensation, not as a permanent law of all virtuality: the later chapters will argue that under a maturing metaverse the nonmaterial apparatus may begin to detach from the humans who instantiate it, so that the *human*-dependence Giddens observes is precisely one of the things at stake, not a fixed point. What I borrow from him is the narrower and more durable claim — that such structures are real and causally efficacious without existing materially. The philosopher Roy Bhaskar reaches the same place from the side of ontology: he treats the virtual not as something contrasting with reality but as constituting "a *sui generis* real emergent stratum of reality with its own irreducible causal powers" (Engholm, 2015). The virtual, on Bhaskar's account, is at once *real* and *causally efficacious* and *not material* — a higher stratum dependent on the lower, yet with powers of its own that the lower does not possess. Giddens gives the non-material instantiation of structure; Bhaskar gives the emergent-stratum realism; between them they say precisely what virtuality is, and why calling it "virtual" need carry no whiff of the unreal.
 The most demanding of the rival conceptions is the most recent — and the closest to a genuine rival, because it too insists on the *reality* of the virtual. This is the now-colloquial view, championed by David Chalmers (2022), which takes the virtual to be *computer-based*. Chalmers states it plainly: "a 'virtual reality' is a computer-based reality" (Chalmers, 2022: 192–93), and "at least to a first approximation, a virtual object is a structure of bits inside a computer" (Chalmers, 2022: 198). His motive is anti-*fictionalist*, and admirable in it: precisely to avoid the illusory or fictitious implications of the older "as if" view, he reasserts the computer-based reading in order to underwrite the realness of virtual things — a realness he grounds in the existence, causal efficacy, mind-independence, non-illusoriness, and genuineness of digital objects within computer systems. This is the burden of his best-known provocations. A virtual cat, he insists, is a real cat: "The cats around you are real cats — it's just that cats in your reality are made of bits" (Chalmers, 2022 [VERIFY page]); and from this it follows that even if we *are* in a simulation — even, that is, if we ourselves are virtual humans — we are not thereby cheated of reality, for "you can lead a fully meaningful life in a virtual world," and "the world we're living in could be a virtual world" without being on that account any less real (Chalmers, 2022 [VERIFY page]). And his account is, importantly, *substrate-independent*: "the same sequence of bits," he notes, "can be encoded in all sorts of substrates — punched cards, mechanical switches, transistors, or patterns of beer cans" (Chalmers, 2022: 165). Chalmers is therefore no naïve materialist about the virtual; he is a realist about it, as I am.
 From Chalmers I adopt two things: that the virtual can be fully real, and that what matters is the structure rather than the particular substrate that carries it. Where I depart from him is *not* on substrate-independence — on that we are agreed, since I too hold that virtuality is tied to no particular matter — but on what the structure in question *is*. For Chalmers the relevant structure is *computational*: a virtual object is "a structure of bits inside a computer" (Chalmers, 2022: 198), so that, whatever substrate carries those bits, it is *computation* that makes a thing virtual. On my account the relevant structure is *semiotic*: a thing is virtual in so far as it does its causal work by what it *means* — by standing within a fabric of signs, statuses, and relations — of which the computational is only one species. The axis between us, then, is not substrate-independent versus substrate-bound; both of us are substrate-independent. The axis is *computational versus semiotic*. Chalmers, I will argue, mistakes a *species* — computational virtuality — for the *genus* — semiotic virtuality; the bit-structure inside a computer is genuinely virtual, but it is virtual because it is meaningful, not the other way around, and there are virtualities aplenty that are meaningful without being computational at all.
 The cleanest proof that the computational cannot be the genus is a case that does not presuppose my own meaning-definition in the first place: money. Money is the strongest single challenge to the computer-based view, and it is decisive precisely because it can be pressed without begging the question against Chalmers. The money in a contemporary bank account just *is* a structure of bits inside computers — it runs on exactly the computational substrate Chalmers points to — and yet no one calls the balance in their account "virtual money." We do not say it, and we are right not to, because money is still money: its identity has been invariant across cowrie shells, grain, salt, minted metal, paper, and now digital digits, none of which is what the money *is*. As Brey notices (2003), where "rocks and trees" or "screwdrivers and chairs" can only be *simulated* in a computer, "money can literally exist" there. If being a structure of bits inside a computer were sufficient for being virtual, then digital money would be virtual money — but it is not, on anyone's ordinary usage; it is simply money, in its latest medium. The identity of money is therefore institutional and semiotic, not computational: what makes it money is a status borne by a sign, and that status is the same whether the sign is shell, coin, paper, or bit. The case establishes, on grounds independent of my definition, that *computational is not coextensive with virtual* — that the bit-substrate is neither necessary (shells were money) nor sufficient (digital money is not called virtual) for virtuality. Computation is one shell that the semiotic can occupy, not the thing itself.
 Having shown by the money case that the computational is only a species, I can now extend the point downward, below the human line — to a domain where leading would have begged the question, since it is a domain that fits my meaning-definition but not Chalmers's computational one. Long before any computer, and indeed before language, animals already trade in something that does causal work by what it *means* rather than by what it physically delivers. Obara describes the gorilla's chest-drumming as a ritual the gorilla performs before entering into true combat, one that serves the function of *avoiding* a fight (Obara & Hani, 1995: 17–19). The drumming does real causal work — it establishes deference, orders the troop, forestalls violence — and it does so by what it signifies, not by any blow struck; the outcome is settled though no contact is made. The same holds for the courtship displays Obara catalogues among cranes and ibex, where a patterned performance accomplishes its intended end in the reproductive register (Obara & Hani, 1995: 22–26). These proto-virtual displays are compelled by *shared meaning*, not by bits — and, as a growing body of animal-culture studies shows ([LOOKUP cite — Whiten et al. on animal culture; de Waal; Hobaiter & Byrne on great-ape gestural meaning; add to Zotero]), such meaning is itself shared, transmitted, and answered to within the group, not the private epiphenomenon the older picture took it for. The "meaning" at issue here is to be understood *biosemiotically*, in Jakob von Uexküll's sense (Uexküll, [VERIFY citekey]): each animal inhabits an *environment-world* (*Umwelt*) populated by *meaning-carriers* (*Bedeutung*) — significances to which it is attuned and which orient its conduct — so that to speak of meaning below the human line is not to project a human category downward but to recognise the meaningful *Umwelten* in which animals already live. Biosemiotics is, in this respect, Peirce's general theory of the sign — invoked above for the *virtus* of the virtual — carried below the human line: this is the same Peircean point made one register lower, since the drumming and the display are *signs* before they are anything human, and they do their work as signs. The Enlightenment, mechanistic habit of mind — Bacon's and Descartes's — would dismiss them as biologically determined behaviours "without meaning," mere mechanism; but that dismissal is anthropocentric, and animal-cognition research no longer licenses it. Once we grant that meaning can be causally efficacious below the human line, a view that ties the virtual to the *computer* cannot be the *definition* of virtuality; it can only be the description of its latest medium.
 A disclaimer is owed here on the word "proto-virtual," lest it claim either too much or too little. In calling these animal displays *proto-virtual* I am deliberately *bracketing* — not settling — the long debate over whether animals possess reason, concepts, or anything we would dignify as full intentionality; "proto" marks that I decline to enter that quarrel, not that I have adjudicated it. What the term is meant to register, positively, is that virtuality is not a human novelty: it is a continuum that extends from animal origins into human worlds, and the human's traffic in signs is an inheritance and elaboration of something already running below the human line, not an unprecedented invention. Obara makes precisely this kind of move for ritual: his *That's Why We Cannot Get Rid of Rituals* (だから儀式はなくならない, *Dakara Gishiki wa Nakunaranai*) (Obara, [VERIFY citekey]) traces human ritual back to its animal origins, and the gesture of this thesis is the same — virtuality already has its roots in the animal. The "proto" thus does double service: it brackets the reason-debate, and it names the continuity, the rootedness of the human-virtual in something older than the human.
 One caution must be entered here, lest the word "pre-computational" claim more than I intend. In calling these displays pre-computational I am making a point about *manifest human meaning-efficacy* and about the *digital-computer substrate* — not denying that the universe might itself be, at bottom, a computation or a simulation. Chalmers is right that this is an open possibility, and nothing in my argument turns on closing it. My claim is independent of the simulation question: virtuality operates *by meaning*, trans-historically, whatever the universe's ultimate substrate turns out to be. Even if the universe were a simulation all the way down, the gorilla's drumming and the value of a coin would still do their work by what they mean — and that is the only sense of "non-computational" the argument needs.
 That virtuality is trans-historical rather than a digital novelty has, moreover, been argued independently. Massimo Durante observes that the human "has always dealt with the virtual," and that "the virtual existed long before and regardless of any technologically digitized virtual reality" (Durante, 2022: 318–19). I cite Durante not as a rebuttal of Chalmers — he does not name Chalmers, and engages a different set of interlocutors — but as independent support, arrived at by another route, for the trans-historical conception this thesis defends.
 The continuum is easy to trace once it is seen for what it is. Initially, humans told stories of gods and practised ceremonies in the hope of bettering their lives in a vast, unknown, and unpredictable universe; these orally communicated gods and embodied ceremonies formed the nonmaterial yet causally efficacious basis of our early worlds. Over time, in many places, gods were displaced by science(s) and ceremonies by laws — documented and conveyed in writing. Nowadays we increasingly receive guidance from AI and are regulated by algorithms through computer-based interfaces and objects. The medium changes — oral, written, printed, computational — while the virtuality persists. And the trajectory does not stop at the computer: some commentators imagine that the virtual will one day move beyond computational substrates altogether, to interfaces that are biologically embedded rather than screen-based. But this rests on a confusion of substrate with form. A biologically embedded or directly neural interface is a *different substrate*; it is not therefore non-computational, since computation is, as Chalmers rightly says, substrate-neutral — biological, neuromorphic, and not-yet-imagined substrates all qualify provided they implement the requisite structure. Virtuality is tied to *no* substrate whatever; that is exactly why it can survive every substrate-shift, and why no medium — beer cans, transistors, or living tissue — can be its definition.
 The money case put to work above against the computational reading carries, beyond that negative lesson, a positive one about *function* — and it is the functional point that I want to draw out here. Searle states the constitutive structure definitively. "It is only given the institution of money," he writes, "that I now have a five dollar bill in my hand. Take away the institution and all I have is a piece of paper with various gray and green markings" (Searle, 1969: 51). The form of the fact is "such and such counts as a $20 bill in our society" (Searle, 2008a: 33); and where money is electronic the point grows starker still, for "there need be no physical reality to the $1,000, other than the representation" (Searle, 2008b: 457). The monetary fact is not in the paper, and where there is no paper there is no less money.
 Brey, however, does not notice that the reason technological objects such as screwdrivers and chairs can only be simulated, and not *reproduced*, in a digital environment may not lie — as he supposes — in the functional limitations of that environment. Given that artificiality is the exosomization of our organs and memories to perform certain functions in fulfilment of our needs, those objects are not reproducible because they are *redundant* — simply *use-less*, the conditions for their specific modes of functioning being absent in such systems. A screwdriver is use-less because "tightening up something" in a digital environment calls for an algorithm rather than a physical object that screws; a chair is use-less because there is neither gravity nor the soreness of our legs that craves a physical object to rest upon. Simulating a screwdriver or a chair may then be only a matter of attending to perceptual inertia, or one day to nostalgia — much as people once used scissors and glue to cut and paste two-dimensional objects from one place to another, where now we simply cut and paste in our devices, with no need to simulate scissors and glue at all; later generations may not even know what scissors and glue were, once every act of cutting and pasting, art-making included, has been digitised.
 The use-lessness of so many material objects in digital environments exemplifies another key feature of artificial environments. While artificial environments are dependent on, and constrained by, the materiality and laws of the universe, they are not reducible to them: in Michael Polanyi's terms, they impose *boundary conditions* that the lower-level laws of physics leave open but do not determine, and so — being irreducible to those laws — they have *laws of their own* (Polanyi, 1968; 2009). In purely biological terms the physically fittest person has the best odds of surviving in the wilderness; yet in a world — an artificial environment — this may no longer hold. The fittest may face a higher mortality rate, being assigned to hunting, war, or heavy labour, while a physically unfit person turns charisma or wit into concentrated power and resources, and so into survival. Despite every human being subject to the materiality and laws of the universe, the local materiality and laws of artificial environments — the mode of artificiality, or world — that direct their members' adaptation, or domesticate them, are not reducible to the former.
 Searle's framework lets me say what *kind* of fact a monetary fact is — and also where this thesis must go further than he does. He distinguishes *brute* facts, which obtain independently of human institutions (the mass of a stone), from *institutional* facts, which obtain only within them (the value of a coin); institutional facts are erected by status functions of the form "*X counts as Y in context C*," and they carry deontic powers — rights, duties, entitlements, authorisations — that move people as surely as any physical force (Searle, 1995). I should be careful here about what I am doing with Searle. Searle himself never uses the word "virtuality"; what I undertake is not a quarrel with him but an *extension* of him. I adopt his realism about the observer-relative — that collectively recognised, causally efficacious nonmaterial structures are real, not "as if" — and I adopt his constitutive-rule analytic, "*X counts as Y in context C*." I then draw the line *wider* than he does. Searle gates the rich apparatus of status and deontic power behind language and collective acceptance, which makes institutional reality constitutively human; I draw it at the causally efficacious nonmaterial *as such*, whether or not it is language-gated. On my account every Searlean institutional fact is a virtuality, but not every virtuality need be a Searlean institutional fact.
 The warrant for drawing the line more widely is, importantly, already present in Searle's own scheme. He does not place the gorilla's display — discussed above among the proto-virtual cases — outside the realm of collective intentionality; he places it outside the *institutional*, which is a narrower thing. Social facts, in Searle's own terms, require "no cultural apparatus, cultural conventions, or language": his own instance is a pack of hyenas hunting a lion, which "and Congress passing legislation are both cases of social facts," the institutional being merely "a special subclass" of the social (Searle, 1995: 37–38). The gorilla's drumming, then, is a Searlean *social* fact — causally efficacious by meaning rather than by mass, social in Searle's full sense, but not yet woven into a fabric of stated rules — which is exactly why it counts, in the proto-virtual sense set out above, as virtuality below the institutional threshold. The extension falls at the social/institutional boundary, not at the human/animal one.
 That the line between the merely social and the properly institutional should fall *exactly* at language — and so exactly at the human — is itself a substantive commitment rather than a neutral observation, and one that animal-cognition research and posthumanist thought increasingly press. Eduardo Viveiros de Castro's Amerindian perspectivism furnishes the sharpest counter-case, and pointedly against Searle by name: in the perspectival ontology, "what we see as 'nature' is seen by other species as 'culture,' i.e., as institutional facts — what we see as blood, a natural substance, is seen by jaguars as manioc beer, an artefact," so that "these institutional facts are here universal, something that is quite foreign to Searle's alternatives" (Viveiros de Castro, 2015: 255). One need not adopt perspectivism wholesale to take its methodological point: the confinement of the institutional, and of deontic standing, to the linguistic human is a contestable boundary — and the proto-virtual, in the bracketing sense set out above, marks the place where the question is forced rather than where it is settled. Virtuality, in short, is not what computation added to the world; it is something the world has run on since before there were humans to formalise it — which is only what one should expect once the tool is granted to be "the core of humanization" (Obara & Hani, 1995: 29), the made and the human having grown up together.
 How this notion of virtuality is grounded ontologically — at the *real* stratum of the stratified picture of the real, the actual, and the empirical introduced in Chapter I — is the work of the typology that follows, where the irreducibility just established is shown to be not uniform but graded.
## D. A typology of the made
 The two distinctions I have drawn so far — the register in which an artificiality compels, conventional or structural, and the manner in which it works, through material arrangement or through virtuality — do not merely describe two unrelated facts about the made. But before assembling them into a map, I must draw the first of those two distinctions explicitly, since the section just closed drew only the second. Artificiality compels in two registers, and the difference between them will organize the rest of this thesis. In its *conventional* register the made holds the way *nomos* holds: it compels because, and only so long as, humans keep enacting it — and keep conceiving it as binding. These are the two dependencies Bhaskar identified in social structures: their activity-dependence and their concept-dependence (Bhaskar, 1998). A statute governs through the standing apparatus that enforces it — police, courts, prisons; a social norm governs through sanction; a commandment governs through belief. Let the enacting practice lapse — let belief fail, enforcement cease, the sanction go unapplied — and the artificiality lapses with it, for it was never anything but sustained. In its *structural* register, by contrast, the made compels not because anyone keeps accepting it but because it has been built into the very arrangement of the environment, so that it conditions action whether or not it is acknowledged. A locked door needs no enforcer; an algorithm sorts regardless of whether anyone believes in it; a paywall forecloses a page without convening a court. Here violation is not deterred but foreclosed — removed from the space of available actions rather than punished after the fact.
 The structural register must be characterized with care, because the temptation is to say the made has here "become nature," and that is not quite right. The structural is not bare phusis — there is nothing self-arising about a paywall or a dam; every such thing is engineered, and a designer stands behind it. It is, more exactly, phusis-in-operation: a made thing whose origin is wholly artificial but whose operation has the as-though-given character of a natural constraint, confronting the agent with the brute non-negotiability of a cliff while remaining, in its provenance, entirely a human work. When in later chapters I describe modernity's drift as a technē coming to stand as a phusis, this is what I mean — not that the made literally acquires nature's principle of self-arising, but that it migrates from the conventional register into the structural, coming to operate with the unanswerable authority of the given while never ceasing to be made. That migration, reserved for full treatment in the chapters on the metaverse, is the engine of the squeeze.
 With both distinctions in hand, the map can be drawn — and it must be drawn for the made as a whole, since the two axes apply across the board: material *and* nonmaterial alike are sorted by register, structural *and* conventional alike are sorted by manner of working, so that the grid is genuinely a two-by-two and not a list of special cases. I set the typology out here, in its own right, because it is the apparatus the later chapters will lean on most heavily, and because the map repays scrutiny: it shows not merely *that* the world is irreducible to the universe but *how* the irreducibility varies, and it locates, in one of its corners, the precise configuration the whole thesis is built to reach. Table 2-2 sets it out, and the paragraphs that follow read it across, down, and into depth.
 The columns separate the two manners of working. A *material* artificiality compels by its physical substrate: it borrows the universe's own matter and runs on the universe's own physics, foreclosing or affording not by shape and mass alone but by the whole range of physical principles its build puts into play — aerodynamics, thermodynamics, friction, leverage, conductivity, and countless more — so that to obey a material artificiality is at the same time to obey the universe's own laws operating through it. A *nonmaterial* artificiality compels by meaning rather than by any such physics — it works through the causally efficacious nonmaterial aspect of the made that I have called virtuality, a status or rule or relation that does real work in the world without being any of the matter through which it works.[^c2-meaning-semiotic] "Meaning" here I take in a deliberately broad *semiotic* key — meaning as sign-relation, the operation of and over signs in roughly the sense of Peirce's general theory of the sign — not in any narrowly human or linguistic one. The semiotic is the genus, and it has a living register and a non-living one. Its living register is the *biosemiotic*, which I take in Jakob von Uexküll's sense: the sign-relations out of which an organism's surrounding-world (*Umwelt*) is built, the carriers of meaning (*Bedeutungsträger*) by which a creature's world is furnished and addressed. I invoke the biosemiotic for one precise purpose — to de-anthropomorphize meaning at the level of the living: meaning is not the proprietary achievement of human minds, for the tick's world is woven of it no less than ours, and it is not reducible to the universe's own *real* and *actual*. The same physical stem is a path to the ant, building material to the cicada larva, and feed to the cow, and what differs across these is not the matter but the meaning impressed upon it. The semiotic also has a non-living register, and it is there that the formal occupants belong: mathematics, code, algorithms, and Stiegler's tertiary protention are sign-systems and operations over signs, not living *Umwelten*. They are semiotic — they are not biosemiotic. What unites the living and the non-living registers, and what matters for the column, is that both work by sign and relation rather than by mass and force. It is in this register — the semiotic — that nonmaterial artificiality does its work, which is why it belongs to the field of virtuality rather than to the physics of the substrate. I state the point plainly for its hardest-looking cases: mathematics, code, algorithms, and tertiary protention compel as sign-systems, not as physical principles — they are formal, technical occupants of the semiotic, and that is precisely why they fall in the nonmaterial column and not in the physics of the substrate.[^c2-semiotic-occupants] The rows separate the two registers. In the *conventional* register the made holds the way *nomos* holds: it compels because, and only so long as, humans keep enacting it — sustained by enforcement, by sanction, by belief, and lapsing the moment the enacting practice lapses. In the *structural* register the made compels not because anyone keeps accepting it but because it has been built into the arrangement of the environment itself, so that it conditions action whether or not it is acknowledged; here violation is not deterred but foreclosed, removed from the space of available actions rather than punished after the fact. Crossing the two axes yields four cells. Cell 1 is material and structural: the steam engine, the wall, the lock, the server and its devices — a made physical form that forecloses by its very build. Cell 2 is material and conventional: the fork that calls for etiquette, the ceremonial object, the vehicle answerable to a traffic-rule — a material thing whose force lies not in its substance but in a use-norm that humans must keep enacting around it. Cell 3 is nonmaterial and conventional: speech, fallible knowledge, the gods, money, the institution, customary law, narrative, the scientific paradigm — pure made-meaning, sustained in being by nothing but collective enactment. Cell 4 is nonmaterial and structural: the formal order — mathematics, codified grammar, the algorithm — and, at its limit, code-as-law and artificial intelligence, made-meaning that has been built into operation so that it binds without an enforcer.
 **Table 2-2. The typology of artificiality.** Each cell is read at three depths: its *enduring conditions or principles* (the deepest layer — what organizes the made into what it is), its *structures or dynamics* (the particular arrangements that order embodies), and the *context-specific phenomena* in which it is encountered. The parenthetical illustrations within the conventional and structural rows trace a single continuum, by which an artificiality migrates from convention toward structure — set out in full after the table.

|  | **Material** (compels by physical substrate; runs on the universe's matter and laws) | **Nonmaterial** (compels by meaning; substrate-independent — the field of virtuality, §IIC) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Structural** (compels by the arrangement of the situation; needs no ongoing enactment; foreclosure, not deterrence) | **Cell 1** — the steam engine, the wall, the lock, the server and its connected devices. *Enduring conditions/principles:* the universe's own organizing principles (mechanics, thermodynamics) operating on the substrate. *Structures/dynamics:* this engine, this lock, this server as configured. *Phenomena:* this running engine; this bolt thrown; this sensor reading. | **Cell 4** — the formal and the automated. *(b) "structural-still-conventional":* mathematical formulas, algorithms-as-notation — once established, followed without enforcement *and* not violable, yet conventional in origin and self-propelling of nothing. *(c) "real-compelling":* code-as-law, AI, the smart contract — it *propels and limits against contingency* (structurally compelling without being deterministic: it pre-empts the field of choice rather than fixing each outcome — Hui); the genuinely new occupant. *Enduring conditions/principles (real-compelling):* the gained capacity to forcibly propel and limit against contingency — self-completion (自足完成化, *jiko kanseika*), "technē & nomos becoming phusis." *Structures/dynamics:* the formalism; this AI, this contract as configured. *Phenomena:* this proof; this automated decision executed off the loop. |
| **Conventional** (compels by ongoing human enactment; ≈ *nomos*; violation deterred, never foreclosed) | **Cell 2** — the fork answerable to etiquette, the ceremonial object, the vehicle answerable to a traffic-rule. *Enduring conditions/principles:* the use-norm as an enduring instituted principle. *Structures/dynamics:* the standing convention of use (table manners, the highway code). *Phenomena:* this meal eaten correctly; this lane kept. | **Cell 3** — speech, fallible knowledge, the gods, money, the institution, customary law, narrative, the scientific paradigm. *(a) pure conventional:* sustained wholly by enactment. *(a′) "conventional-becoming-structural":* codified grammar — once established, followed without enforcement, **but still violable**. (The prime marks a developed form *within* (a), not a new category: a → a′ tracks one step along the migration continuum, still inside the conventional cell.) *Enduring conditions/principles:* the institution, belief, or convention as enduring instituted principle. *Structures/dynamics:* its enactments (a court in session, a rite performed). *Phenomena:* this ruling; this transaction; this sentence spoken. |

 The single most important thing the table shows is not visible in the cells taken one at a time but in the path that runs *through* them, from the bottom-left of the nonmaterial column to the top-right — for the conventional and the structural are not a partition but the two ends of a continuum, and the made migrates along it. The pure case of the conventional sits at one extreme: spontaneous human speech, the institutions, the gods, the money, the customary law of Cell 3 — each compels only by being kept up, and each may at any moment be violated, defied, or simply allowed to lapse. The first step toward the structural is taken by *codified grammar*. Once a grammar has been written down and established, it is followed without anyone enforcing it — no constable stands over a sentence — and in this it has begun to harden out of the purely conventional; yet it remains, decisively, *violable*: one can break it, and be understood breaking it, which is why I would file it within Cell 3 as a *conventional-becoming-structural* case rather than promote it across the line. (I label this case a′ rather than give it a new letter precisely to mark that it is a developed *form of* the conventional — a-prime, a stage within (a) — and not a fresh category alongside it; the lettering tracks the migration continuum itself, a conventional → a′ conventional-becoming-structural → … → the structural cells, so that the prime records movement *within* a register rather than a crossing out of it.) The next step is taken by *mathematical formulas and algorithms*. These too are followed without enforcement, but they differ from grammar in a way that matters: they *cannot be violated* — there is no breaking the rule that two and two make four, only erring in the attempt to follow it — and so they have crossed into the structural register and belong in Cell 4. And yet they are *structural-still-conventional*: their structurality is real but their origin is conventional, a human formalism, and, crucially, they *do not propel themselves*. A formula sits inert until a human (or a machine a human has set running) takes it up; it constrains without compelling, foreclosing error without forcing anything to happen. The continuum's far end is reached only by *code-as-law and artificial intelligence*. Here the made-meaning does not merely refuse violation; it *propels and limits against contingency* on its own — the locked transaction that will not execute, the model that decides and acts where no operator intervenes. It is essential, at just this point, not to mistake *structural* for *deterministic*. To compel structurally is to bind by the arrangement of the situation rather than by ongoing enactment; it is not to fix every outcome in advance. There are forces that propel and limit without determining — that narrow the field of the possible, weight it, foreclose some paths and incline toward others, yet leave the particular issue underdetermined — and artificial intelligence is the exemplary case. A generative or probabilistic model does not compute a single fated output the way a falling stone computes its trajectory; it is non-deterministic in operation, its products contingent on sampling, on training data, on a prompt it could not have foreseen. And yet it is, in the strongest sense, structurally compelling: it propels action and bounds it, against contingency, on its own initiative, without any party keeping it up. Non-determinism, then, is no objection to placing it in the structural register; it is precisely the kind of structural compulsion the metaverse stage introduces — propulsion-and-limitation without fixed determination.
 Yuk Hui's account of *preemption* names this combination exactly. The algorithmic system does not coerce by foreclosing choice outright; it appears, on the contrary, to offer choices — and that is the point. "Such a determination is always preemptive," Hui writes, "in the sense that the machine has already anticipated what the options will be: In this case, freedom means choice"; what looks like an open field of options is in truth "a reduction of the contingent to the most probable," the menu itself already drawn up by the structural conditions of the algorithms and their training data.[^c2-hui-preemption] The choices are real, and they are non-deterministic — which option the user takes is not fixed in advance — yet the *space* of choice has been pre-structured by the recursive evaluation of past data that "anticipate[s] the future, which in turn determines the present." This is structural compulsion that works *through* apparent freedom rather than against it, and it is why I locate code-as-law and AI at the far, real-compelling end of the continuum: they propel and limit against contingency precisely by pre-empting the field within which contingency would otherwise play. This is the *real-compelling* occupant of Cell 4, and it is genuinely new. It is what I name, with a term the later chapters will carry, *self-completion* (self-sufficient completion, 自足完成化, *jiko kanseika*, "the made completing itself"): the made acquiring, at the level of its enduring organizing principles, the capacity to drive and bound action against the contingent — and it is here, and only here, that the provocation "technē & nomos becoming phusis" earns its keep, for this is the cell that was empty until the metaverse stage and that the metaverse fully occupies. The conjunction is deliberate: the structural-nonmaterial corner takes in both the made-*technical* (the formalism, the model, the executing system) and the made-*normative* (code-as-law, the smart contract that *is* the rule it enforces), so that what migrates into the structural register is at once technē and *nomos* — code-as-law is exactly *nomos* becoming phusis, the made norm acquiring the unanswerable bite of the given.
 Reading the typology in depth — across its three layers rather than its four cells — guards against a confusion that the migration just traced makes tempting, and it is here that the stratification does its sharpest work. Take Cell 1's lock. At the layer of *context-specific phenomena* the lock is this bolt, thrown now, in this door. At the layer of *structures or dynamics* it is the locked door as a standing arrangement — the configured constraint that forecloses passage. But what compels at the deepest layer, the layer of *enduring conditions or principles*, is not the door at all; it is the law beneath the structure — the rigidity of the metal, the friction and resistance that no act of will can talk its way past, the universe's own organizing principles to which the door merely gives a human shape. The structure (the locked door) is the *arrangement*; the *law* — the organizing conditions and principles that make the arrangement bite — is the real beneath it. The same threefold reading disciplines the new cell. For code-as-law it is essential not to mistake the layer at which the novelty lies. A particular artificial intelligence, a particular smart contract, is a *structure or dynamic* — a configuration, one made thing among others, which could have been built otherwise. What is genuinely new sits one layer deeper: the *gaining* of a real-level capacity to forcibly propel and limit against contingency, the organizing principle that any such configuration now embodies. Keeping the configuration (a structure) distinct from the capacity (an enduring principle) is what lets me say that the metaverse occupies a cell the formal sciences never did, without the absurdity of claiming that this or that particular algorithm is itself the deep principle. The kind — "the structural-nonmaterial that compels at the level of its organizing principles, against contingency" — is the enduring feature; any given system is only its passing instance.
 Two cautions keep the typology from hardening into something it is not, and both are needed before the later chapters put it to work. The first is that the cells name a dominant tendency, not an absolute partition: almost every real artificiality mixes registers, and the honest way to read the table is as a field of gravitational centres rather than a set of sealed boxes. The railway makes the mixing plain. Its rails, its electrical circuits, its signalling interlocks belong to the *structural* register — they foreclose, by their build, what a train can physically do, and a points-lock will not pass a train onto an occupied line whatever the driver intends. But its design specifications, its operating protocols, its published timetable belong to the *conventional* register — they hold by being enacted, are revisable in principle, and can be, and routinely are, departed from. A railway is neither a structural nor a conventional artificiality; it is a working composite of both, and to ask which cell "the railway" occupies is to ask the wrong question of the table. The second caution concerns the continuum itself: the migration from convention toward structure is a directional tendency the later chapters will track, not a ladder every artificiality is fated to climb. Codified grammar need not become a formula, nor a formula become code; the point of arranging the cases along a single line is to make legible the *drift* — the steady conversion of what was negotiable into what is merely operative — that I will argue defines the metaverse stage, in which the conventional margin shrinks and the structural-nonmaterial, real-compelling corner fills out. That corner, the deepest cell of the grid, is where the made comes to foreclose with the unanswerable authority of the given while remaining wholly, visibly authored — as hard as a cliff and as made as a promise — and the account of what it is to live squeezed inside it is the work of the chapters to come.
## E. The double edge of the made — and the squeeze it prepares
 The previous section fixed *what kinds of made there are*: the grid of register and aspect, with one cell — the structural-virtual — newly populated at scale. This section asks what follows from that fact, and the answer is not a catalogue of risks. It is a single claim, and I want to state it before defending it, because everything in the chapters ahead leans on it.
 I argue that artificiality carries its harm not as an accident that befalls it from outside but as a tendency internal to its own working: artificialities at sufficient scale, or sufficient depth of social embedding, tend to generate the very conditions of the harm they were instituted to relieve, *unless* active counter-work holds the tendency off. This is stronger than the familiar observation that tools have unintended consequences. Unintended consequences are external — a misuse, a side-effect, a failure of foresight that better design might have caught. What I am claiming is that the inversion is *built in*: it follows from the autonomy established in the last section, the made running ahead of its maker, and it would arrive even if every maker were prudent and every user well-intentioned. The double edge is not a flaw in the artificiality; it is the shape of the artificiality.
 That autonomy is worth fixing precisely, since the whole claim rests on it. Because the made has organizing conditions and principles of its own — this was the burden of the typology — it acquires, in the aggregate, a momentum of its own. A tool, once made, has affordances and constraints of its own: the structures, at the level of the actual, that its material properties make possible, those properties standing as its organizing conditions at the level of the real. Once tools are in use, their affordances, constraints, and material properties beget further tools; and the world of tools, once underway, develops in a direction no single maker chooses. This is nothing more mysterious than self-organization: as the made compounds, its parts come to depend on and enable one another, and the whole acquires a directionality that none of its makers authored. The made then turns back upon its maker and remakes him — the human that built the artificial environment began, within it, to be bred by it, recall that the brain was finished by the world it had started to build — and so there opened a gap between the biological animal the human remains and the accelerating artifice it now inhabits. Obara names that gap a dis-synchrony (ズレ, *zure*): "the change in the systems and the things produced, and the change in the biological human, differ in speed and in kind, and a *zure* arises." That gap, appearing first at the scale of the single mismatched body, is the earliest form of the structure this thesis calls the squeeze.
 This is not my discovery, and the strength of the claim rests on its not being mine alone. Five traditions, developed in settings that had never seen a metaverse, each arrived independently at the same structural fact, each naming a different mechanism by which the inversion proceeds, a different counter-work that holds it off, and a different failure-state when the counter-work is absent. I set them side by side not to borrow their authority but because their convergence is itself the evidence: a structural property of artificiality-at-scale, diagnosed five times over from five vantages that did not consult one another.
 **Table 2-3. Five diagnoses of the made's tendency-toward-inversion.**

| Tradition | Mechanism of inversion | Counter-work that holds it off | Failure-state when counter-work is absent |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| The *pharmakon* (Stiegler, reading Derrida reading Plato) | Every exteriorised making is at once remedy and poison; which it becomes is undetermined by the artifice itself | *Care* — the cultivated, time-taking relation that keeps the remedy a remedy | Drift to the poison-edge under time-economies that foreclose care |
| The two watersheds (Illich) | Past a second threshold a tool inverts its function and works a *radical monopoly* — it eliminates the conditions under which alternatives could exist | Threshold-vigilance; conviviality; deliberate scale-limitation | Radical monopoly: the structural elimination of the unmediated path |
| Self-petification (Obara; self-pet-ification, 自己ペット化, *jiko petto-ka*) | Having sealed itself inside its own artificial ecosystem, the human is bred to the capsule; biological capacities atrophy because they go unexercised | Preserving the ecological exposure that keeps biological capacity in use | Capsulised life in which somatic competence rots unfelt |
| "The noble person is not a vessel" (*jūnzǐ bù qì*, 君子不器; *Analects* 2.12) | Institutional artificiality tends to reduce the human to a vessel (*qì*, 器), legible only as its function | Self-cultivation (*xiūshēn*, 修身) of the capacities no function exhausts | Generalised instrument-ification (*qìhuà*, 器化): human-being-ness collapses into what the apparatus can read |
| The dialectic of Enlightenment (Adorno & Horkheimer) | The reason that emancipates becomes the instrumental reason that administers | *(developed in Chapter IV.A)* | *(developed in Chapter IV.A)* |

 Read down the third and fourth columns and the convergence is plain: the counter-works differ, but each is a labour that runs *against* the artificiality's own momentum, and each fails the same way — not through the tool's breakdown but through its success. I take the four traditions whose home is here in turn, briefly, and reserve the fifth almost entirely for the chapter where it belongs.
 The *pharmakon* is the oldest name for the double edge. In the *Phaedrus* Plato has Socrates report the Egyptian myth of Theuth, who offers writing to King Thamus as a remedy (*pharmakon*) for memory, and Thamus answers that the same gift is a poison: it will breed forgetfulness, an outer mark standing in for inner recollection. Bernard Stiegler, by way of Derrida, generalises the lesson to every made thing: once a making is exteriorised from the body it gains an independence from its maker, and in that independence it can return as either cure or contagion. "Any tertiary retention is a *pharmakon*," he writes — any of the material supports into which we offload memory and mind (Stiegler, 2016: 32). Writing extends memory and atrophies it; the question of which is never settled by the artifice but by what Stiegler calls *care* — the cultivated, time-taking relation that keeps the remedy a remedy. The pharmacological disposition is decided by the regime, not by the artifact. Here is the first diagnosis of a pre-metaverse squeeze: the made already pulled two ways, and only a labour of care holding it to the curative edge.
 Illich gives the inversion its historical mechanism. In *Tools for Conviviality* he argues that every industrial institution crosses two watersheds: at the first it achieves genuine effectiveness, delivering on its promise; at the second its effectiveness turns against its purpose, and it begins to produce the opposite of what it was built for. Past that second threshold a tool exercises not a commercial but a *radical monopoly* — not market dominance over a need, but the elimination of the conditions under which the need could be met by any other means. His clearest case is the automobile: "That motor traffic curtails the right to walk, not that more people drive Chevies than Fords, constitutes radical monopoly" (Illich, 1973: 65). The car achieves its monopoly not by outselling its rivals but by remaking the city so that walking ceases to be a way of getting anywhere. This is the second diagnosis of a pre-metaverse squeeze, and the one whose full development this thesis reserves for Chapter VI, where the car-restructured city is worked through as the precursor case: an artificial ecosystem grown so encompassing that the unmediated path out of it has been engineered away.
 Obara reaches the same structure through biology rather than philosophy or sociology. The human, on his account, is the animal that secreted its own ecosystem and then settled inside it — and, settling, came under selection pressures of its own making, a process he names auto-artificial selection (self-induced human selection, 自己人為淘汰, *jiko jin'i tōta*). The benign form is self-domestication; the severe form, which he sees gathering in modern sealed life, is self-petification (self-pet-ification, 自己ペット化, *jiko petto-ka*): a total capsulisation in which the biological capacities of the organism atrophy because the capsule no longer calls on them. The whole made world around us, he writes, is "the *tools* and things (*mono*, 物) that self-domesticate the human" — televisions, toys, the relations they engender, the pets, the furnished room.[^c2-obara-petification] The diagnosis is exact and unsettling because the failure-state is not felt as failure: the lapdog does not know it has lost the yard — not, at least, until its own biology recoils, in the diseases of civilisation that register the loss the mind does not. This is the third diagnosis of a pre-metaverse squeeze — the squeeze registered in the body, as competence quietly going out of use.
 The fourth diagnosis is older than all of these and comes from a tradition that built its counter-work into an ethics. "The noble person is not a vessel" (*jūnzǐ bù qì*, 君子不器), in the *Analects* (2.12), is a negation that states a positive: the cultivated person is precisely what cannot be reduced to a single function, to a vessel (*qì*, 器) good for one use and legible only as that use. The Confucian tradition understood, that is, that institutional artificiality at scale tends toward instrument-ification (*qìhuà*, 器化), the reduction of the human to the role the institution can read; and it answered with self-cultivation (*xiūshēn*, 修身), the cultivation of capacities no office exhausts — a counter-work whose seeds I take up again in the Conclusion. This is the fourth diagnosis of a pre-metaverse squeeze, and it has no other home in the thesis but this one. I note, tentatively and for later correction, that the late-imperial examination system in its eight-legged essay (*bāgǔ*, 八股) form may furnish a worked historical case of the diagnosis turned real. The eight-legged essay was the rigidly formatted prose form made mandatory in the late-imperial civil examinations, prescribing a fixed eight-part structure into which every candidate's argument had to be poured; and it is dehumanising in precisely this thesis's terms, for it subordinated the mind to a template — a training of the person into a vessel, an instrument-ification (器化) of exactly the kind the ideal of the noble person who is not a vessel was meant to forbid. A bureaucratic artificiality, that is, whose selection criteria narrowed, over centuries, the very kind of person who could pass through it.
 The fifth tradition I name and then set aside. Adorno and Horkheimer's dialectic of Enlightenment gives the inversion its most uncompromising form — "Myth is already enlightenment, and enlightenment reverts to mythology" (Horkheimer & Adorno, 2002: xv) — but the labour of unfolding that claim, and of showing how the reason that emancipates becomes the reason that administers, belongs to Chapter IV.A, where it does its proper work; to attempt it here would be to import the engine of a later chapter into the closing of this one.
 What unites the four, and the fifth, is not a shared dislike of artificiality. It is a shared discovery about its structure, and the discovery is what licenses the move the rest of the thesis makes. For if the inversion is internal — if the made tends to turn against its purpose by its own logic rather than by misuse — then the metaverse-stage condition is not an avoidable corruption of the structural-virtual artificialities mapped in the last section. It is what those artificialities *become* when the tendency every tradition named is carried to its limit. The metaverse is not a misuse of cell-4 making; it is cell-4 making come into its own.
 And so each diagnosis points past itself. None of the five describes a metaverse — they could not — but each describes a tendency that the metaverse-stage does not abolish but *radicalises*, and the radicalisation takes a distinct form in each case, which I can state here and elaborate in Chapter VI. The first is **rate-compression**. The *pharmakon* always required care to hold it to its remedy-edge, and care is slow — it moves at the pace of a life, of a generation, of a culture learning to use what it has made. The structural-virtual artificiality mutates at the pace of its own optimisation, in months; care cannot be paced to meet it; and so the mismatch that was contingent in the case of writing becomes structural, the inversion outrunning the counter-work as a matter of design rather than of neglect.
 The second is **typological migration with monopoly-extension**. Illich's second watershed was crossed, in his cases, by artificialities of brick and asphalt and institution — the car, the school, the clinic — and it took them a century or more to cross it. The same dynamic, migrating into the structural-virtual cell, accelerates by an order of magnitude and widens its reach: where the pre-metaverse radical monopoly captured a specific function — mobility, schooling, healing — the platform monopoly captures the conditions of *any* function, mediating participation, recognition, and livelihood as such. The path out is not narrowed; it is removed.
 The third is **scope totalisation** — the mode named by the Frankfurt tradition, and, like that tradition, developed elsewhere (Chapter IV). The administered domain that instrumental reason carved out of social life ceases to be a domain and becomes the encompassing environment: the outside contracts toward zero. I state the mode and pass on.
 The fourth is **structural enforcement**, or instrument-ification (器化) generalised. What was, for the Confucian diagnosis, a *tendency* under institutional conditions — a pressure the cultivated person could resist, in a culture that prized the resistance — becomes the operative logic of a field in which what cannot be measured, optimised, or rendered legible is, by that fact, unsupported. The not-a-vessel ideal (不器) loses its institutional ground; the vessel is no longer one form a life may decline to take but the only form the apparatus can register.
 These four are not four phenomena. They are four readings of one transition, and the transition is the subject of the rest of this thesis: the artificial side of the universe–world–metaverse triad acquiring, at the structural-virtual cell, its own stratum of enduring laws — a made order operating with the authority of a given one, as the previous section's grid already foresaw. Read as the *pharmakon*'s, the transition is the inversion-cycle outrunning the care-cycle; read as Illich's, the second watershed migrating and totalising; read as Obara's, the capsule grown seamless; read as the *Analects*', the vessel made compulsory. Each is the same event seen from one tradition's vantage. And that event — the made acquiring its own laws-stratum, and so standing as a second given against the first — *is* the squeeze, the structural condition this thesis is written to name. The double edge of artificiality, which pre-metaverse making already bore, is what the metaverse-stage sharpens to a point; the convergence of these five diagnoses is the warrant for treating that stage not as one more instance of artificiality-at-scale but as its limit case. Why it is the limit case, and not merely the latest case, is the question Chapters IV through VI take up.
 
# III. The Enlightenment's Disposition to Universalization
 Chapter II argued that artificiality is the human mode of survival, and that the world it builds is irreducible to the universe beneath it. That argument was made about artificiality as such — about what is common to every human world, from the Roman household to the Maya court to the digital platform. This chapter narrows the lens. It concerns one mode of artificiality among the many that human history has produced, and it advances a single thesis about that mode: that the form of world-making born of the European Enlightenment carries an intrinsic disposition to universalization — a tendency to overwrite the organizing conditions of every other world it meets, and to carry its own made world as though that world were the given universe itself. This disposition belongs to one mode; it is not a property of artificiality in general. Establishing that it belongs to one mode, and not to all, is the precondition for the squeeze hypothesis that the rest of the thesis develops.
 Two terms must be held apart from the outset, because the whole argument turns on the distinction. The universe, in the sense Chapter I fixed, is the given substrate — the Umgebung, the surrounding world that is there independently of any inhabitant, answering, in Rémi Brague's phrase, to neither a god nor a man.
