Universalization is something else entirely: it is a property not of the universe but of a world. It is what happens when one made world — one Umwelt, one inhabitant's worked-over surroundings — comes to treat itself as the Umgebung-of-all, the single given frame within which every other world must be measured and placed. The universe does not universalize; only a world can, and only by mistaking itself for the universe. When, in the chapters on the metaverse, I describe a made order that has begun to operate with the unanswerable authority of the given — a tekhnē that has come to stand as a phusis — it is the terminal case of exactly this movement that I will mean.
 A note on vocabulary follows from Chapter II and governs this chapter too. I do not use technology for the wide field of the made; that word has narrowed, in ordinary use, to a domain of devices, and the mode I am describing reaches into metaphysics, law, and the stance a civilization takes toward nature, none of which is a device. I use artificiality, in the broad sense already defended. And I retire the prefix neo-, which marks a thing only as coming after something else without saying what it is; where I need to name the most recent phase of the mode, I will call it metaverse-stage artificiality, naming the phase by its destination rather than by its mere succession.
 The argument proceeds in eight movements. The first establishes that there is a genuine plurality of human worlds, and recovers, beneath that plurality, the synchronic squeeze between a world and the universe that underlies every one of them. The second turns to collapse, reading it as that squeeze in its extreme form — a mismatch, not an unfitness — and locating in the survivors of collapse the regeneration base on which every recovery has drawn. The third earns the name: it argues why the mode in question is rightly called the Enlightenment mode, rather than Western or modern, and identifies the universalization of the sovereignty of the subject as its defining characteristic. The fourth denaturalizes that sovereignty by setting it beside ways of being a self — Buddhist and Daoist among them — that do not posit it, so that the sovereign subject is seen as one configuration among others rather than the human condition. The fifth and sixth give the mode its conditions of emergence — material in the fifth, nonmaterial in the sixth — without making either into a claim of superiority. The seventh meets the most natural objection head-on, that a mode which has universalized must thereby be the fittest, and shows why universalization is not fitness but its opposite. The eighth turns the mode's disposition inward, toward the pursuit of a life lived wholly as intended, and so hands the argument to Chapter IV.
## A. The plurality of worlds and the squeeze beneath them
 In the criteria of AOS in Chapter I, I already show the incommensurability of personhood across different human worlds as AOSs. Implied by such incommensurability is the distinct ways of organizing each AOS, including persons as its components. Historical accounts of cross-civilizational encounters help illustrate such plurality. The rejection of Britain's trade request by Emperor Qianlong of the Qing Empire to Macartney, while likely read as infamously arrogant and ignorant today (which is not necessarily wrong), some truth about my definition of plural worlds as AOSs is manifested in it: Not only the kowtow and tribute-vs-trade disagreement surfaces the difference in the operating logic from interpersonal to international relations between Qing and Britain, the claim that "Our Celestial Empire possesses all things in prolific abundance and lacks no product within its borders. There is therefore no need to import the manufactures of outside barbarians in exchange for our own produce" also shows Qing's AOS has achieved its own homeostasis (at least from the system operator's perspective) among the system, its members, and the local universe as its background environment, showing resistance against external interference on this homeostasis. This is not only seen in the international relations policy of the Qing Empire, but also not uncommon and other civilizations or even tribal societies (although this is not to make a normative judgment about it). 
 
 Philippe Descola's comparative anthropology provides one of the possible ways of comprehending the plurality of worlds with their underlying operating logic – the different ontologies on which the configuration and functioning of a world is founded. He identifies four schemas by which human collectivities distribute the two properties he takes to be basic — interiority (an inner life, intention, soul) and physicality (bodily form and material substance) — across the beings of the world (Descola, 2013). Animism grants a shared interiority but different physicalities: the Achuar and Makuna of the Upper Amazon treat animals and plants as persons with souls like their own, living in their own longhouse societies behind merely different bodies. Totemism shares both interiority and physicality within a class joined by common descent: in Aboriginal Australia a human group and its totem — the Kariera and their kangaroo — are alike the embodiment of one underlying essence. Analogism multiplies singular beings and reweaves them through correspondences, as in the Renaissance Great Chain of Being or the Chinese cosmos of resonances, where society, man, and world are known by analogy alone. Naturalism — the schema of the modern West, and, as Descola insists, the exact inversion of animism — does the reverse: it grants one shared physical nature, studied by the sciences, beneath a multiplicity of distinct human interiorities we call cultures. 
 These are not four cultural decorations of one underlying reality. They are four different ontologies — four different settlements of what kinds of being there are and how interiority and physicality are parcelled among them — and each furnishes a distinct foundation on which whole families of worlds, with their own persons, kinds, and modes of artificiality, can be built. The animist substrate grounds one range of possible worlds; the analogist substrate grounds another; the naturalist substrate grounds the worlds of the modern West.
 Naturalism, which feels to those inside it simply like seeing the world as it is — one real nature studied by the sciences, over which cultures lay their merely cultural interpretations — is itself only one ontological foundation among several, no more given than the others; and it is the particular foundation upon which the Enlightenment mode of artificiality, the subject of this chapter, raises its world. I take Descola's four not as a closed or exhaustive grid but as a demonstration that beneath the visible plurality of worlds there lies a plurality of ontologies, each capable of bearing many worlds, of which the naturalist is one.
 
 What this plurality of ontologies grounds is a plurality of actual worlds, not a plurality of possible worlds in the logician's sense, nor a plurality of cultural versions of one real world. The worlds are actual — really built, really inhabited, really structuring the lives lived within them — and they differ, as Chapter I put it, all the way down to their enduring organizing principles.
 
 Beneath this plurality lies a structure common to every world, and recovering it is the second task of this movement. Chapter I and Chapter II described a squeeze of one kind: the mismatch between an inhabitant and its environment that opens across time, as inherited structures come to lag a changed world. The Great Oxidation Event of Chapter I and the hominin mismatch of Chapter II were squeezes of this kind — dyadic, because they set one inhabitant against one environment; and diachronic, because the gap opens over time, the inherited equipment falling out of step with surroundings that have moved on. But there is a second squeeze, and it is the one this thesis is finally about. It is triadic and synchronic. An inhabitant of a world stands, at one and the same time, within two environments whose demands need not agree: the made world it actually lives in, and the given universe that world is built upon and never escapes. When the demands of the two diverge — when what the world requires of its inhabitant and what the universe permits begin to pull apart, down to their enduring organizing principles — the inhabitant is squeezed between them. This is the universe–world squeeze, and unlike the diachronic mismatch it is not a lag that time might close; it is a standing tension built into the very condition of living in a made world laid over a given one.
 Obara's ecological vocabulary lets us state the tension precisely, and more substantially than a single word can carry. The human lives within an artificial ecosystem (人工生態系, jinkō seitaikei) — a made web of provision, built of tools and their derivatives, that has displaced the natural niche so thoroughly that "even soil and air are, in our time, no longer primal nature" (Obara, in Obara and Hani, 1995). Obara's image for our predicament is the coral: the human is the polyp that has built the reef of the city and can no longer live outside it. Within that reef a dis-synchrony (ズレ, zure) opens, and Obara is exact about its two dimensions — it is a gap of speed and of kind at once: "the change of the system and of the things produced (モノ, mono), and the change of the human as an organism, differ in both speed and quality, and a zure arises between them" (Obara, in Obara and Hani, 1995). The made web races; the biological human barely moves; and the gap between an exponential artifice and a near-static animal is the standing condition of the artificial ecosystem.
 Uegaki hardens this slippage into something sharper, and names its architecture. Where Obara sees a zure that strains, Uegaki describes a severance (切断, setsudan) — a cutting-apart of what was once continuous — and stages it as a sequence of historical "singularities," each cutting a tie the previous age had taken for granted. The first is the mediation of the human and nature (〈人間〉と〈自然〉の間接化, ningen to shizen no kansetsuka): the made world inserts itself between the human and the given, so that the relation between them ceases to be direct and becomes routed through artifice. This is not yet a cut but an interposition — the very work this thesis has named world-construction as an adaptational operating system (AOS) in Chapter I, the layer through which the human henceforth meets the universe. The second singularity is the severance of society from nature (〈社会〉と〈自然〉の切断, shakai to shizen no setsudan): once fossil energy releases the social order from the carrying capacity of its territory, society "continues to expand itself by the logic of society alone, ignoring any consistency with nature." Here interposition becomes severance proper — the made order no longer answers to the given but follows its own momentum, which is precisely the diagnosis of the Western rise that Pomeranz and Miura supply below. The third, and the one that defines our own moment, is the severance of society from the human (〈社会〉と〈人間〉の切断, shakai to ningen no setsudan): the social order so fully provisions existence that, "for the first time in seven million years of human history, living no longer requires the existence of flesh-and-blood others" (Uegaki, 2022a). This last severance is the condition that the type-3 and type-4 squeeze will name in Chapter VI — the human held apart not only from the natural given but from the fellow humans through whom a world was always made. The three are thus not a flat list but a deepening: an interposition that becomes a severance from nature and then a severance from one another, each stage pulling the made world further from the substrate it was built upon. That progressive severance is the squeeze in its severe form, and it is the form toward which, this thesis will argue, the Enlightenment mode tends; Uegaki's diagnosis is revisited, and pressed harder, in Chapter VI.
 One qualification must be entered with care, because the whole moral of the chapter depends on it: to say that a world can be squeezed against the universe is not to say that it must be. The squeeze is a danger built into world-making, not a fate sealed by it — and human traditions have known the danger and built against it. What such traditions hold in common is not a doctrine but a default orientation: the made world is understood as embedded within the given, and the human as answerable to it. The relation runs in that direction — the human answers to the universe and to the world it sustains — and it is precisely this direction that the Enlightenment mode reverses, setting the subject over the given and demanding that the given answer to it instead. Embeddedness and answerability are the baseline; sovereignty is the departure from it.
 The Sinic figure of harmony (he/ho, 和/合) is one mode of that baseline, and worth marking precisely because the English "harmony" misleads.[^harmony-heho] In its own idiom it names not a settled, finished state but a processual attunement — a never-completed effort to be in accord with a counterpart, to co-become with it. Classical formulations make the point: harmony without sameness (和而不同, hé ér bù tóng), in which accord is achieved between differences rather than by erasing them; and harmony generates the myriad things (和實生物, hé shí shēng wù), in which accord is generative, the very source of plurality rather than its dissolution. [VERIFY: locus + translation source for 和而不同 / 和實生物, or recast as the author's own gloss] So understood, the Confucian unity of Heaven and humanity (天人合一, tiānrén héyī) names a world deliberately tuned to keep its made order answerable to the given one, and the Daoist principle of non-action (wu wei, 無為) — not inaction but, in Needham's rendering, "refraining from activity contrary to Nature" — describes a stance that "produces without possessing, acts without self-assertion, and develops without dominating" (Needham, 1956). Yet harmony (he/ho) is only one approach to embedding the made world in the given. The impulse is not uniquely Chinese: Descola's animism is another such settlement — the Achuar and Makuna of the Upper Amazon meet animals and plants as persons with souls like their own, a relation of negotiation rather than dominion (Descola, 2013) — and the Andean ideal of good living (sumak kawsay, buen vivir) and the Māori principle of guardianship (kaitiakitanga, the care of land and water held in trust across generations) each articulate, in their own idiom, the same binding of the made order to the given one. [LOOKUP: verify and cite sumak kawsay / kaitiakitanga, or substitute already-verified Descola/Viveiros examples] These are not claims that such worlds were ever perfectly realized, nor that they were superior; the point is only that traditions have articulated embeddedness and answerability as constitutive values, and in doing so held the squeeze at bay. A mode of artificiality that abandons that answerability for mastery does not merely differ in style from them; it courts the very squeeze they were built to prevent. The Enlightenment mode, I will argue, is such a mode.
## B. Collapse and the regeneration base
 If the universe–world squeeze is real, then there should be a recognizable thing that happens when it grows severe — when the slippage between a made world and the given universe widens into a fracture. There is, and we call it collapse. I want to read collapse not as a verdict of unfitness on the world that suffers it, but as the universe–world squeeze in its extreme form: a mismatch between a world's organizing arrangements and the conditions the universe has come to impose, severe enough that the arrangements can no longer be sustained. The distinction between mismatch and unfitness is the whole of the matter, and the fall of the Ming dynasty, as reconstructed by the historian Timothy Brook, shows why.
 Brook's recent work assembles what he calls the longest series of prices in China before the eighteenth century, using, in his words, the price of rice to quantify the disaster and track its course through the dynasty's final decades (Brook, 2023: 106, 5). The series tracks the grain-price crisis of the 1630s and 1640s to its source, and Brook's verdict is unambiguous:
It was global climate, not global trade, that drove Ming grain prices to crippling levels. (Brook, 2023: 105)
 The Little Ice Age — a planetary cooling that shortened growing seasons across the northern hemisphere — pressed on a state whose granary system, the ever-normal granaries built to adjust the price of grain in times of dearth (Brook, 2023: 111), was never designed for a climatic shift of that depth or duration. The world's standing arrangements, adequate to the ordinary fluctuations of its given environment, could not absorb an extraordinary one. Brook is careful, and his care is instructive, to make the fall neither purely natural nor purely human:
The dynasty was not singlehandedly destroyed by climate, but its collapse cannot be explained in the absence of climate and human responses to climate. (Brook, 2023: x)
 This is the universe–world squeeze stated as historiography. The given universe shifted; the made world's arrangements lagged; the gap between them widened until, as the price regime broke, the political regime broke with it, and Beijing fell in April 1644 (Brook, 2023: 131). In his earlier survey Brook had already described the dynasty passing through a series of what he calls sloughs — troughs of climatic and epidemic stress — and concluded that their combination was what brought down the house of the Ming (Brook, 2010: 70–71, 255). The fall was a mismatch grown fatal, not a confession that the Ming world had been, all along, less fit than the world that followed it.
 Two consequences follow from reading the Ming fall this way, and both bear directly on the chapter's thesis. The first concerns what came after. The dynasty that replaced the Ming was not the Ming restored but a different world altogether. The Qing was a Manchu conquest régime that governed through what Pamela Crossley calls a simultaneous emperorship — a rulership addressed in Manchu, Chinese, Mongolian, Tibetan, and Arabic at once, each constituency met in its own idiom rather than absorbed into a single Chinese one (Crossley, 1999: 10–11). Mark Elliott describes the Qing order as one of ethnic sovereignty, securing a pax Manjurica rather than a pax Sinica, and enforcing its distinctness through the queue and the Eight Banners (Elliott, 2001: 2–6). The new order did not merely sit beside Han identity; it reshaped it by force. The queue edict compelled Han men, on pain of death, to shave the forehead and wear the Manchu braid, conscripting the very surface of the body into the conqueror's order; and the literary inquisitions (文字獄, wénzìyù) of the Qianlong reign policed the written word for disloyalty, destroying texts and authors alike in the course of the imperial book-collecting project. Identity was altered from the outside, by decree over hairstyle and costume, and from the inside, by control over what could be thought and written. This was a different adaptational operating system, in the sense Chapter I gave that term — a different settlement of persons, kinds, and rule, imposed in part by overwriting the markers of the world it replaced — not the previous one repaired. That collapse opens onto a genuinely different world, rather than restoring the old, is itself evidence for the plurality this chapter defends: if there were only one viable human world, collapse could only ever return us to it.
 The second consequence concerns what survives. Collapse, the archaeological record suggests, is almost never total. Joseph Tainter's analysis treats collapse as a rational economizing — a society facing negative marginal returns on its own complexity sheds that complexity and returns to a lower, more sustainable level of organization (Tainter, 1988). And what makes recovery possible afterward is that something is always carried across the discontinuity. Glenn Schwartz and John Nichols, surveying cases of regeneration after collapse, state the principle directly:
A crucial factor in regeneration is the survival of institutions or ideas from the era before collapse, supplying a base for the eventual re-creation of complex societies. (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006)
 Call this the regeneration base: the stock of surviving arrangements — institutions, techniques, memories, ways of organizing a world — from which the next world is rebuilt. Because collapse is almost never total (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006: 6), the base is almost always there. And here the argument reaches its hinge. The regeneration base on which the human species as a whole draws is not the residue of any single world but the plurality of worlds itself: the fact that, when one way of organizing existence fails, others persist from which recovery can be drawn. A mode of artificiality that universalizes — that overwrites the organizing conditions of every other world and presses toward a single planetary monoculture — does not merely dominate the others. It consumes the regeneration base. It narrows the species' stock of alternative ways of being to one, so that a failure of that one has nothing to recover from. Plurality, on this argument, is not a luxury of cultural richness; it is the species' regeneration base, and its erasure is the gravest cost of the Enlightenment mode's disposition to universalize.
## C. Why "the Enlightenment mode"
 I have been calling the mode whose disposition this chapter analyzes the Enlightenment mode of artificiality. The name needs to be earned rather than assumed, and earning it requires two steps: first to take, from authoritative accounts of the Enlightenment, the thread that this thesis means by the term; and then to show why that name is more precise than its two natural rivals, Western and modern. I take the first step here.
 A clarification must come first, because the word, left unglossed, will mislead. By the Enlightenment mode I do not mean the eighteenth century — not the Aufklärung as a period, not Kant's essay and the philosophes, not a chapter in the history of European ideas. I mean a metaphysical disposition rather than a date: enlightenment in the widest sense, not the property of a century but of a stance. The eighteenth century is where the stance reaches self-consciousness and gives itself its proudest name — the crystallization, not the origin, and not the whole.
 What, then, is the stance? By the Enlightenment mode I mean a mode of artificiality whose defining characteristic is the universalization of a single figure of the self: the sovereign subject. Adorno and Horkheimer gave the widest version of the idea when they spoke of enlightenment not as a century but as a stance that "has always aimed at liberating human beings from fear and installing them as masters of a disenchanted world" (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 1); and I will return to them, because they saw something real. But mastery is not where the mode begins. It begins one step earlier, with what the contemporary Japanese philosopher Uegaki Takahide calls the human's spatio-temporal self-subsistence (時空間的自立性, jikūkanteki jiritsusei): the assumption that the human can stand as "a single individual substance, a subject, prior to the others and the systems of meaning that surround it" — a self that has been, in his exact word, dis-embedded (脱埋め込み, datsu-umekomi) from the connectedness of existence that had always constituted it (Uegaki, 2022a). The clearest philosophical portrait of this self is the one Uegaki sets against his own position: Max Scheler's world-open (weltoffen) human, the being that, alone among the living, is "not bound to drives and environment, but 'environment-free' and … 'world-open': such a being has 'world'" (Ein solches Wesen ist also nicht mehr trieb- und umweltgebunden, sondern 'umweltfrei' und … 'weltoffen': Ein solches Wesen hat 'Welt'; Scheler, Die Stellung des Menschen im Kosmos, 1928 [VERIFY printed page — Frings 2009 / Meyerhoff 1961]). For Scheler the human, possessed of Spirit (Geist, 精神), suspends its reflexive drives and so is "freed from the fetters of the environing world" (environing world = Umwelt, 環世界, kankyō-sekai, Uexküll's term), becoming a free being raised above it (Uegaki, 2022a: 90). I cite Scheler not as a claim I endorse but as the purest expression of the very stance this thesis is written to correct — the picture of a human who has overcome the Umwelt, escaping the given into transcendence, a face of the dis-embedded sovereign subject. Uegaki rebuts it on the page (2022a: 90–91): world-openness is no transcendence from the Umwelt but a peculiar structure within the human's own environment, and that own environment is an artificial one. The thesis follows Uegaki, and joins to him Obara's account of the human's artificial ecosystem: the human does not escape the Umwelt; it builds a peculiar, made one. Scheler's "world-open" subject is thus the sovereign subject in its most flattering self-description — the dis-embedded self mistaking the environment it has rebuilt for an environment it has left behind. This is also the figure Charles Taylor, tracing its modern emergence, calls the buffered self, sealed against a world to which an earlier self had been porous (Taylor, 2007 [VERIFY page]); and the one Michael Sandel names the unencumbered self, the self imagined as prior to its ends and attachments. To picture such a self fully, Sandel observes, "is not to conceive an ideally free and rational agent, but to imagine a person wholly without character, without moral depth" (Sandel, 1998: 179 [VERIFY edition pagination]; first stated in Sandel, 1984: 90) — a self Uegaki himself invokes in just this connection (Uegaki, 2022b: 112). The mode's first move is not to dominate the given but to raise a self above it.
 With that elevation comes a reversal of the direction of answerability, and it is the reversal, more than any particular act of mastery, that the rest of this thesis tracks. The embedded human of the older worlds stood answerable to its surroundings: it attuned itself to a given it did not author, reading what the world asked of it. The sovereign subject inverts the relation. It takes up what Uegaki describes as a kind of "God's-eye view" (神の視点, kami no shiten) over an environment, a body, and other persons now reduced to objects to be known (Uegaki, 2022a) — the very standpoint Scheler's weltoffen human claimed in overcoming the Umwelt, here redescribed not as a liberation from the given but as a posture toward it. And from that height the given no longer makes demands but owes them — it is the world that must answer to the subject, furnishing what the subject takes to be its due. Artificiality is the instrument of this reversal. It is the means by which the recalcitrant given is offloaded and entrusted — Uegaki's to entrust (委ねる, yudaneru) — to a made order that will be made to deliver what the subject intends, so that the subject need no longer bend itself to the world. The phrase "reversal of answerability" is my own redescription; the structure it names — the dis-embedded subject, the God's-eye standpoint, the entrusting of the work of living to the made — is Uegaki's.
 Here the most important clarification of the chapter must be entered, because the familiar critical vocabulary of "domination" and "control" tends to swallow it. To install the sovereign subject is to confer an entitlement: the given is now the subject's to dispose of as it sees fit. Domination — the active mastering of the world, the bending of the given to the intended — is one way the subject may exercise that entitlement. It is not the entitlement itself. The endowment may be claimed, and then the mode wears its mastering face: the nature-subduing, tradition-displacing, world-overwriting reason that Adorno and Horkheimer, Bacon,[^bacon] and Descartes,[^descartes] all in their different registers describe. But the same endowment may equally be declined — and that it can be declined, without the subject ceasing for a moment to be sovereign, is the surest sign that sovereignty, not mastery, is the root. Controllability, then, is not the mode's essence but the operative principle of one of its faces; I borrow the term from Hartmut Rosa, who shows the modern relation rendering the world verfügbar — which can be rendered not only "controllable" but "available," "disposable," "at one's disposal," or "accessible"[^verfugbar] — along four dimensions,[^rosa-dims] and I subordinate it deliberately: it names what the sovereign subject does when it chooses to master, not what makes it sovereign.
 For the sovereign subject has more than the mastering face, and the others are just what a diagnosis pitched entirely at "domination" cannot see. One is stewardship — dominion exercised as care. The endowment may be discharged not by subduing the given but by tending it, and this benevolent disposal is now widely taken as the very premise of environmental protection. Its charter text is the same one that licenses mastery: the human is told to "have dominion … and subdue" the earth (Genesis 1:28) and also "to till — to work — and keep" the garden (Genesis 2:15). Lynn White Jr. read the first as the root of the crisis — "Christianity … is the most anthropocentric religion the world has seen," teaching "the rightful mastery of [man] over nature" — and proposed Francis of Assisi, who would unseat "man's limitless rule of creation," as its corrective (White, 1967: 1205, 1206–1207). But stewardship, for all its gentleness, does not leave the structure. The steward still receives the given as his to keep; answerability still runs from the given up to the subject who answers for it; the disposal is benevolent, but it remains a disposal. Stewardship is therefore not an exit from sovereignty but a third way of exercising it — alongside mastery and the withdrawal to which I now turn. When mastery fails, or wearies of itself, the same dis-embedded self does not return to embeddedness; it withdraws. It turns to the disengagement Uegaki anatomizes as listlessness (無気力, mukiryoku), resignation (諦め, akirame), and finally the void (虚無, kyomu) — the condition of a self "cut off from the connectedness of being" (Uegaki, 2022a). In the contemporary Sinosphere this face has found its own name in the refusal called lying flat (躺平, tǎngpíng): the individual, taking its life to be answerable to no one, declining to strive at all. Mastery and withdrawal are not two moods but two exercises of one entitlement — the sovereign subject disposing of a given it takes to be wholly its own, in the one case by remaking it, in the other by refusing it. And it is exactly this second face that marks the limit of the great critical-theoretical accounts. For a theory that has defined the mode as domination, the withdrawn, nihilistic, lying-flat self is an embarrassment it can absorb only by declaring it deceived: Adorno and Horkheimer must read it as the work of a culture industry that manufactures consent, and Habermas as a "fragmented consciousness" that "blocks enlightenment by the mechanism of reification" (Habermas, 1987: 355). Each must treat the withdrawal as a failure to see. The sovereign-subject diagnosis needs no such auxiliary: it reads mastery and withdrawal as the two native dispositions of one elevated self, neither of them a mistake about the self's true interest, both of them the self exercising precisely the sovereignty the mode conferred. (Uegaki's ethic of non-intervention (不介入, fukainyū) belongs not here, among the pathological symptoms, but downstream: it is an ethical principle he derives from the as-intended axiom — one must not intervene in another's life-as-intended, each bearing full responsibility for its own (Uegaki, 2022a: 77, 278). I carry it forward to Chapters IV and VI as the self-completing regime's live-and-let-live ethic, of which the listless, lying-flat withdrawal is the two-sides-of-one-body (表裏一体, hyōri-ittai) underside.) The lying-flat refusal and nihilism are the thesis's own extension of Uegaki's withdrawal-structure, not his terms.
 It is now possible to take from Adorno and Horkheimer exactly what they offer and no more. Their verdict that "enlightenment is totalitarian" (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 4), that the mode seeks to "use [nature] to dominate wholly both it and human beings" (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 2), is the truest description we have of the mastering face — of what the sovereign subject does when it claims its endowment to the full. I keep it for that. What I do not take is its sufficiency: read as the whole of the mode, the verdict can accommodate neither the withdrawn face nor the stewardship face, and so must dismiss half of what the present plainly shows as false consciousness. Adorno and Horkheimer are, in this chapter, the indispensable witness to one of sovereignty's faces — not the name of the disposition itself. The full reckoning with their account, and with Habermas's correction of it, belongs to Chapter IV, where the three thinkers are set side by side.
 What makes universalization the present chapter's concern is that the controllability-relation does not stay within the world that bred it. A mode of artificiality is, in this thesis's terms, one actual world among many — one way of constituting a meaning-space within the given universe — and most such worlds have been content to be local, knowing themselves as one way among others of inhabiting the given. The Enlightenment mode is not. It carries its own made world as though that world were the given universe itself; and wherever it meets another world — another way of being a self, often a relational or world-embedded one — it does not encounter it as a peer but overwrites its organizing conditions, recoding them in the terms of the sovereign subject: the relational person remade as the self-subsistent individual, the world-embedded self as the buffered one. It does this by conquest where it must, but by seduction where it can — and the seduction is the more powerful, for the mode does not only impose the sovereign subject by force; it offers it. To a self bound in a web of mutual dependence it offers the sovereign individual's freedom, the relief of being answerable to no one — the very entrustment-felt-as-freedom that Chapter IV will anatomize. That a world can be overwritten by appeal as readily as by force is precisely why the mode is so hard to see and to resist: it arrives not only as a conqueror but as a liberator. That is the outward face of the mode, and it is what this chapter tracks: not the mode's internal dynamics, which wait for Chapter IV, but its comportment toward the worlds it displaces.
 Why, then, the Enlightenment mode rather than the more usual labels for this universalizing world? Consider first Western. The word names a region, and naming the mode by a region commits two errors. It essentializes: it implies that this disposition to mastery and universalization is somehow proper to the peoples or places of the geographical West, a feature of their character rather than of a particular historical settlement that arose among them and could have arisen, or be adopted, elsewhere. And it mislocates the mode in time and space, for the mode has already outlived Western political dominance. A future in which the leadership of this mode has passed to East Asia, or anywhere else, would not be the refutation of the mode but its fullest confirmation — the mode universalized so completely that it no longer needs a Western body to carry it. This is the precise point at which my question parts from a familiar one. Ian Morris and others ask which region leads — why the West rules, for now, and who may rule next (Morris, 2010). That is a real question, but it is orthogonal to mine. I am not asking which region runs the world; I am asking which mode of artificiality rules, whoever runs it. A change in the regional leadership of the mode leaves the mode, and its disposition, entirely intact.
 That this universalizing should have issued from the West, and not elsewhere, is the question the literature on the great divergence has long worried, and the thesis takes from it a deliberately modest lesson. Needham asked why the systematic, controlling science that remade the world arose in Europe and not in a China that had long been its technical equal; Pomeranz answered, for the economic divergence, that much was contingent — coal where it happened to be needed, and the windfall of New World land and labor — rather than written into any civilization's essence. To this material story the thesis adds a metaphysical layer: that what the West universalized was, before any particular technology, the controllability-relation itself; and that the universalizing was rarely gentle. Miura's reading of Locke shows the mechanism in miniature. The labor that mixes the self into the world converts the given into property; the earth is to be subdued and made over to the industrious and rational; and on that ground the lands of others — judged unimproved, merely natural — become appropriable without their consent, so that a theory of property does the work of a justification of conquest, the ownership of nature bound up with domination over alien peoples (Miura, 2006: 95–99). The overwriting of worlds is not a figure of speech; it has a documented history, and the controllability-relation is its principle.
 Yuk Hui gives this confirmation-by-adoption its sharpest form. What is spreading across the planet, he argues, is a monotechnology — "a single technical-cosmological program presenting itself as the one rational way to inhabit the world," which he ties to Vandana Shiva's "monoculture of the mind" (Hui, 2024: 225–226) — and its spread is achieved precisely under the banner of pluralism even as it dissolves the plurality it claims to honor: the slogan of pluralism, on his argument, has been accompanied by the disappearance of other cosmologies. Against this Hui sets the projects of cosmotechnics and technodiversity — the recovery of plural ways of relating the technical to the cosmic and moral order. For my argument, Hui's monotechnology is the terminus of the Enlightenment mode's disposition to universalize: the point at which the mode has so completely overwritten its rivals that only one way of world-making remains visible, and it no longer looks like a mode at all, but simply like the world.
 Consider, second, modern. This label fails in the opposite direction: where Western says too much, fixing the mode to a place, modern says too little, fixing it to nothing but a date. To be modern is only to be recent, to come after some earlier thing; the word is an empty temporal index that names no content. And because it names only recency, it generates an endless regress of qualifiers — modern, then late-modern, then post-modern, then post-post-modern — each marking a further interval without ever saying what the thing in question is. The mode I am describing has a content: a specific disposition, a specific stance toward nature and toward other worlds, traceable to a specific intellectual settlement. Enlightenment mode names that content; modern merely dates it, and dates it badly. For these reasons I keep the name. It is not a gesture of either praise or blame toward the historical Enlightenment, whose achievements and whose critics this thesis takes seriously in equal measure. It is a precise designation of one mode of artificiality, identified by its disposition, so that the disposition itself — not a region, not a period — can be the object of analysis.
 A discipline must travel with the name — the same one this thesis imposes wherever it speaks of the West. To call this the Enlightenment mode is not to say that the West is this mode, or that a civilizational essence drives some peoples toward control and spares others. The mode is a strand: one tendency, selected and radicalized out of a far more various inheritance. Its eponymous ancestors are conventionally named Platonic (the idea set over the given) and Aristotelian (the given known and ordered), and I will use those names — but as eponyms, not histories. Greek thought was never reducible to Plato and Aristotle, still less to idealism and empiricism; the sophists, the mystics, the atomists, the Stoics, and the Skeptics all stand off that axis, and neither Plato nor Aristotle was the modern idealist or empiricist the labels evoke. What the Enlightenment mode did was not to inherit the Greek mind but to select two strands from it, sharpen them, and universalize them, while the others — the traditions of withdrawal, of attunement, of dwelling within the given — were subordinated or forgotten. The mode is a distillation, then, and not a destiny; which is exactly why it might have been otherwise, and why to name it is to identify a tendency, not to convict a civilization.
 None of this is yet a verdict. To say that the Enlightenment mode meets the given as a thing to be controlled, and that it universalizes that relation over the worlds it meets, is to describe a comportment, not to indict it: the freedoms and the masteries this mode has won are real, as the chapters to come will insist as firmly as they insist on the cost. What the description fixes is only the shape of the thing whose expansion this chapter follows. Its eventual collision with the very given it declared itself independent of — the return, in Rosa's word, of an uncontrollability the mode can neither absorb nor abide — is what the later chapters will name the squeeze.
## D. The sovereign subject as one configuration among others
 To name the mode by the sovereignty of the subject is to make a claim the mode itself cannot easily hear: that this sovereignty is a configuration — one settlement of what a self is — rather than the shape of the human as such. The point does not require an exhaustive taxonomy of selves, and I do not attempt one; I have already drawn the distinctions the argument needs, and a comprehensive typology here would make empirical claims the argument can do without. It is enough to set the sovereign subject beside some ways of being a self that do not posit it, so that it is seen as a selection and not a given. Consider three, offered as illustrations rather than as a closed grid. There is the self-subsistent self that masters the given — the sovereign subject this chapter studies. There is the self-subsistent self that withdraws from it — the renouncer who keeps his separateness but turns from mastery to release: the contemplative of the desert; the yogin of classical Sāṃkhya, for whom liberation (kaivalya) is the discernment that pure consciousness (puruṣa) is not, and never truly was, entangled in nature (prakṛti) (Larson, 1979); the soul of Advaita Vedānta seeking to recognize its non-difference from Brahman behind the veil of a world deemed illusion (māyā) (Deutsch, 1969). This withdrawing-but-still-separate self is the ancient cousin of the modern nihilist and lying-flat refusal noted above — both decline mastery without dissolving the separate self.
 And there is a third settlement that does the decisive work, because it declines at the root the very separateness the first two share. This self is not un-separated — collapsed into an undifferentiated continuum — but embedded: nested within a given whose layers of irreducibility it does not erase. Embeddedness keeps the strata — the person within a world, the world within the universe; what it refuses is the elevation of the self above them. The Buddhist teaching of no-self (無我, anātman) holds that there is no abiding, self-subsistent self to be sovereign in the first place — "this is not mine, this I am not, this is not my self" (SN 22.59, in Bodhi, 2000). The Daoist equalizing of things (齊物, qí wù, in the Zhuangzi) levels the self with the myriad things, refusing it the privileged standpoint from which sovereignty could be claimed: "Heaven and earth were born at the same time I was, and the ten thousand things are one with me" (Zhuangzi, ch. 2, trans. Watson, 2013). Where the sovereign subject begins from a self set over a world, the embedded self begins from no such elevation — and from layered nestedness, not flat fusion.
 The contrast must be drawn with one discipline above all: this is not a contrast between a sovereign West and a self-dissolving East, a romance this thesis refuses. The declension of the separate self has its own Western lineages — Meister Eckhart's apophatic releasement (Gelassenheit), in which the willing self is emptied so that God may be born in the soul (Walshe, 2009); and Schopenhauer, who read the principle of individuation (principium individuationis) as a veil of māyā over a deeper undivided reality, drawing the thought, by his own account, from Indian sources (Schopenhauer, WWR I, §63). One nuance marks a genuine difference rather than a likeness to be smoothed over: the Western mystical self-emptying is an emptying into God, a transcendent ground that receives the dissolved self, whereas the Buddhist no-self dissolves into no ground at all. Both decline the sovereign subject; they do not decline it identically. What is at issue, then, is not a geography but distinct settlements of what a self is — and the embedded settlements show that the separateness on which the sovereign subject is built is something some traditions decline outright.
 These traditions are invoked here for one purpose only, and it is important to be exact about what that purpose is not. They are not offered as a program to be adopted; this thesis counsels no one to become a Buddhist or a Daoist, and it does not imagine that the predicament it will diagnose is one an individual escapes by private enlightenment. The squeeze, as the later chapters will show, is a structural and collective condition, not a knot a single soul unties by adjusting its metaphysics of the self. What these configurations offer is not an exit but a vantage — the one vantage from which the sovereignty of the subject can be seen at all, because they are standing outside the water long enough to see that it is water. That is the whole of their work in this argument; the navigation they might suggest is reserved for the Conclusion, and held there under the same anti-prescriptive discipline.
## E. The material conditions of the mode
 Having earned the name and fixed the disposition, I turn to the conditions under which the Enlightenment mode emerged — first the material conditions, in this movement, then the nonmaterial conditions, in the next; the core of the comparative synthesis developed across both movements I first set out in Yang (2024). A caution governs both. To give a mode its conditions of emergence is not to call it superior. I am not explaining why the better mode won; I am explaining how a particular mode, with a particular disposition, arose where and when it did. The whole burden of the previous movement was that the mode's dominance is no proof of its fitness, and nothing in the account of its conditions is meant to take that back. What the material conditions explain is not a victory but a possibility — how a set of fortuitous circumstances allowed one interventionist world to break a constraint that had held its rivals in check.
 Joseph Needham spent a scholarly lifetime on the question these conditions answer: why the systematic, controlling science that remade the world arose in early-modern Europe and not in a China that had long been its technical equal. His answer was never that Europeans were cleverer; it was that a web of material conditions ran differently in the two civilizations and produced, at its far end, two different relationships between knowledge and nature. I take that web from him — but as a web, not a chain. The relations are reciprocal, not a single line of dominoes: geo-ecology shapes a mode of production, which shapes institutions and a stance toward nature, which shapes a characteristic science — but the science funds the military competition that rewards it, the institutions entrench the mode that produced them, and the metaphysics licenses the very interventions that confirm it. With that caution, the strands can be set out.
 The first strand is the mode of production and the stance toward nature it breeds. Where a civilization lives primarily by tending plants, Needham observed, "the less he interferes with the growth of his crops the better… they follow their Tao" — and he found the disposition crystallized in a parable: "Let us not be like the man of Sung. There was once a man of Sung, who was grieved that his growing corn was not longer, so he pulled it up" — and killed it (Needham, 1956). The would-be improver who yanks the seedling to hasten it is the very figure of interventionism rebuked; the agrarian world's wisdom is non-action (wu wei), "no action contrary to Nature." Where a civilization lives instead by herding and seafaring, the psychology inverts. Following André Haudricourt, Needham traces the contrast to the work itself: the command attitude is bred by the labour of herding and seafaring — "the cowboy or shepherd drives his animals about," and "the sea-captain gives orders to his crew" — so that "pastoral dominance over animals consorts well with legislation over things as well as men" (Needham, 1956). The cosmos such a people pictured was a machine of separable parts under externally legislated law, where the agrarian cosmos was an organism to be accorded with.
 One feature of this command-psychology must be drawn out, because the disposition does not confine itself to the universe. The command Haudricourt describes does not stop at animals: "pastoral dominance over animals," in Needham's words, "consorts well with legislation over things as well as men," so that "law in Nature would have been derived from the masteries of shepherds and sea-captains as well as kings" (Needham, 1956). The same command-and-control comportment is exerted upon whatever it takes as its object. The West exerted it, historically, upon bonded human beings — mass chattel-slavery, "such as one found in the Mediterranean civilizations," and largely absent from a China "modelled… not [on] slavery, but [on] free farmers" (Needham, 1969b): the command turned upon men. And it exerted the same comportment, in the interventionist science of the modern age, upon nature: the command turned upon things. Chattel-slavery and interventionist technoscience are not cause and effect; they are two objects of one disposition — command over men and command over things — both downstream of the masteries of shepherds and sea-captains. (This is to be kept distinct from Needham's separate, economic observation that mass slavery also retarded labour-saving invention; that concerns why China gained an early technical lead, not the command-disposition as such.)
 The second strand is institutional, and it is the heart of Needham's "why not China." The European side ran through "the aristocratic-military feudalism of Europe, out of the womb of which mercantile and then industrial capitalism… could be born" — a hereditary landed aristocracy, on terrain fragmented by sea and forest into many jostling small lordships, out of whose decay an autonomous merchant class and the city-states arose. The Chinese side ran through "an extremely elaborate civil service… the 'mandarinate', enjoying no hereditary principle… but recruited afresh in every generation," atop a social order whose "classical enumeration of the four ranks" placed the merchants dead last (scholars, farmers, artisans, merchants). That mandarinate "nipped capitalist accumulation in the bud" and "creamed off… the best brains… into its own service," so that science kept a "relatively 'official' character" — the astronomer "a civil servant lodged… in part of the imperial palace" rather than an independent inquirer (Needham, 2004). Needham's own hinge is exact: "whoever would explain the failure of Chinese society to develop modern science had better begin by explaining the failure of Chinese society to develop mercantile and then industrial capitalism" (Needham, 1969a, ch. 1, "The Role of the Merchants" [VERIFY page against P:\books — print page not embedded in the PDF; passage confirmed in ch. 1]). The same hinge is developed in Within the Four Seas (Needham, 1969b), to which the citation can be re-anchored if The Grand Titration is not added to Zotero.
 The third strand is military, and it loops back into the second. Fragmented Europe was a field of "numerous warring nations… jostling military aristocrats," and "in the West war was mainly a conflict between nations," a perennial competition that "gave… stimuli… to science and technology." Unified China, "a coherent agrarian land-mass… since the third century B.C.," had civil wars but, in Needham's striking phrase, "internal national conflicts never," and "correspondingly there was to some extent an absence of the stimuli that war gave to science and technology in Europe" — so much so that gunpowder, lacking knightly cavalry or feudal castles to overturn, "produced no perceptible effect upon the age-old civil and military bureaucratic apparatus" (Needham, 2004). The intergenerational private accumulation that hereditary aristocracy and then mercantilism permitted both funded and was spurred by that competition; in China the non-hereditary mandarinate and the suppression of commerce starved the same loop.

Table 3-1. Two modes of world-making and their material conditioning (after Needham).

| | Sinographic sphere (East Asia) | Mediterranean littoral (proto-Europe) |
|---|---|---|
| **Mode of production** | Self-sufficient settled agriculture; its hydraulic basis (water-works, flood control) demanded large-scale coordination, yet the imperial bureaucracy's reach into everyday peasant production stayed limited — "heaven is high, the emperor far away" (cf. the "Asiatic mode of production"): central coordination for taxation, conscription, and waterworks, not for production itself. | Animal husbandry (Needham's term) together with oared and manned seafaring and trade; terrain fragmented by archipelago, peninsula, and forest, scattering land among small, independent feudal lords — a hereditary landed aristocracy. |
| **Ontology** | Organicism: the cosmos as a living, self-regulating whole, known by correlative resonance (yin–yang, the five phases) — "a mirror image," in Needham's phrase, of an integrated bureaucratic society. | Mechanism: the cosmos as separable parts moved by externally legislated law — a machine to be taken apart and reassembled. |
| **Praxis / stance toward nature** | Non-intervention: wu wei, accord, attunement, harmony; an observational and correlative science, "propitious for the natural sciences, yet non-experimental." | Intervention: command, mastery, the subordination and obedience of the object; an experimental science pursuing "the more complete… domination of nature." |

 Two disciplines must be observed in reading this table, and they pull in the same direction. The first is Needham's own. For all that the right-hand column describes the mode this thesis is most worried about, Needham never reads the contrast as a verdict of East-good and West-bad. The left-hand column is a different configuration, not a moral victory; the organicist mode had its own limits, including the non-experimental character that, on Needham's account, kept its considerable natural science from becoming the experimental science of early-modern Europe. The second discipline is the one on which I must part from Needham. His grand narrative bends, in the end, toward teleology: the many ethnic cultural rivers of the world's sciences flowing at last into the one ocean of modern science, a single universal knowledge toward which all the partial knowledges were tending. That image is precisely what this thesis denies. There is no ocean toward which the rivers were flowing; there is one mode of artificiality that universalized, and presented its universalization as the ocean. I take from Needham the comparative chain — the material conditioning of a stance toward nature — and leave behind the teleology that would make the mechanist outcome the destination of all the others. One guardrail must be stated before the contrast can do any work. The opposition is ideal-typical, not a description of two uniform blocs: each civilization is internally diverse and shot through with exceptions and counter-currents, and the table makes no claim that every Chinese thinker was an organicist or every European a mechanist. What the contrast explains is a dispositional tendency — the enduring organizing principles that gave each mode of world-making its characteristic slope — not the local particulars of any one place, period, or thinker. It is a claim about the underlying disposition of each mode, not a census of its instances.
 That the European escape from the agrarian constraint was contingent, and not the unfolding of any civilizational superiority, is the burden of Kenneth Pomeranz's revisionist economic history, and it reinforces the modesty this movement requires. Western Europe around 1750, on Pomeranz's account, was a none-too-unusual economy; its most advanced region, England, was not obviously ahead of the most advanced regions of China. The Lower Yangzi delta, he shows, consumed about as much cloth per capita in 1750 as Britons did in 1800, which prompts his pointed reversal of the usual question — not why China failed to become England, but why didn't England become the Yangzi Delta?, locked like it into an advanced but involuting agrarian economy (Pomeranz, 2021: 23, 27). What broke England out, on his analysis, were two crucial discontinuities, one created by coal and one by colonies (Pomeranz, 2021: 7). Coal happened to lie in particularly fortunate places — near the surface, near water transport, near the manufacturing core — so that its exploitation owed as much to geographic accident as to ingenuity, an advantage Pomeranz calls fortuitous, and specifically English (Pomeranz, 2021: 66, 72). And the New World furnished a vast ghost acreage — land and resources, worked in great part by coerced labor, equivalent to tens of millions of acres England did not have to find at home — whose contribution Pomeranz reckons to have exceeded even that of coal (Pomeranz, 2021: 274–276). The escape, in short, was bought with a windfall of fossil energy and appropriated land, not earned by a superior way of being.
 If Pomeranz explains how the constraint was broken, the Japanese philosopher Miura Mitsuru explains why the breaking, once begun, entrenched itself — why a fortuitous escape hardened into a self-reinforcing mode. The mechanistic conception of nature, Miura argues, was not merely a theory but a convenience: by recasting nature as pure matter wholly devoid of anything mental, it freed the human, in his words, from the old religious view of nature, from taboos, reverence, and ethical restraint, and so answered precisely to the demand of capitalism and the modern state for domination over nature (Miura, 2006: 162). The metaphysics and the political economy locked together: a stance that licensed unlimited intervention served interests that rewarded it, and the reward entrenched the stance. The colonies completed the circuit, fixed as resource-supply zones within what Miura, following world-systems theory, calls a core-periphery structure that, he observes, still basically persists today (Miura, 2006: 233–234). And the whole arrangement carries, built into it, a vicious cycle (悪循環, akujunkan): a mode that, in Miura's phrase, at the very moment it achieves its specific purpose produces new evils requiring further intervention to manage (Miura, 2006: 156). Most telling for the argument of this thesis is the dependence engine Miura traces in a later essay (Miura, 2004: 15–16): a region pinned as a supply-zone and market is driven to export great quantities of resources to obtain foreign currency, exhausting its own ecological base in the process — and the structure persists, as he puts it, basically intact, long after the formal empires that built it have gone. The material conditions of the Enlightenment mode, in sum, did not merely permit its rise; once in place, they wound it tighter, converting a contingent advantage into a self-perpetuating disposition to intervene. That self-reinforcement at the material level is the counterpart, in this movement, of the universalization the chapter is named for; the next movement turns to its nonmaterial engine.
## F. The nonmaterial conditions of the mode
 The material conditions of the previous movement explain how the mode could arise and entrench itself; they do not explain the feature that makes it dangerous. A mode of artificiality may be interventionist without being universalizing — may master its own corner of the world without claiming to be the measure of all worlds. What converts intervention into universalization is something at the level of how the mode knows: a specific structure in its way of relating thought to being. This movement traces that structure, and its spine is a single diagnosis, drawn from Roy Bhaskar's critical realism, to which I add one extension and around which I arrange a set of corroborating witnesses.
 Bhaskar locates the deepest fault in Western philosophy at what he calls the Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line — the inheritance, running from the two thinkers whose names this thesis uses as eponyms rather than histories, of a single recurring move — a diagnosis I have applied to modern knowledge in Yang (2024). He gives that move a name: the epistemic fallacy.
 
The epistemic fallacy consists in the view that statements about being can be reduced to or analyzed in terms of statements about knowledge; i.e. that ontological questions can always be transposed into epistemological terms. (Bhaskar, 2008: 25) 
 The fallacy, in plainer terms, is the reduction of the question what is there? to the question what can we know?, so that being is silently rewritten as the knowable, and whatever does not fit our way of knowing is denied the status of the real. Stated plainly, the epistemic fallacy de-realizes the world: it lets our knowledge stand in for what there is. In Bhaskar's own terms, it collapses the intransitive dimension — the real structures and mechanisms that exist and act independently of us — into the transitive, the historically given materials of inquiry.[^epistemic-transitive] Bhaskar calls the result an ontification of knowledge — the dressing-up of our epistemic categories as the furniture of the world itself — and ties it to what he names identity-thinking, the refusal of any reality that does not answer to the knowing subject's own terms (Bhaskar, 2008).
 But the epistemic fallacy never travels alone, and naming its companion is what this section adds, because the companion is the deeper form of the disposition this whole thesis tracks. Bhaskar gives it a name of its own: the ontic fallacy. If the epistemic fallacy reduces being to knowledge, the ontic fallacy runs the move in reverse — Bhaskar's compact phrase for it is the "compulsive determination of knowledge by being" (Bhaskar, 2008a). Stated plainly: our made knowledge is projected onto the universe as the universe's own structure — the map mistaken for the territory. Where the epistemic fallacy lets our way of knowing swallow what is independently there, the ontic fallacy takes our historically produced, fallible knowing and installs it as the world's own eternal furniture.[^ontic-symmetric]
 The two are not alternatives but accomplices. Bhaskar speaks of the "tacit complicity" of the epistemic and ontic fallacies — "twinned fallacies" that "characteristically presuppose each other" (Bhaskar, 2009: 16): a way of knowing that has first reduced being to its own knowable then projects that knowable back onto the world as its very furniture, fusing world and knowledge into a single closed loop in which the human's construction confronts the human as the given itself. This loop is the recurrent figure of the whole thesis, named here in its philosophical form. It is the hypostatization — treating something made (a concept, an idea) as if it were a free-standing thing in the world — that returns at every level of the argument: the dis-embedded subject of the previous movements, hypostatized from a useful abstraction into a metaphysical fact; the made order that, in the chapters on the metaverse, comes to stand with the unanswerable authority of a given — a tekhnē that has come to function as a phusis, a made order operating with the authority of the given. The ontic fallacy is that movement caught at the level of knowledge: the made mistaken for, and installed in the place of, the given.
 The consequence is structural, and it is the hinge of this whole chapter; it is now the joint work of the two fallacies, not the epistemic alone. A way of knowing that has installed itself as the measure of being:
inevitably splits reality into two: that which conforms to its criterion and that which does not. (Bhaskar, 2008)
 Here, at last, is the mechanism of universalization stated in its most general form. A mode founded on the epistemic fallacy cannot recognize another world as simply another way of being; it can recognize it only as a candidate for its own criterion of the knowable — and a failing one. What conforms to the mode's way of knowing is admitted as real; what does not is consigned to the unreal, the merely subjective, the superstitious, the not-yet-known. The mode does not, then, meet other worlds as peers and defeat them; it is constitutionally unable to see them as worlds at all. It sees only one reality — its own — and, scattered around the edges of it, error. This is why universalization is not an aggressive policy the mode might choose to drop, but a disposition built into its way of knowing: to know, for this mode, is already to totalize and to exclude.
 This is why the diagnosis that follows — the anthropic fallacy and its variant enlightenment-centrism — is best understood as the ontic fallacy run in one particular key. To universalize is, at the level of metaphysics, to commit the ontic fallacy with a specific content: to take this mode's made knowing and hypostatize it as the structure of being as such, so that every other world's way of knowing appears not as another fallible purchase on the same universe but as so much error scattered around the one reality. The mode does not merely reduce being to the knowable (epistemic); it crowns its own knowable as being (ontic). That is the precise mechanism by which a local way of knowing forgets its own locality.
 Bhaskar himself extends the diagnosis one step in the direction this thesis needs. Alongside the epistemic fallacy he names an anthropic fallacy — the analysis of being in terms of human being, the silent assumption that to be is to be as it is for us. The anthropic fallacy folds together an anthropomorphism (reading the world in the image of the human) and an anthropocentrism (placing the human at the center of what is real), and it admits of regional variants: any geo-historical variant of anthropocentrism — Eurocentrism among them — that takes one human community's way of being as the measure of being as such, and one that reaches an extreme form in modernity. I take up this opening and give the relevant variant its own name. The disposition this chapter is tracking is an enlightenment-morphism and an enlightenment-centrism: the reading of all reality in the image of the Enlightenment mode's way of knowing, and the placing of that mode at the center of the real. Universalization, named at the level of the mode's metaphysics, just is enlightenment-centrism — the anthropic fallacy run in the particular key of one mode of artificiality.
 And here Bhaskar supplies a figure whose name is not accidental to this thesis. He locates the twin fallacies on what he calls the Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line, and argues that thought trapped there is subjected to a "primal squeeze" (Bhaskar, 2016).[^primal-squeeze] Stated plainly: thought that lacks any sense of real depth is forced to choose between two ways of closing the gap between knowledge and reality — remaking the world in the image of the mind (rationalism) or dissolving it into sense (empiricism) — and between these two the independent real, the natural necessity of a mind-independent universe, is squeezed out. I take the resonance with this thesis's central figure to be more than verbal, and it is worth being exact about how. Bhaskar's primal squeeze is a squeeze of thought: it operates at the level of the relation between knowledge and being, and what it crushes is the real's independence. This thesis's squeeze is a squeeze of the human: it operates at the level of existence, and what it crushes is the human pressed between two givens. But the link is constitutive, not coincidental — and the ontic fallacy is what links them. The very operation Bhaskar diagnoses — the hypostatizing of made knowledge until it usurps the place of the given and the genuine given is squeezed out — is, run not in a seminar but across a civilization, the operation by which an artificial order installs itself as a second given and presses the human between it and the universe. The primal squeeze is the epistemological ancestor of the squeeze this thesis is finally about: the same disposition, diagnosed first in the theory of knowledge, then traced into existence. I reserve the bare word "squeeze" for the existential figure; Bhaskar's is always the attributed "primal squeeze."
 A self-correction must be made at once, because the argument is poised over a trap. If the epistemic fallacy were simply a flaw in human reason as such, then every world would commit it, and the chapter's thesis — that this is the disposition of one mode among others — would collapse into a truism about the human mind. That is not the claim. The Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line is an inheritance of one specific intellectual lineage, the Western one; it is not constitutive of human reason in general. There is nothing in Māori or Amazonian or classical Chinese thought that runs the epistemic fallacy in the form Bhaskar diagnoses; those are different settlements of the relation between knowing and being, with their own structures and their own characteristic errors, but not this one. The anthropic in enlightenment-centrism is, therefore, not humanity's man but the Enlightenment's man — a particular, historically formed subject mistaking its particular way of knowing for the shape of being itself. The diagnosis is local even though its object is a mode that claims to be universal; indeed, that is exactly the pathology — a local way of knowing that has forgotten its own locality.
 Two guardrails keep this diagnosis from sliding into positions the thesis rejects. The first concerns the nature of the error. To say the Enlightenment mode commits the epistemic fallacy is to say it makes a projection — it mistakes its own made world for the given universe, taking the structure of its knowing for the structure of being. But a projection presupposes something projected upon; the criticism is therefore realist to its core. There is a universe, a given substrate with its own determinate character, about which the mode is mistaken — and the mode's error is precisely to have lost sight of it beneath the image of its own knowing. The charge is not that the mode believes in a real world; it is that it has confused which world is the real one. The second guardrail concerns the alternatives. When I speak of other ways of knowing surviving in other worlds, I do not mean a flat relativism in which every world's account is as good as every other's, and the very notion of a mind-independent universe dissolves. I mean other fallible ways of knowing the same one universe — partial, corrigible, sometimes wrong, but answerable, as the Enlightenment mode's way is also answerable, to a reality none of them invented. The plurality this chapter defends is a plurality of fallible perspectives on one real world, not a plurality of incommensurable realities; and it is just this realism that lets the chapter say that the mode is mistaken, rather than merely different.
 Why rest the diagnosis on Bhaskar rather than on the more famous critics of Enlightenment reason? Because three of those critics each name a real face of the same thing, yet each, for the thesis's purposes, stops short. Adorno and Horkheimer, whose verdict that Enlightenment is totalitarian opened this chapter's third movement, capture the pathos and the self-undoing of the mode — the way its drive to master nature recoils into the domination of human beings — better than anyone; but their account is ontology-shy, diagnosing a dialectic of domination without ever quite saying what, in the structure of reality, the mode gets wrong. Derrida names the formal shape of the splitting move — the logocentrism, the metaphysics of presence, the privileging of one term of every pair over its excluded other — with unmatched precision; but his analysis dissolves the referent, leaving no positively real excluded middle, nothing on the far side of the split but more text, where the thesis needs there to be a real universe wrongly excluded. Habermas names the social form of the encroachment — the colonization of the lifeworld by the imperatives of system, money, and administrative power; but his diagnosis is social rather than ontological, and the resistance he mounts to it stays, for the thesis's purposes, too local, a defense of one (modern, Western) lifeworld rather than of the plurality of worlds as such. Each of the three is right about something; none gives what Bhaskar gives — a name for the positively real excluded middle, a genealogy of the splitting move, a structural mechanism for it, and an account that integrates the epistemic, the ontological, and the stance toward nature in one diagnosis. That is why Bhaskar is the spine and the others are witnesses.
 And there are further witnesses, working far from critical realism, who describe the same projection from their own vantages and so corroborate it. Edmund Husserl, tracing the crisis of the European sciences to Galileo, charged modern science with a substitution — it substitutes the mathematically substructed world of idealities for the only real world, the actually experienced life-world — and named the result a garb of ideas (Ideenkleid) thrown over lived experience until the garb is mistaken for the world (Husserl, 1970: 48–51). That is the epistemic fallacy described phenomenologically. Heidegger named the same move twice over — as the mathematical projection of nature, the prior casting of the world as measurable that lets modern physics find only the measurable in it, and as the age of the world picture, in which the world is set up as a picture before a representing subject (Heidegger, 1977). And John Dewey, from American pragmatism, traced the whole disposition to a quest for certainty and the spectator theory of knowledge it underwrites — the picture of the knower as a detached eye before a finished, separate reality (Dewey, 1929). Phenomenology, fundamental ontology, and pragmatism converge, from three directions, on the structure Bhaskar names: a way of knowing that takes itself for the measure of being.
 It remains to say what the splitting does in the world, for the exclusion the epistemic fallacy performs in thought is enacted, historically, as a politics. Walter Mignolo has named the apparatus: a colonial matrix of power whose rhetoric of modernity — progress, development, science, salvation — is inseparable from a logic of coloniality that subordinates and erases the knowledges it meets (Mignolo, 2011). Boaventura de Sousa Santos has given the erasure its sharpest name, epistemicide: the killing-off of other knowledges across what he calls an abyssal line, the line modern reason draws between the knowledges that count as knowledge and those cast into the zone of the non-existent (de Sousa Santos, 2007). And Yuk Hui's monotechnology (Hui, 2024: 225–226), met in the third movement, is the same erasure seen from the side of the technical: the spread of one technical-cosmological program achieved through the disappearance of other cosmologies. The epistemic fallacy, the ontic fallacy, the anthropic fallacy, enlightenment-centrism, the colonial matrix, epistemicide, monotechnology — these are not so many different complaints but one structure described at several depths, from the most abstract diagnosis of how the mode knows to the most concrete record of what its knowing has destroyed. That structure is the nonmaterial condition of universalization; and with the material condition of the previous movement, it completes the account of how an interventionist world became a world that could not see any other world as real.
## G. Universalization is not fitness
 An objection now presses, and it is the most natural one the chapter faces. If the Enlightenment mode has spread across the whole planet, overwriting its rivals until little stands outside it, is that not the plainest possible proof that it is the fittest — that whatever its philosophical sins, it has simply outcompeted every other way of making a world, as the fitter organism outcompetes the less fit? The objection has the force of the obvious, and meeting it is the work of this movement. The reply turns on a distinction the objection runs together: between fitness to a world and fitness to the universe.
 On the easier reading — that to be fittest is to prevail within the world the mode itself has made — the claim is a circle wearing the costume of an argument: the Enlightenment mode sets the very standard of prevailing (productivity, growth, measurable control), so to say it best achieves those is to say nothing. Alfred Crosby, the historian of ecological imperialism, supplies the principle that exposes the circle. A "weed," he observes, "is not a scientific word"; it "refers not to plants of any specific species… but to whatever plants spring up where humans do not want them" (Crosby, 1986: ch. 7) — superiority is meaningless except relative to a particular environment and a particular standard of fit. Change the standard and the ranking changes with it. And the change is not hypothetical. Flip the standard from expansion to biodiversity-facilitation, and a different mode of world-making rises to the top. There is now substantial evidence that the long Indigenous management of Amazonia did not deplete its forests but helped compose them: domesticated species are, on one large study, "five times more likely than nondomesticated species to be hyperdominant," their abundance rising on and around ancient settlement (Levis et al., 2017), while the anthropogenic dark earths (terra preta) record a deliberate enrichment of soil itself (Clement et al., 2015). On a ledger that counted the fostering of biodiversity as success, an Amazonian mode of world-making would rank among the fittest, and the European mode — which Crosby shows succeeding precisely by simplifying and replacing the biota it met — among the least.
