The mode is fittest by its own lights; but its own lights are not the universe's, and there is no neutral scale on which its kind of success counts as success as such. On the scale-dependence of "fittest," the circular objection collapses into a tautology.
 The harder reading is the one worth answering: granting that the internal standard proves nothing, is the mode not fitter to the universe — by the one standard not of any world's making, endurance under real constraint? Asked in the universe's own coin, the verdict reverses. A mode that has universalized has, by that fact, converted a plural biosphere of world-making strategies into a monoculture of one — and monoculture, by everything the science of resilience has learned, is the fragile form, not the fit one. The simplest model makes the danger vivid. In a classical predator–prey ecology, let the sheep gain solidarity and drive off every wolf: released from predation, the flock grazes the grass faster than it can regenerate, the pasture fails, and the sheep that had mastered their environment starve in it. Dominance over an environment is not fitness but the manufacture of fragility — and the resilience literature names why at each turn, each result with its own visible case. C. S. Holling separated engineering stability (fast return to one equilibrium) from resilience proper (the capacity to persist and reorganize through large disturbance), and showed that a system optimized for the first is brittle to the second (Gunderson and Holling, 2002); his own forests make it concrete — a stand held in a single tidy state by fire suppression looks stable for decades while it quietly accumulates the fuel, or the spruce-budworm pressure, that one disturbance then releases catastrophically — the flock tuned to grow is the flock that cannot survive the shock. Brian Walker and David Salt draw the management lesson: a system cannot maximize persistence, adaptability, and transformability at once (Walker and Salt, 2006); an over-optimized rangeland, stocked and grazed for maximum present yield, is precisely the one that fails when the drought it was never built to absorb arrives — the sheep bought present performance with future adaptive capacity. Nassim Taleb sharpens it: suppressing volatility does not remove risk but stores it, converting many survivable shocks into one unsurvivable one (Taleb, 2012); the decades of damped volatility before 2008 did not abolish financial risk but concentrated it into a single systemic break, just as a levee that spares a valley every small flood is building toward the one flood it cannot hold — no wolves, no small corrections, until the one large correction is starvation. And Tainter's diminishing returns on complexity complete the picture (Tainter, 1988): like the late Roman Empire or the Classic Maya, each buying ever-costlier layers of administration and infrastructure until the marginal return went negative, the universalized mode keeps buying control at a rising marginal cost until the cost of holding the monoculture together exceeds what it can pay.
 Put together, these results invert the objection exactly. The plurality of worlds is not decorative variety that a fitter mode can dispense with; it is, at the scale of the species, precisely the redundancy and the reserve of alternative strategies on which resilience depends — the biosphere's stock of other ways to be, held against the day when the prevailing way fails. A mode that universalizes does not climb to the top of the universe's ledger; it dismantles the very thing the universe rewards. It mistakes its dominion over a world for fitness to the universe, when in fact, by liquidating the plurality that was the species' resilience, it has made the whole more fragile than any of the worlds it replaced. Universalization is not fitness. It is the manufacture of a single point of failure on a planetary scale.
 The fragility has a particular source, and naming it precisely matters, because it is easy to mistake the argument for a moral one it is not making. The mode appears to succeed only by continually externalizing its costs — discharging them onto a colony, a future, an atmosphere, a buffer somewhere outside the ledger it keeps for itself. I do not raise this as a moral indictment. Evolution is impartial, and the universe keeps no fairness-ledger; the charge against externalization is not that it is unjust but that it is structurally self-undermining, because the buffers it draws on are finite and it has no plan for their exhaustion. Clive Ponting, surveying the environmental history of civilizations, found the recurring pattern: "a succession of ever more complex and environmentally damaging ways of meeting the same basic human needs," each escalation buying time by reaching for a larger buffer than the last (Ponting, 1991). But the buffers deplete. The ghost acreage of the New World was spent; the deep carbon that replaced it is being spent now; and the manufactured dependence by which the mode pins other regions as supply-zones — the engine Miura traced (Miura, 2004: 15–16) — is a relation, contingent and reversible, not an intrinsic superiority that could be relied on to last. A mode that endures only by finding an ever-larger outside to consume is fit only so long as the outside holds out, which, on a finite planet, is not a property the universe will reward indefinitely.
 The deepest form of the cost is what universalization does at the level of a world's enduring organizing principles, and here the monoculture is not only agricultural but ontological. The agricultural case names the mechanism plainly: when the United States planted some eighty-five per cent of its corn on a single cytoplasmic source, a single blight in 1970 took roughly a sixth of the crop — uniformity is vulnerability (National Research Council, 1972); the Irish dependence on one clonal potato a century earlier had taught the same lesson at the cost of a famine. What holds for a crop holds for a world. To overwrite another world is not equally costly at every depth, and the cost scales with the layer overwritten. At the surface — particular tools and recipes — transfer is nearly free; worlds have always borrowed there. One layer down, at structures and dynamics, transfer means a functional re-coding, exacting a real but bounded price. But at the deepest layer — the enduring organizing principle, the Dao (道), of a world, its basic settlement of what there is and how a life is to be lived — there is no re-fitting, only replacement. This is what recasts colonization in the terms of this thesis: not, at its deepest, the seizure of land or labour, but the replacement of one world's ontology by another — the abolition, at the Dao-layer, of an entire alternative way of organizing a world at all. The Enlightenment mode's universalization reaches past the phenomena and the structures to the principles, and there it does not exchange but erase; and each such erasure is one more strain of the planetary monoculture, one more alternative way-of-being subtracted from the species' reserve.
 And this coheres exactly with the lesson of the second movement, closing the chapter's circle. There I argued that collapse is a mismatch, not a verdict of unfitness — that the fall of a world proves nothing about its worth, only that its arrangements met conditions they could not sustain. The fitness argument is the same coin's other face. Just as the collapse of the Ming proved it unfit only to those who mistake a mismatch for a judgment, so the reach of the Enlightenment mode proves it fit only to those who mistake dominion over a world for endurance in the universe. Dominance is not fitness, and collapse is not unfitness; both are phenomena of the squeeze between a world and the universe, and neither is the universe's verdict on the worth of a world. The mode's universalization, read in the universe's own coin of endurance, is not the sign of its fitness but the mechanism of a coming fragility — which is the threshold the chapter must now hand to the chapters ahead.
## H. From the mastery of worlds to the mastery of life
 The argument of this chapter can now be gathered, and gathered around the right centre. I began by establishing that human worlds are genuinely plural, plural down to the ontologies on which they rest, and that beneath every one of them runs the synchronic squeeze between a made world and the given universe it never escapes. I read collapse as that squeeze in its extreme — a mismatch, not a verdict of unfitness — and found in its survivors the regeneration base, the plurality of worlds itself. I then named the mode whose disposition is this chapter's subject: the Enlightenment mode, identified not by a date and not, first of all, by domination, but by the elevation of the sovereign subject — the dis-embedded self that takes the given to owe it answer, and to which mastery (controllability), stewardship, and withdrawal are three exercises of one entitlement. I traced its conditions of emergence, material and nonmaterial, and showed why its universalization, far from proving it the fittest, manufactures a planetary monoculture and a single point of failure.
 One movement remains, and it carries the chapter into the next. A mode founded on the sovereignty of the subject does not, in the end, confine the subject's entitlement to the world outside the human. The same self that takes the given world to owe it answer turns, eventually, upon the one stretch of the given that is nearest and least tractable — its own life. For the human life is not lived as intended: the body sickens and ages against one's intention, others resist being made over to one's design, the given in oneself declines to be merely disposable. Uegaki gives this recalcitrance its name in the negative — the reality that no life is lived entirely as intended (意のままにならない生, i no mama ni naranai sei), the anti-intended in the self as in the world — and the sovereign subject, having taken its life to be its own to dispose of, cannot meet that recalcitrance as a condition to be lived with. It meets it as a hindrance to be overcome, or, failing that, as a void to be borne. The pursuit of a life lived wholly as intended (意のままになる生, i no mama ni naru sei) is the form the sovereign subject's entitlement takes when it turns upon the human itself.
 That turn is the subject of Chapter IV. There I take up what I will call the self-finishing tendency of human life: the drive, native to the Enlightenment mode but rooted in something older in the human, to complete and perfect the given self by remaking it — to close the gap between the life one has and the life one intends until nothing recalcitrant remains. The mastery of worlds and the mastery of life are, I will argue, one disposition working at two scales; and it is the inward turn, more than the outward, that drives toward the terminal form of the squeeze this thesis is finally about. For when the mode turns its demand for controllability upon a human life that the universe has built not to be wholly controllable, it sets the made and the given against each other at the very site of the self — and prepares the ground on which a self-completing, metaverse-stage artificiality will, in the chapters that follow, press the human into the final squeeze between the universe and the metaverse.
 
# IV. The Pursuit of Life-as-Intended and the Self-Finishing Tendency of Human Life
 Chapter III ended with the Enlightenment mode turning its demand for control inward, upon the one stretch of the given that is nearest and least tractable of all: the human's own life. This chapter follows that inward turn to its destination. Its subject is what I call the self-finishing tendency of human life — the drive to close the gap between the life one has and the life one intends, until nothing recalcitrant remains — and its thesis is that this tendency is not an aberration of the Enlightenment mode but its immanent completion, the form the mode necessarily takes when it is brought to bear on the self. To show this, I read the same phenomenon through three thinkers who saw it from three distances, and I arrange them not as rival theories to be adjudicated but as three faithful renderings of one thing, each true to the moment from which it was written. Theodor Adorno and Max Horkheimer saw the tendency from its darkness, as domination; Jürgen Habermas stood at its hinge, naming both the darkness and what the dark view could not see; and the contemporary Japanese philosopher Uegaki Takahide (上柿崇英) sees it from its brightness, as relief and liberation. The chapter's final movement shows that the darkness and the brightness are two sides of one coin — two appearances of a single real mechanism — and that the coin, seen whole, is the squeeze this thesis is finally about.
## A. A view from its darkness: Adorno and Horkheimer
 The darkest reading of the tendency, and the one against which the others must be measured, is the one Adorno and Horkheimer gave in the Dialectic of Enlightenment. Their thesis can be put as a single claim about reason itself: that what drives the Enlightenment is, at bottom, self-preservation (Selbsterhaltung), and that reason in its dominant modern form is nothing other than the organ of self-preservation refined into method. Self-preservation, they write, is the constitutive principle of science, the soul of the table of categories (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 68); reason has become the organ of calculation, of planning, whose principles are those of self-preservation (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 65). They find the formula already perfected in Spinoza, for whom, as they quote, the endeavour of preserving oneself is the first and only basis of virtue — a maxim they call, pointedly, the true maxim of all Western civilization (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 22).
 From this principle Adorno and Horkheimer trace three movements. The first is that the rupture between the human and a disenchanted nature makes reason instrumental. Once nature is set over against the human as mere object, reason's only office is mastery: what human beings seek to learn from nature, in the line Chapter III already cited, is how to use it to dominate wholly both it and human beings. Nothing else counts (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 2). The cost is borne first by the human's own inner nature, which must be regimented to serve the work of domination — industrialism makes souls into things (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 21) — so that the history of civilization becomes, in their phrase, the history of the introversion of sacrifice, a history of renunciation in which the self masters itself by mutilating itself, as Odysseus has himself bound to the mast to hear the Sirens and survive (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 43).
 The second movement is that instrumental reason, having no principle but self-preservation, cannot stop at any boundary; it totalizes, and in totalizing it hardens into a second nature (zweite Natur) — a made order that confronts its makers with all the opacity and inevitability of the given one. Enlightenment, on this account, is totalitarian as only a system can be (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 18), and its totality is driven by fear: nothing is allowed to remain outside, since the mere idea of the outside is the real source of fear (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 11). Adorno develops the point under the name of natural history (Naturgeschichte): the made world, grown total, presents itself as a nature from which there is no exit, until there is nothing outside any more, precisely because it is total, and nature turns into an irresistible parable of imprisonment (Adorno, Negative Dialectics: 354–361). Enlightenment, having set out to dispel myth, reverts to mythology (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: xviii): the made world becomes a fate.
 The third movement is that this second nature turns against the self that built it. The same instrumental rationality that mastered outer nature, having mastered inner nature too, becomes a force of self-destruction — for a reason that knows only domination must in the end dominate the very self it was meant to preserve. Enlightenment, they write, proved to be a destructive natural force no longer distinguishable from self-destruction; the two principles combined in a murky fusion (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 71), until the mode has made the control of internal and external nature the absolute purpose of life (Horkheimer and Adorno, 2002: 24). The drive to preserve the self, carried to its limit, consumes the self. This is the darkness in its full depth: a reason that is nothing but self-preservation, a self-preservation that totalizes into a second nature, and a second nature that turns, at last, against the life it was built to secure.
 This reading has the force of its consistency, and that consistency is also its limitation. To make self-preservation the constitutive principle of reason as such, Adorno and Horkheimer must read the whole canon of Western thought as a single engine turning on that one axle: Hobbes's war of all against all, Locke's laboring proprietor, Bacon's knowledge is power, Spinoza's conatus, Descartes's nature reduced to extension — even, in the Excursus, Kant's categorical imperative — all become expressions of the one drive to secure the self by mastering what is not the self. The monism is the source of the book's tragic power; it is also its cost. For if reason is nothing but domination, then everything the Enlightenment also brought — the genuine extensions of freedom, the real abolitions of real subjections, the conviviality that some of its institutions made possible — must be either illusion or ruse, the cunning of domination wearing the mask of liberation. And that conclusion is too strong. The freedoms are not nothing; the person no longer owned by another, the body no longer broken by toil it cannot refuse, the mind no longer bound to inherited authority, are not merely dominated in a subtler key. A theory that cannot tell the difference between a real emancipation and a disguised domination has lost the resolution it needs to describe the present, in which both are unmistakably at work at once.
 The limitation is not a refutation. Adorno and Horkheimer saw something real — the dark face of the tendency is not invented, and the chapters ahead will insist on it as firmly as they qualify it. What is needed is not to discard the dark view but to find a vantage from which its truth can be kept while its monism is loosened: a vantage from which one can say that the tendency really does darken into domination, and also that it really does deliver liberations that are not illusions. That vantage is what the next two thinkers supply — Habermas by naming precisely the contradiction into which the dark view's totality drives it, and Uegaki by describing, from inside the present, the brightness the dark view was structurally unable to see.
## B. The hinge: Habermas
 Jürgen Habermas stands at the hinge of this chapter's argument, because he names precisely the difficulty that the dark view runs into and supplies the thread that leads from the darkness toward the brightness. His diagnosis is exact. By making instrumental reason the whole of reason, he observes, Adorno and Horkheimer expand it into a category of the world-historical process — they trace it back behind the capitalist beginnings of the modern age, into the very beginnings of hominization as such — until the contours of the concept of reason are in danger of becoming blurred (Habermas, 1984: 366), and reason can no longer be distinguished from the domination it is said to be. The trouble is sharpest as a matter of logic, and it is the same self-cancellation Chapter III noted in passing: a totalized critique of reason — one that declares reason as such to be nothing but domination — must itself be made in the name of something, by some exercise of reason, and so saws off the branch it sits on. Habermas names this the performative contradiction inherent in an ideology critique that outstrips itself (Habermas, 1987: 127), and he is careful to credit Adorno with having seen it: Adorno was quite aware of this performative contradiction inherent in totalized critique (Habermas, 1987: 119). The dark view is not refuted by the contradiction; it is shown to be, by its own admission, unable to ground the standpoint from which it speaks.
 Habermas's way out is to deny the premise that reason is one thing. Instrumental reason — the reason of self-preservation and mastery that Adorno and Horkheimer described — is, he argues, only one complex of rationality among others; alongside it stands a communicative rationality, a reason oriented not to the control of objects but to reaching understanding among subjects, whose telos is agreement rather than mastery. The failure of the dark view, on this account, is that it does not direct our thought to the path that is nearest at hand — the differentiated analysis that would distinguish reason's several logics rather than collapsing them into one (Habermas, 1984: 382). Once communicative rationality is admitted, the freedoms the Enlightenment delivered need no longer be dismissed as domination in disguise: they can be the real achievements of a reason that was never only instrumental. This is the thread that leads toward the brightness, and it is the reason the dark view's monism could be loosened without being discarded.
 But Habermas does not only supply the bright thread; he also continues the dark one, and it is this continuation that the brightness chapter will inherit. For communicative rationality, on his account, is under siege. The systems that instrumental reason builds — the market steered by money, the state steered by administrative power — grow in complexity until they detach from the lifeworld of shared understanding that gave rise to them and begin to colonize it. Habermas names this the colonization of the lifeworld (Kolonialisierung der Lebenswelt). The rationalization of the lifeworld, he writes, makes possible a heightening of systemic complexity so hypertrophied that it unleashes system imperatives that burst the capacity of the lifeworld they instrumentalize — and he calls this, with deliberate echo of Adorno and Horkheimer, the irresistible irony of the world-historical process of enlightenment (Habermas, 1987: 155). The result, in the present, is not the false consciousness the older critique diagnosed but the subtler fragmented consciousness Chapter III already drew on — the consciousness that blocks enlightenment by reification, in which alone the conditions for a colonization of the lifeworld are met (Habermas, 1987: 355).
 This dark thread is the one Uegaki picks up, and the connection is explicit rather than my imposition: Uegaki derives his own central concept — the autonomization (自立化) of the social apparatus — directly from Habermas's colonization of the lifeworld, naming the Theory of Communicative Action and the Kolonialisierung at the point where he does so (Uegaki, 2022a). The hinge, in other words, runs through Uegaki's own text. What Habermas describes as system colonizing lifeworld, Uegaki will take to its terminus — a society in which the apparatus has so fully absorbed the work of living that the lifeworld of mutual understanding has thinned almost to nothing. But — and this is what makes Uegaki the figure of the brightness rather than a second prophet of the dark — he re-reads the very uncoupling that Habermas diagnoses as pathology. The detachment of the apparatus from the lifeworld is, for the people who live by it, also and at the same time a liberation: it is what frees them from the burdens that the lifeworld of mutual dependence had always imposed. The same process is colonization seen from above and emancipation seen from within. Holding both of those readings together, without letting either cancel the other, is the work of the next movement.
 Before turning to Uegaki, one word is owed on why the thesis leads with him and not with the more familiar object-pole lineage that runs alongside his — Weber, Adorno and Horkheimer, and, in our own moment, Hartmut Rosa. Chapter III settled the substance of that choice: the root of the mode is the elevation of the sovereign subject, of which controllability is only the mastering face, the operative principle of one disposition the subject may claim or decline. Here it need only be restated in the vocabulary this chapter is building. The object-pole lineage diagnoses the modern predicament as a stance toward objects — disenchant them, master them, render them controllable (verfügbar); Uegaki diagnoses what the elevated subject takes itself to be, and therefore to demand. He calls his approach an anthropology of freedom (jiyū no ningengaku, 自由の人間学), a diagnosis of the subject's self-constitution rather than of its handling of the world. The two stand, once their axes are crossed, as species to genus. Rosa's axis runs from the controllable to the uncontrollable; Uegaki's from the as-intended to the anti-intended; and the as-intended is the wider category, since it annexes much that Rosa files as uncontrollable.[^iv-asintended-genus] Rosa's controllability is one engine of the as-intended life — the instrumental engine that renders the object available, the Aristotelian pole whose signature is Bhaskar's epistemic fallacy. Uegaki's as-intended life is the whole of which that engine is a part, carrying also the idealist, soteriological engine of the elevated subject — the Platonic pole, the endowed nature the object-pole tradition does not theorize because its gaze is fixed on the object. To lead with Rosa would be to mistake a part for the whole and to sound like one more critique of controllability; to lead with Uegaki is to keep the subject in view, and so to say what the controllability lineage cannot — that the squeeze is not first a fact about a world made too available, but about a subject that has taken its life-as-intended to be its birthright, and reads whatever in existence will not answer to its intention as an oppression to be overcome. Rosa names the symptom; Uegaki names the cause; and there is, I suspect, a reason the cause is the harder thing for the object-pole lineage to see — its thinkers are themselves heirs of the Enlightenment world, and the self-subsistent subject is the very air they breathe, the standpoint from which they see everything else and so the one thing they cannot easily set before the gaze as one settlement among others. The vantage that makes it visible tends to belong to one who stands at the seam: inside the mode enough to know it from within, heir to another world enough to feel its edge. That Uegaki writes from Japan, and that this thesis is written from Taiwan, is in this respect not incidental but a condition of the seeing — though no claim of pristine exteriority is intended, since the everyday locality of any contemporary Japanese or Taiwanese thinker is already, in Chapter III's sense, heavily overwritten by the Enlightenment world.
## C. A view toward its brightness: Uegaki
 The third thinker writes from inside the present that Adorno and Horkheimer could only foresee and Habermas could only begin to diagnose. Uegaki Takahide, in his study The Formation of the Self-Finishing Society (〈自己完結社会〉の成立, 2022), describes the same tendency the dark view described, but from its bright face — not as a cage closing but as a burden lifting. The difference is not that Uegaki is naïve about the cost; it is that he writes from a vantage from which the brightness is undeniable, and insists that a theory which cannot see it has missed something real. Before the analysis, the plain version of his claim should be stated, because it is simpler than its apparatus makes it sound: modern people increasingly live lives in which the things that used to be hard — securing a livelihood, being recognized, getting through the day's needs — are done for them by a vast apparatus, so that they experience as freedom what an earlier generation experienced as dependence; and this experience, Uegaki insists, is not an illusion. The freedoms are real. They are simply purchased at a price the bright face cannot see, and which it is the work of this chapter to name.
### (A) Three terms
 Three of Uegaki's terms carry the argument, and each must be set down with care, because each names something for which English supplies no ready word. The diagnosis is, throughout, a diagnosis of the West: Uegaki writes from Japan, but the worldview he anatomizes is the one the Enlightenment mode universalized, and it is that mode — not Japan — that is the referent of every term below. The first is the as-intended life (i no mama ni naru sei, 意のままになる生): a life that answers to one's intention. "Intention" must here be heard in the full reach of the Sinic intention (i, 意) — not the narrow voluntarist "will" but the whole mind-heart, the seat at once of cognition, of feeling, and of striving. This is the inclusive mind Kant gathered under Gemüt and divided into the three faculties of cognition, the feeling of pleasure and displeasure, and desire — and it is the directedness Brentano marked as the intentional character of every mental act, the reference toward an object common alike to thinking, feeling, and willing.[^iv-gemut] The as-intended life, then, is life as it answers to the whole intending mind — to what one knows, feels, and wants, taken together. Its opposite is the anti-intended (i no mama ni naranai, 意のままにならない): whatever does not answer to the intending mind, whatever resists. And the sharpest name Uegaki gives the ideal is the worldview-and-view-of-the-human (sekaikan = ningenkan, 世界観＝人間観)[^iv-worldview] of the dis-embedded life (mugen no sei, 〈無限の生〉) — where "dis-embedded" carries not the literal "limitless" but its sense: the project of bending the given without remainder to the shape the mind intends, the limitless actualization of an imagined ideal upon a reality from which the self has cut itself loose. Its true opposite is the embedded life (yūgen no sei, 有限の生): a life that affirms its embeddedness — its constitution by a body, by others, and by a given it did not author — rather than treating these as hindrances to be overcome.[^iv-rendering]
 The second term is the social apparatus (社会的装置). By this Uegaki means the whole artificial system to which one entrusts (委ねる) the work of living — the markets, bureaucracies, infrastructures, and now digital platforms that take over the tasks a person once had to perform within a web of mutual dependence. The decisive point, and the one that separates Uegaki's analysis from Adorno and Horkheimer's, is the phenomenology of the apparatus: it is felt not as domination but as relief. To entrust one's livelihood to an economy, one's safety to a state, one's needs to a service, is experienced as a liberation from the burdens that the old face-to-face dependencies imposed. Whatever the apparatus is doing structurally, what it feels like from inside is the lifting of a weight. Any reading that imports the vocabulary of domination at this point — that insists the relief is really subjection — has already refused the datum Uegaki is asking us to hold.
 The third term is self-finishing (自己完結化), and it names the destination. A life becomes self-finishing when it is closed into itself, made complete-in-itself, able to run its loop — to meet its needs, secure its recognition, fill its days — without essential reference to other people. The apparatus is what makes this closure possible: by absorbing the functions that once required others, it lets a life be lived, in principle, alone. The term carries a deliberate double meaning that the English self-finishing preserves: to finish is both to complete and to bring to an end, and the self that finishes itself in the first sense risks finishing itself off in the second. The completion is also a kind of self-undoing. But — and this is Uegaki's brightness, held to the last — the people living the self-finishing life are not dupes. Through the apparatus's absorption of the anti-intended, they really do hold the modern values: freedom, equality, autonomy, conviviality, held as users (〈ユーザー〉) of the apparatus that delivers them. Against Adorno and Horkheimer, these are not illusions. The life of a person today is, by these measures, really freer than the life of a person a century ago. That is the datum. The chapter's task is to understand what it costs.
### (B) The soteriological structure
 The modern values, Uegaki argues, are not held flatly, as bare political facts; they are grounded ontologically, given a metaphysical weight that turns them from arrangements into destinies. And the structure of that grounding, on his reading, is a secularized salvation-history — the Christian drama of fall and redemption transposed into a worldly key. Three terms carry the structure. There is ontological freedom (存在論的自由): the conviction that the human has a true nature owed to it, a way it is meant to be — what Uegaki calls the human as it ought to be (本来の人間) or one's true endowed nature (約束された本来性), and what the German tradition named the vocation of the human (Bestimmung des Menschen). This, Uegaki observes, derives from Renaissance Christianity — it is the imago Dei and the covenant, the promise of a redeemed humanity, secularized into a this-worldly entitlement. There is ontological oppression (存在論的抑圧): the condition of being held back from that true nature — the secular form of the Fall, appearing as Rousseau's chains, as Marx's estrangement, as Kant's self-incurred immaturity. And there is liberation (解放): the movement from oppression to freedom, the secular form of redemption. Adorno and Horkheimer themselves belong to this lineage; their critique is itself a redemption-story, which is part of why it could not see its own ground.
### (C) The slippage from a political into an ontological program
 The danger lies in a slippage from the political register to the ontological one, and the case of human rights shows it most clearly. At the political stage, ontological freedom overlaps with something this thesis wholly affirms: the right not to be subjected to unjust power, the claim of the enslaved, the colonized, the disenfranchised against those who hold them down. So far the ideal does real and legitimate work. But when the same ideal is read ontologically — as the promise of a true nature owed to the self — the right against unjust subjection slides into a right against the given as such: a claim not merely against the tyrant or the master but against any limit whatever, including the limits the universe imposes on every life. Uegaki marks the fork with two phrases: the reality-accompanying ideal (現実に寄り添う理想), which works within the given to better it, and the reality-negating ideal (現実を否定する理想), which sets itself against the given as such. The slide from the first to the second is, in the critical-realist vocabulary this thesis has used, an instance of what Bhaskar called the ontic fallacy — the compulsive determination of knowledge by being, the taking of a hypostatized ideal for a fact of the world (Bhaskar, 1993: 4). Here the hypostatized idea is the human as it ought to be (本来の人間), the anthropic ideal, taken as though it were a reality the given is failing to deliver — so that the given itself stands convicted of withholding what was owed.[^iv-cr-mugen-yugen]
### (D) The problem of others
 This soteriological structure runs into two limits that the rest of the analysis turns on, and they concern the two things most stubbornly anti-intended: other people, and one's own body. The first limit is the problem of others. Other people are, finally, the anti-intended as such — they do not exist, become, or act as one intends, and no project of self-realization can simply will them into compliance. The soteriologist's solution is an axiological universalization: the freedom claimed for oneself is extended, in principle, to all, so that each is to be emancipated into their own true nature. But this is, Uegaki argues, naïve, because it leaves the anti-intended unaddressed rather than resolved. It would be unjust, though, to call the solution a mere wish. The thinkers who pressed it did not stop at proclaiming universal freedom; they built, against great resistance, the institutions meant to make the freedom of each compatible with the freedom of all — the separation of powers, constitutional limits on the sovereign, the framework of human rights. These are the real and hard-won fruits of the tradition, and the thesis honours them as such; nothing in the ontological critique that follows is a verdict against the rule of law or the rights of persons. The point is rather that these solutions are themselves works of artificiality — political-institutional apparatus erected to discharge a burden the value-engine could not discharge by proclamation — and that, as artificiality, they belong to the very confluence the next section anatomizes, and to the recoil the later chapters trace. For the institutional answer does not dissolve the underlying question; it relocates it. If everyone is to be freed from the burden of the anti-intended, who carries the burden? The right not to bear the weight of others becomes, in the limit, part of what one is owed as an irreplaceable self (かけがえのないこの私) — the me-as-I-am (ari no mama no watashi, ありのままの私) who is owed acceptance exactly as she is, and, at the same stroke, the self-as-it-must-be (kō de nakereba naranai watashi, こうでなければならない私) who is owed the conditions of becoming what she ought to be (Uegaki, kyojinnokata.net 16-12). Between these two figures the other, precisely as anti-intended, comes to figure as a high-risk, high-burden being that oppresses the irreplaceable self (意のままにならない他者). Emancipation, pressed to the ontological register, turns the other from a fellow into an encumbrance.
### (E) The problem of the body
 The second limit is the problem of the body. Western thought has, since the Greeks, ranked soul above body, and the body is, after the other, the most intimate site of the anti-intended (意のままにならない身体) — it sickens, ages, tires, desires against one's intention, and finally dies. The soteriological logic, pressed to its limit, arrives at a figure Uegaki names the mind-as-being (思念体)[^iv-tulpa]: a self that has shed even the body, a pure intending mind freed at last from the recalcitrance of flesh. This is, in effect, the res cogitans of Descartes returned as an aspiration — the thinking thing without extension — and it wears, in its modern form, the look of a secular Gnosticism, salvation as escape from the body rather than redemption of it. (The identification of Uegaki's mind-as-being with the Cartesian res cogitans is mine, not his; and the rendering of 思念体 is provisional — it is the limit-form toward which the disembodiment of the as-intended life tends.) These two limits — the other reduced to an encumbrance, the body to a hindrance to be escaped — are not incidental difficulties. They are where the soteriological engine, left to itself, drives; and they are why, as the next section shows, it cannot run without the second engine, the instrumental one, to carry the burden it cannot otherwise discharge.
### (F) The confluence of the two engines
 Here is the spine of the chapter, the claim that everything before it has been preparing and everything after it will draw on. There are two engines in the Enlightenment mode's drive toward the as-intended life, and they are not rivals but partners — two expressions of a single root, which converge not by historical accident but by internal logic. The first is the engine just described: the soteriological value-engine, the drive that supplies the ought. It says that the human has a true nature owed to it, that the given withholds it, and that the given must therefore be remade until the promise is kept. Read in the critical-realist vocabulary, this is the Platonic pole, and it runs on the ontic fallacy — the hypostatized ideal taken for a reality the world is failing to deliver. The second is the engine Chapter III anatomized: the instrumental engine, the drive that supplies the can. It reduces the given to what can be measured, predicted, and worked, so that it may be brought under control. This is the Aristotelian pole, and it runs on the epistemic fallacy — being reduced to the knowable, the knowable to the calculable, the calculable to the controllable.
 That these two are one at the root can be shown directly. Both grow from the single posture Chapter III named as the root of the whole mode — the sovereignty of the subject: the human posited as standing prior to, and over against, a disenchanted given, what Uegaki called the spatio-temporal self-subsistence (時空間的自立性) of the subject. From that one posture, two demands follow. Because the self stands over the given, the given owes the self its true nature (the value-engine); and because the given is mere object before the self, it lies open to calculation and control (the instrumental engine). The ought and the can spring from the same separation. And this is why they must converge rather than merely coexist: the value-engine's promise cannot be kept without the instrumental engine's apparatus. To deliver the true nature owed to every self — to free each person from the anti-intended other and the anti-intended body — requires that the burden of the anti-intended be borne by something. The soteriological engine, by itself, can only universalize the demand; it cannot discharge it. Only the instrumental engine, by building an apparatus that absorbs the anti-intended, can make the promise good. The value-engine writes the cheque; the instrumental engine is the only account that can cover it. Their convergence is not a contingent alliance but a structural necessity: the one cannot complete itself without the other.
 The two fallacies, and their kinship, were set out in Chapter III: the epistemic fallacy that reduces being to the knowable, the ontic fallacy that projects made knowledge back onto the world as its own furniture, and the "tacit complicity" by which the twinned fallacies "characteristically presuppose each other" (Bhaskar, 2009: 16), routing back through the same Platonic/Aristotelian fault-line. What this chapter adds is the pairing of each fallacy with an engine of the as-intended life. The value-engine is the ontic fallacy made practical: it hypostatizes the anthropic ideal and convicts the given of failing to deliver it. The instrumental engine is the epistemic fallacy made practical: it admits of the given only what its method can grasp, dissolving the rest. And the decisive consequence is that both engines, in their different ways, foreclose the same thing — the intransitive real, the universe that is simply there, neither owing the self its true nature nor reducible to the self's calculations. Where Chapter III diagnosed that foreclosure as a squeeze of thought, here it is a squeeze of the human: the value-engine convicting the given, the instrumental engine reducing it, and between them no standing-room for the given to be the given. That double foreclosure — the squeezing-out of the intransitive real by both engines at once — is what the chapter's final movement will show to be the squeeze itself.
### (G) The migration from the conventional to the structural
 How the apparatus discharges the burden of the anti-intended can be described precisely in the vocabulary Chapter II established. The apparatus does not dominate; it receives what one entrusts (委ねる) to it. And what is entrusted is offloaded along two axes. Horizontally, the apparatus takes over the functions once carried by the people around one — the roles, the trust, the forgiveness that a web of mutual dependence supplied; one no longer needs a neighbor's goodwill where a service will do. Vertically, it takes over the deeper supports of a life — the realization of survival (one's livelihood), one's existence-in-society (one's standing as a recognized person), and succession (the handing-on of life across generations). As these are offloaded, the values once held in a fabric of mutual dependence come to be held instead, in Uegaki's vivid image, as users hanging from (ぶら下がっている) the apparatus — freedom, equality, autonomy, and conviviality enjoyed as features and services the system renders rather than as relations sustained. And the directionality of this offloading is exactly the migration Chapter II's grid charts: from the conventional register, where the made holds because people keep enacting it, to the structural register, where it holds because it is built into the operating environment and runs whether or not anyone attends to it. The life of mutual dependence was conventional; the life hung from the apparatus is structural.
 This migration is not a thought-experiment; it has a legible history, and Japan furnishes an unusually clear case of it — not because Japan is the cause of the tendency, nor because the case is parochial, but because there the compression of the change into roughly four generations, from the Meiji era to the present, makes visible what elsewhere is spread too thin to see. (The plurality of worlds established in Chapter III is untouched by this: Japan is one legible instance of a migration the mode drives wherever it reaches, not the template of a single world.) Uegaki narrates the change as a sequence of five periods, and as a shift in the figure of the self — from the Traveller (旅人), who leaves a home port for a destination, to the Drifter (漂流人), who has lost port, destination, and compass alike. Table 4-1 sets out the arc.
Table 4-1. The migration from mutual dependence to the self-finishing society, after Uegaki (2022): five periods in the Japanese case.

| Period | Figure of the self | Dominant register (along the conventional-to-structural continuum) | Key markers |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| 1868–1945 | The rooted self (descriptive) | Conventional, at the level of enduring principle: the order holds only through the ongoing, face-to-face enactment of custom and obligation, and asks the member's continual participation in both deed and shared meaning. | Village (ムラ) and stem-household (家); mutual aid (結, 講); a population still largely agrarian. |
| 1945–1970 | The Traveller (tabibito, 旅人) | Conventional, but beginning to turn structural: the market and the bureaucracy still require human participation in act and concept, yet their financial flows and coded procedures increasingly escalate and run of themselves, propelled by protocol rather than by the participants' communicative agreement — still enacted, but no longer dependent on what any participant understands. | A home port at one's back and a destination ahead; liberation from mutual aid required the market economy and the bureaucratic apparatus. Collective-employment trains; the company as community; public-housing estates (団地). |
| 1970–1995 | The settled consumer (descriptive) | Further along the continuum: the conventional thins as consumption and the commute replace shared productive labour, and the structural margin widens. | The suburb and mass consumption; new towns and the nuclear family; local community substantively loses its meaning. |
| 1995–2010 | The Drifter (hyōryūjin, 漂流人) | The hinge to the structural: the infosphere takes hold as a background environment that compels by its own architecture, no longer transmitting the meanings or skills the conventional order once carried. | The infosphere (情報世界) as a structural environment; port, destination, and compass all lost. The employment ice age; recognition-anxiety; the mobile phone. |
| 2010–present | The self-finishing self (descriptive) | Structural dominance, at the level of enduring principle: the apparatus compels by its own architecture, running whether or not anyone enacts or comprehends it; survival, relation, and succession are no longer done through the apparatus but are what the apparatus is. | Deep dependence on the social apparatus — IT, robotics and AI, life-manipulation; the necessity of relating to flesh-and-blood others lost. Single-person households the most common type; solitary death; an ethic of non-intervention (不介入). |

 Read off the table, the arc is unmistakable: a life once held together by the enacted bonds of others is, period by period, rebuilt as a life hung from a structural apparatus that asks no enactment in return. And the migration runs not only across the grid's cells but down its strata — from the surface arrangements of a life toward its enduring organizing principles, until the very terms on which a life is lived have been re-laid. The seam to the next chapter is already visible here: an apparatus that holds by itself, without anyone's enactment, is an apparatus approaching the point at which it no longer needs its users at all.
 The same migration can be read in the present tense, by watching what becomes of the two great sites of the anti-intended once the apparatus has matured. The anti-intended other — the person who will not exist, become, or act as one intends — was met, in the conventional world, by the hard work of non-intervention, the etiquette of not making trouble. In the structural world of the platform it is met differently: by the recommendation that need never surface a stranger, the block, the mute, the unmatch, the algorithm that forecloses an unwanted encounter not as a thing one declines but as a thing that never appears as an option at all. The other is managed not by tolerance but by architecture. And the anti-intended body — the body that sickens, ages, and tires against one's intention — is met at the material cells by pharmacology, the wearable, the cosmetic procedure, and at the structural-virtual cell by the quantified self, the apparatus of measurement that promises prediction and control over the very flesh, and in promising it treats the body as one more anti-intended object to be brought to heel: a disembodiment pursued, paradoxically, through ever-closer attention to the body's data.
 The undisguised statement of where this tends is transhumanism, and it is best read here, as the premise of the whole mode made explicit and unashamed. The transhumanist takes the human, in Max More's words, to be just one point along an evolutionary pathway, a way-station with no claim on our allegiance, which we are free to reshape as we deem desirable (More, 2013); the present human is, for Nick Bostrom, only a half-baked beginning that we have the means and the right to finish (Bostrom, 2005). What this names openly is the as-intended life pressed to its limit: the body, the lifespan, the species itself recast as anti-intended material to be remade until nothing recalcitrant remains. The transhumanist program sorts, tellingly, by how much of the universe-calibrated organism it proposes to keep. Longevity research preserves the organism and merely extends it, working at the material cells. Enhancement is ambiguous, improving the organism while leaving it broadly intact. But the limit case is mind-uploading, and there the universe-pole is abolished outright. For Hans Moravec the person is a pattern, not a substance, so that the body is mere jelly to be discarded once the pattern is copied to a better substrate (Moravec, 1988: 117); for Ray Kurzweil the goal is frankly to transcend our biological bodies, retiring the human as version 1.0 in favor of a version 2.0 no longer made of flesh (Kurzweil, 2005). Here the self is to be moved wholly into the structural-virtual cell, and the given body — the last and most intimate anchor to the universe — is to be left behind.
 It is worth being exact about why mind-uploading is the limit case and not merely the extreme one. It does not deepen the squeeze; it completes the self-finishing by abolishing the very capacity to feel the squeeze at all. A self that has shed the universe-calibrated body has shed the site at which the anti-intended is registered — the aching, aging, mortal flesh that is the body's standing reminder that not everything answers to one's intending mind. Such a self no longer experiences the difficulty of the given because it has discarded the organ of that experience; it no longer knows that it is being squeezed, because it has dissolved the part of itself through which the squeeze was ever felt. Mind-uploading is therefore not the squeeze's culmination but its disappearance from view — the self-finishing tendency arriving at a self so complete-in-itself that the universe it has fled can no longer reach it to press. That this looks, from inside the ideal, like the final liberation, and is in fact the final severance, is the whole of the chapter's warning.
 The same offloading can be seen, finally, in what becomes of the three deep supports of a life that the apparatus absorbs vertically. The realization of survival — one's livelihood — is increasingly entrusted to the gig platform, which mediates work without the standing bonds of a workplace. One's existence-in-society — one's standing as a recognized person — is entrusted to the infrastructure of the online self, where recognition is rendered as metrics. And succession — the handing-on of life across generations — is entrusted to the technologies of reproduction and of digital inheritance, by which even continuity is offloaded onto the apparatus (Uegaki, 2022a: 138). All three, once carried in a fabric of mutual dependence, now run through the structural-virtual cell, toward the enduring organizing principles of a self-finishing society — which is the threshold the next chapter takes up under the name of self-completion.
### (H) The completion of the ideal
 It would be a misreading — the misreading the dark view invites — to take the self-finishing society as a pathology, a derangement of the Enlightenment ideal. It is the opposite: the self-finishing society is the immanent completion of that ideal, the form it was always tending toward. Uegaki's argument here is precise. A society that promises total individual freedom — each self owed its true nature, freed from the anti-intended other and the anti-intended body — and yet must somehow remain a working common life, has only one form available to it in which both demands can be met at once: the form in which the apparatus carries everything that the demand for freedom cannot be asked to carry. Self-finishing is that form. It is, in Weberian terms, instrumental rationality placed in the service of value rationality — the Aristotelian engine harnessed to the Platonic end — the apparatus built precisely so that the promise of the value-engine can be kept. Its utopian self-description is self-realization (自己実現): a life in which everything anti-intended is done for one, so that one is free to become, without hindrance, whatever one intends. This is not the betrayal of the Enlightenment promise; it is its fulfilment.
 Uegaki himself reads the fulfilment as a catastrophe — as a relational pathology (関係性の病理) and a disarray of life (生の混乱), in which the thinning of relationality and the disembodiment of life leave the self-finishing person isolated, unrecognized, and unwell. This normative reading is his, and I record it as his; the thesis does not rest its case on it. For one might dispute, as some do, whether the thinning of mutual dependence is a sickness or a maturation, whether the solitary, apparatus-supported life is impoverished or simply different — and that dispute, conducted in the register of well-being and relationship, is not the one this thesis means to win. The thesis's own verdict is not a moral one about flourishing but an ontological one about the squeeze, and it is reserved for the chapter's final movement. What the present section establishes is only the structural claim, which holds whether or not Uegaki's pathology-reading is accepted: that self-finishing is the immanent completion of the Enlightenment mode, the destination toward which the two engines, working together, drive.
 One last distinction marks the seam to the next chapter, and it must be drawn carefully so as not to claim here what belongs there. This chapter has established two things: that there are two engines — the soteriological and the instrumental — and that their convergence drives, in principle and in history, toward a self-finishing completion. It has not yet described the consummation of that completion, in which the engines themselves become autonomous: in which reason, the idealist engine, is externalized as artificial intelligence, and sense-data, the empiricist engine, is externalized as the vast accumulation of recorded fact, and the two are joined into an apparatus that runs the whole loop of as-intended life by itself, without any user to hang from it. That autonomized consummation — the social apparatus become a self-completing whole, a new and worldly Geist — is the subject of Chapter V, under the name of self-completion (自足完成化). The present chapter establishes the two engines and the necessity of their convergence; the next shows what happens when their convergence is itself entrusted to the apparatus, and the apparatus closes the loop on its own. The through-line from here to there is the two engines; what changes is who, or what, is running them.
## D. Two sides of one coin
 The chapter has now set two readings of the self-finishing tendency side by side: Adorno and Horkheimer's, for which it is domination, and Uegaki's, for which it is liberation. The natural impulse is to ask which is correct, and to award the palm to one. That impulse should be resisted, because the two are not competing theories of one object but faithful renderings of two different moments — and the apparatus for seeing this was supplied by the critical realism this thesis has used throughout. Adorno and Horkheimer wrote from the industrial mid-century, from within the catastrophe of the world wars and the culture industry, and what they saw — domination — was the actual face the tendency wore in that moment. Uegaki writes from the digital, globalized present, and what he sees — liberation, the user freed by the apparatus — is the actual face the tendency wears in this one. Each is true to its moment. The error would be to take either actual face for the whole, and the critical-realist name for that error is the conflation of the actual with the real: mistaking one manifestation, however genuine, for the underlying mechanism that produces it. Adorno and Horkheimer risk taking the actual domination of their moment for the real as such; a careless reader of Uegaki risks taking the actual liberation of ours for the real as such. Both would be reading a surface for a depth.
 Beneath both actual faces lies one real mechanism, and naming it is the work this whole chapter has been doing. The mechanism is the universalization of the sovereignty of the subject — the root Chapter III named — operating, on its mastering face, as universalizing controllability: the drive, made possible by the rupture that set the subject over a disenchanted given, to bring the whole of the given — world, other, body, self — under the subject's intending mind. (By the sovereignty of the subject I mean not the narrowly transcendental subject of one school but the broad posture Chapter III named — the self posited as prior to and over the given, the spatio-temporal self-subsistence that even the empiricist shares, since his materialism still erects the rupture. Adorno and Horkheimer's self-preserving instrumental ego, set over a disenchanted object-nature, is one form of it.) Domination and liberation are not two properties of this mechanism but its two actual-empirical manifestations — the same offloading of the anti-intended onto an autonomizing apparatus, read once from the side of what the apparatus does to the world (a cage) and once from the side of what it does for the user (a relief). The discipline of the given does not vanish in the bright reading; it is outsourced to the apparatus. The user experiences as freedom precisely what the system enacts as control — and there is no contradiction in this, because freedom-for-the-user and control-by-the-system are the same event described from its two faces. That is what it means to call them two sides of one coin.
 From this there follows a paradox that must be stated with great care, because it is the most easily misheard claim in the chapter, and the misreadings are worse than the silence they would replace. The paradox is this: a critique that sides with liberation against domination can itself carry the mode's premise unawares. This is not an attack from outside on the emancipatory project, and it does not essentialize or condemn that project; it is a question raised within it, about a premise it has not examined. The project of emancipation — the freeing of the enslaved, the colonized, the subordinated from unjust power — is one this thesis wholly endorses, and nothing in what follows retracts that endorsement. Uegaki is equally explicit on this point, affirming the historical role of the emancipatory tradition even as he questions it (Uegaki, 2022b: 80n). The question is not whether to emancipate, but where emancipation sets its terminus.
 The structure of the paradox is precise. The ideal of ontological freedom demands liberation from ontological oppression — and because the oppression is defined as whatever holds the self back from its true nature, the demand cannot, by its own logic, ever be satisfied. Each time one chain is struck off, a further chain comes into view, because the ideal, as Uegaki puts it, can never accept the human reality that no human exists who is bound by no chain at all. The pursuit therefore has no end; it is, in his arresting phrase, the no-respite hell (mugen jigoku, 無間地獄) of the dis-embedded life (Uegaki, 2022b: 118). And the endlessness is not a flaw in the fight against oppression — that fight, where the oppression is real and unjust, is right and finite. The endlessness is a property of an ideal that has quietly reset the terminus from the removal of unjust subjection to the removal of all hindrance whatever, the given itself included. It is at that reset, and only there, that the emancipatory will joins the as-intended-life engine: the will to be free of all hindrance is the will of the as-intended life, whatever the hindrance happens to be — a tyrant, or a body, or the fact of other people, or mortality.
 Here the precise hedge must be drawn, because everything turns on it, and it is Uegaki's own. The thesis does not question political freedom: the liberation from despotism, from unjust hierarchy, from subordination by power one did not choose. That liberation is real, finite, and right, and Uegaki affirms its institutional establishment without reservation (Uegaki, 2022b: 189, 193). What the thesis questions is the extension of the emancipatory demand from the political register into the ontological one — the slide from no person shall be subordinated by unjust power to the self shall be answerable to nothing given at all. The first is the reality-accompanying ideal, working within the given to right a wrong within it; the second is the reality-negating ideal, set against the given as such. The critique offered here is therefore immanent in the strict sense: it shares the emancipatory project's commitment to freedom entirely, and presses only on freedom's terminus, asking it not to slide from the wrong done by a master to the wrong of there being a given at all. And the chapter's own point, beyond Uegaki's, is the structural one this whole analysis has built toward: the will to be liberated from all hindrance simply is the as-intended-life engine, whatever its object — so that a critique can be impeccable in its opposition to domination and still, at its outer edge, carry the very premise of the mode it opposes.
 The alternative the critique points toward can be stated in the critical-realist vocabulary the thesis has used throughout, and it is the reality-accompanying ideal made precise. Such an ideal does not begin by setting what is — the painful, the cruel — against a hypostatized picture of what the human is owed; it begins by acknowledging the embeddedness of the self, and of the predicament itself, within a stratified reality that both affords and constrains. It asks first where and how the predicament arises — in which stratum, under what conditions — rather than convicting the given wholesale of failing to deliver an ideal. And it pursues emancipation from within that embeddedness: a struggle to better the predicament that stays attuned to the environment it works upon, rather than a refusal of the given as such. The difference is not between fighting oppression and accepting it; it is between a freedom sought within the stratified real and a freedom defined as release from reality's having any hold at all. The first can reach its destination; the second, as the no-respite hell shows, cannot.
 The metaverse is the point at which the coin is seen whole — at which the two faces, domination and liberation, are shown to be one. For the universalizing controllability that this chapter has traced inward, when it is given the apparatus of Chapter V, forms a real new background environment: a made order so complete that it stands as a second nature in Adorno and Horkheimer's sense and an autonomized social apparatus in Uegaki's, at one and the same time. It comes to lie parallel to the universe rather than nested within a world — a made environment that no longer reads as one settlement within the given but presents itself as the encompassing given itself. That is the threshold of the next chapter, which takes up the self-completion (自足完成化) by which the apparatus closes its own loop; the present chapter only brings the argument to its edge. And at that edge the dialectic recoils, exactly as both readings predicted from their opposite sides. The controllability that realizes the values is the same second nature that hollows them — the value-rationality swallowed by the instrumental rationality built to serve it, as Adorno warned. The entrustment that delivers the freedom is the same uncoupling that pathologizes the relation — the colonization Habermas named, the no-respite hell Uegaki named. Liberation and domination arrive together because they were always the same event.
 The thesis's own verdict, however, is neither Adorno and Horkheimer's domination nor Uegaki's pathology. It is ontological, and it is the squeeze. The argument is short, because everything has been laid for it. As long as there is a human at all, Uegaki concedes, there always remains an anti-intended (意のままにならない) — something in the self and the world that will not answer to the intending mind (Uegaki, 2022b: 120). That residue is not a deficiency to be engineered away; it is the intransitive, stratified real of Chapter I and Chapter III — the given universe, with its own determinate character, which no controllability subsumes because it was never the subject's to command. It is what Rosa, from the object-pole, calls the uncontrollable (Unverfügbarkeit). And the squeeze is its monstrous return. The very program of rendering the world controllable, Rosa observes, produces behind our backs a world that is utterly uncontrollable — so that modernity's drive to make the world available threatens to produce a new and radical form of uncontrollability, categorically different from the old and worse (Rosa, 2020). The given, pressed hard enough by the demand that it answer wholly to the intending mind, returns not as the mild recalcitrance it always was but as catastrophe: the climate that will not be managed, the body that breaks under optimization, the social fabric that tears where every relation was outsourced.
 That this return is structural, and not a contingent overreach that better engineering might avoid, is the last thing to establish, and Rosa supplies the principle. A world rendered fully controllable would be a dead world — for what is wholly at one's disposal, wholly predictable and available, has lost exactly the capacity to reach, surprise, and address us that makes it a world one can be alive to (Rosa, 2020: x). Total controllability is therefore self-defeating in principle: a liveable relation to the given requires that the given remain, in part, beyond command — only semi-controllable, never fully so. It follows that the Enlightenment mode, carrying its demand for universalizing controllability to the perfection the metaverse promises, must collide with the very given it declared itself independent of. The collision is not an accident of implementation; it is written into the demand. The made order, completed, sets itself against the given order it was built upon and can never escape — and the human, the creature of both, is caught between them. That is the squeeze: not the domination Adorno and Horkheimer feared, nor the disarray Uegaki diagnosed, but the structural collision of a made world perfected against a given universe that reasserts itself, with the human pressed in the closing gap. The chapters that follow take up that collision directly — first the self-completing apparatus that brings the made order to its perfection, and then the squeeze itself, read off the five working criteria this thesis has carried from the beginning.
# V. Western Modernity's Self-Completing Tendency and the Emergence of the Metaverse

## A. The handoff, and the gap

Chapter IV ended at a precise threshold. It showed that the sovereign subject, reading whatever will not answer to its intending mind as a wrong to be overcome, does not bear that anti-intended but offloads it — entrusts it to a built apparatus that takes the burden over. And it named the engine of the offloading: instrumental reason, the appropriative drive that renders the given calculable and so handles it. But instrumental reason explains only why the subject hands the work off. It does not explain how the apparatus, having received the work, comes to run it by itself — to keep, maintain, and renew the whole loop of a life without the human who first delegated it. That is the gap this chapter closes. Its claim, in one line, is that the offloaded function does not merely sit in the apparatus; it is taken up by a process that, at its far edge, closes into a circuit able to run without us — and that closing is what this thesis calls self-completion (自足完成化): the apparatus operating, maintaining, and renewing itself without continuous human direction.[^v0-naming]

A word on how the chapter reads its object, because the rest depends on it. This thesis has carried, since Chapter I, a way of setting out any domain — the universe, the world, the operating system a people builds within the world — as three layers: the enduring conditions or principles that organize things (道); the structures and dynamics those principles organize (理); and the context-specific phenomena, experienceable or observable to a subject, in which the structures are manifested (相).[^v0-strata] The metaverse, when it arrives, will have its own three layers, marked with the prefix 偽 — 偽道 / 偽理 / 偽相 — where 偽 carries its old Xunzi sense of the humanly-made (人為), not "fake."[^v0-wei] The argument of this chapter can be put as a claim about where, in those layers, the genuinely new thing sits — and the surprising answer is that almost nothing is new. The metaverse inherits its principles from the world; it adds only one. The whole can be laid out as a single map across the three domains:

Table 5-1. The three strata (道, 理, 相) across the universe, the world, and the metaverse.

| | 道 — enduring conditions/principles (the real) | 理 — structures and dynamics (the actual) | 相 — observable phenomena (the empirical) |
|---|---|---|---|
| 宇宙 · universe | physical law — thermodynamics, biology, ecology; finitude and mortality (constant) | the climate system, ecosystems, the metabolizing body (semi-stable — slow, bounded) | a heatwave, a pandemic, aging and death — the return of the uncontrollable |
| 世界 · world | the computational strand of artificiality — exosomatization, grammatization, concretization, tertiary retention (old; §V.C) | institutions and means — markets, law, language, kinship, the professions (migrating to 偽理) | lived enactments — a favor, a ruling, a conversation, a neighbor's trust (migrating to 偽相) |
| 偽境 · metaverse | 偽道 — opaque, mutating, self-rewriting conditions (the governing model), disposed toward self-completion, substrate-contingent (§V.E) | 偽理 — the four organs as one recursive loop: IoT, big data, AI, code-as-law (§V.G) | 偽相 — the agent off the loop: OpenClaw, MoltMatch, the agent-internet (§V.G) |

Read down, each domain falls into the same three layers; read across, the universe's and the world's rows are the background this chapter inherits (Chapters I–IV; the world's computational strand, §V.C), while the metaverse's — 偽道 / 偽理 / 偽相 — is what it builds. The one genuinely new thing is the recursive closure at the forged Dao (偽道, §V.E); the rest the metaverse took from the world and the universe and merely runs at its most advanced stage.

## B. Offloading is the putting of organs outside the body

To offload a function is, at bottom, to put an organ outside the body. The thesis takes this in the strong, literal sense Bernard Stiegler gave it, under the name exosomatization — the human's defining manner of evolving not by growing new organs within the body, across generations, but by making and wielding instruments outside it.[^v1-stiegler] Stiegler draws the term from Alfred Lotka, who, contrasting the human with every other species, observed that human adaptation has "followed an entirely new path": in place of slow anatomical change, "increased adaptation has been achieved by the incomparably more rapid development of 'artificial' aids to our native receptor-effector apparatus, in a process that might be termed exosomatic evolution."[^v1-lotka] The organs in question have a determinate shape, which Jakob von Uexküll had already set down: every animal meets its world through a receptor organ, which takes the world in, and an effector organ, which acts upon it, the two bound by a control apparatus into a single functional cycle (Uexküll, 2010: 46, 49). Exosomatization is the growing of that cycle — receptor, effector, and the correlating apparatus between them — outside the body, in the technical apparatus.

This is the form of all the offloading Chapter IV described, seen from the apparatus's side. When a population entrusts its livelihood, its recognition, its handling of others and of its own body to a built system, what it is doing, organologically, is exosomatizing its perceptual and effective organs into that system. The point to fix here is only that this is old and general — the human manner of living as such, not anything peculiar to the digital age. What follows traces one particular strand of it.

## C. The world's old strand: discretizing, consolidating, self-tightening

Among the many strands of exosomatization, one is the deep root of what will later make computation and digitization possible. It has three moves, and each is ancient.

The first is the breaking of the continuous flow of human life — speech, gesture, attention, habit — into discrete, recordable, repeatable traces. Stiegler calls this grammatization, and he is emphatic that it is not new: it "began at the end of the Upper Palaeolithic age, when humanity first learnt to discretize and reproduce, in traces of various kinds, the flux and flow running through it and that it generates" — cave paintings discretizing the mental image, writing discretizing speech, the production line discretizing gesture — so that digital tracking is merely "the most advanced stage" of a tendency tens of millennia deep.[^v2-gram] The second move is what becomes of those traces once they accumulate: they are a held, exosomatized past. Stiegler, after Husserl, calls an exosomatized memory of this kind a tertiary retention — the past lodged outside any living mind, in the archive, the recording, the trace — and grammatization is precisely the manufacture of tertiary retentions.[^v2-retention] The third move belongs to the made object itself: over time a technical object tends to tighten, its parts growing interdependent until it approaches the self-sufficiency of a natural thing. Gilbert Simondon calls this concretization: the evolved technical object "comes closer to the mode of existence of natural objects, tending toward internal coherence, toward a closure of the system of causes and effects," until it "becomes capable of doing without the artificial milieu" and is, at the limit, "its own condition."[^v2-concretization] Simondon insists this is only a tendency, never an attained natural status — a guard the thesis keeps.[^v2-guard]

Discretizing, consolidating into a held past, self-tightening: these are the strand's organizing principles, and in the stratified vocabulary of this thesis they sit at the 道 of the world — the enduring conditions of human artificiality, the world's own made-layer. They are not the metaverse's invention. The metaverse, when it comes, will run them at their most advanced stage, but it will not have authored a single one of them.

## D. The one new move: anticipation put outside, and the loop that closes

What the metaverse adds is one move, and it is the move that turns a system that stores into a system that acts. Stiegler's apparatus held an exosomatized past; what it lacked was an exosomatized future. Yuk Hui supplies the missing term: tertiary protention — anticipation itself put outside the body, the machine pre-computing what is to come.[^v3-protention] Such a system, Hui writes, exercises "a new form of determination," one that is "always preemptive, in the sense that the machine has already anticipated what the options will be" — a "reduction of the contingent to the most probable."[^v3-preempt] And with both the past and the future exosomatized, the circuit closes on itself: the apparatus, in Hui's exact formula, "constantly evaluates the past in order to anticipate the future, which in turn determines the present."[^v3-loop] That recursive closure — past read, future computed, present determined, the result fed back as new past — is the one genuinely new thing. Everything that feeds it (the discretizing, the held past, the self-tightening) the apparatus inherited from the world; the closing of the loop is what the metaverse-stage adds, and it sits at the forged Dao, the one principle proper to the metaverse. The closing of the loop is self-completion (自足完成化): the moment the apparatus no longer needs the hand that built it to keep running.[^v3-selfcompletion] What kind of ground that self-completion installs — and why it is a counterfeit of the universe's own — is the next section's concern.

## E. The mutating conditions and principles: the forged Dao (偽道)

The same loop, looked at again, tells a harder truth about what sits at the 偽道. Self-completion is a closure, yes — but the loop that closes is a loop that constantly re-evaluates. Hui's formula is worth hearing again with the stress moved: the apparatus "constantly evaluates the past in order to anticipate the future." A system that constantly evaluates is a system that constantly revises — its model of what to expect, and so the very rule by which it organizes, never settles.
