This grid is to be read in two ways. Read across the rows, it shows how the types differ, and the distinctness of the metaverse becomes legible as the bottom row rather than asserted in advance. But read alone it would mislead, for it makes the types look like a sequence of replacements, each cancelling the last. They do not replace; they accumulate. A later type contains the earlier ones, and the theory that explains an earlier type remains in force for every type after it: the evolutionary mismatch named at Type 1 still explains the human caught in the metaverse, and the over-reach of complex societies named at Type 2 still applies at Type 4. I therefore carry a second spine alongside the grid — a theory-accumulation table, set out in §VI.F where its point becomes vivid — which records, for each explanatory resource, the type at which it enters and the fact that it never exits. The grid analyzes difference; the table analyzes continuity; the squeeze is the figure both track.

What deepens across the four types is the stratum at which the two environments' principles diverge, and that deepening obeys a single rule: a divergence at any stratum entails a divergence at every stratum above it. If two environments agree at the enduring principles but part ways at the structures, then only the structures and the phenomena above it diverge, and the enduring principles remain a shared floor beneath — a common ground one can fall back to, calibrate against, regenerate from. This is the condition of the first three types: their squeezes cut at the 理 and above, while the universe's deep enduring principles stay shared, which is exactly why those squeezes, however severe, leave a floor and remain in principle recoverable. Type 4 is the first squeeze to cut at the enduring principles themselves — the universe's enduring principles against the metaverse's forged Dao — and by the rule, a divergence there propagates all the way up, so that enduring principles, structures, and phenomena all diverge, nothing is shared beneath, and no common floor remains. The single fact of which stratum the divergence reaches is what the bottom row of the grid records and what the rest of the chapter unfolds. The same deepening can be told as a change in how the made stands to the given: through the first three types the made is nested inside the one encompassing given, a world built within the universe; at Type 4 the made stands up beside the given as a second, parallel given. The "two givens" is the new fact at Type 4, and the squeeze, which through three types was the pressure between a given and a meaning-space, becomes for the first time the pressure between two givens.

Beneath the criteria lies a configuration of parties that differentiate and re-align as the squeeze evolves, and — fittingly — the cast grows as the types do. The parties, each fully stratified, are: the universe, the encompassing environment whose 道 is constant and whose 理 and 相 shift only locally and slowly; Homo sapiens, the biological organism whose real is its gene; the human, the world-having being, which is not simply sapiens; the world, the buffer the human lives within; the mode of artificiality, the artifice-making subset of the world's means, which I will call the strategy; and, only at Type 4, artificiality become a background environment in its own right — the metaverse. Four axes re-arrange this cast: the adaptational direction, which runs from the subject toward the environment in the first two types and then reverses; the strategy's standing, embedded within the world, then dis-embedding from it, then escaped into an environment of its own; two alignments — that the human runs roughly within its biological range, and that the strategy remains a means of the world — which hold in Type 2, break in Type 3, and shatter in Type 4; and the exit, which runs from defection through narrowing to foreclosure. Each type below states its place on the grid and then shows the cast re-arrange.

## B. Type 1: the organismic squeeze, and the birth of the world
The first and barest form of the squeeze is the organism's. A single given, the universe; the subject an organism; no artificiality; a ground that changes but stays stable; an exit that runs, over generations, through adaptation or extinction. Naming this form first does two kinds of work: it anchors the figure in phenomena no reader will dispute, and it locates the hinge from which everything human in the chapter descends.

The bare form is evolutionary mismatch. An organism does not merely inhabit its environment; it alters it, and the alteration need not run in the direction the organism's own adaptation requires. The Great Oxidation Event is the limit case I have used since the first chapter: cyanobacteria, respiring, saturated their world with a gas lethal to most of what then lived, including much of their own kind — an environment remade by its inhabitants into a pressure on those same inhabitants, with adaptation to the remade world in no way guaranteed. The structure recurs wherever a niche is built, from the beaver's pond to the long record of anthropogenic extinction, in which species are caught between a niche their human neighbors have altered past recognition and an artificial ecosystem their inherited equipment cannot meet. Here the universe enters only through its changeable part — the local structures and phenomena, the shifted climate, the invaded niche — while its deep 道 holds constant; and because a shared 道 remains beneath, the organismic squeeze is, at the scale of the lineage, recoverable: other niches remain, and adaptation, though it may cost the individual everything, can over generations refit the line. Adapt or go extinct — but the floor of physical law does not move.

Type 1 is not only the destructive precedent, however; it contains the hinge on which the human chapters turn. When the genus Homo emerged, it was an organism whose biology had been selected for one habitat and which increasingly lived in another it was itself building — a Type-1 squeeze, an evolutionary mismatch between an animal and a niche of its own making. The answer Homo found is the most consequential event in the whole typology: it did not wait for its biology to catch up, but built a buffer — the artificial food chain, the operating system, the world — and lived inside that. The world is born, in other words, as the resolution of a Type-1 squeeze; it is the layer Homo inserted between its slow biology and its fast-changing habitat so as not to be squeezed raw. Two consequences follow from that single origin, and the chapter needs both. The world that answers the organismic squeeze is also what opens the squeezes to come: once there is a world nested in the universe, there can be a squeeze between the world and the universe, and later between the world's own apparatus and the universe. And the same origin shows that a world is, at root, a device for not being squeezed — which is the seed of the question this chapter closes on: if a world was once built to buffer a biology against a new habitat, might a new world be built to buffer the human against the new habitat the metaverse has become?

In the cast, Type 1 has only two players, the organism and the universe, and only one axis is live: the adaptational direction runs from the organism toward the given. The reversal of that arrow lies three types away, but it is worth marking now that everything human in the chapter is the story of that arrow turning around.

## C. Type 2: the civilizational squeeze, in the embedded mode
The second form is the squeeze of civilizations. Still one given, but now the world is nested within it; the subject is the world-as-operating-system and, through it, its members; artificiality is present and run in the embedded mode; the ground's manifestations are dynamic but largely exogenous; and the exit is real, for alternatives remain viable and, crucially, a regeneration base survives a collapse. This is the squeeze of most cultures for most of history — and, with the restriction that the rest of the chapter turns on, it is the squeeze of non-Enlightenment worlds, the road the Enlightenment did not take.

What is squeezed here is not only a world but, through it, its members; it would be too simple to say the members merely suffer the world's troubles. They stand between the world's principles and the universe's, at the point where the world's enduring principles and structures meet the universe's shifting phenomena, so that when the climate turns or the granary fails it is bodies that starve — the universe's recalcitrance reaching the member through a world that could not absorb it. The world is the buffer; in Type 2 the buffer works but is finite, and where it fails the members pay. Two families of failure recur, and the collapse literature has named both. A world may be overtaken by a change in the universe's manifestations for which it has no answer — famine, flood, epidemic, the slow turn of climate — the register of Brook's account of dearth and dynastic crisis and of Morris's "two-way street" between geography and social development, in which the land shapes the society that then re-shapes the land (Morris, 2010; Brook, 2023). Or a world may grow so complex, so heavy with the very apparatus that once paid, that it crosses the point where added complexity stops returning and starts costing — the Malthusian ceiling, Tainter's negative marginal returns on complexity, Wright's "progress trap," the achievement that becomes the snare (Tainter, 1988; Wright, 2004).

The defining fact of Type 2 is not how it fails but the mode in which its artificiality is run, and here the chapter's debt to the fourth is repaid. The non-Enlightenment world runs its making in the embedded mode — the mode Uegaki calls finite life, 有限の生. In this mode the anti-intended — whatever will not answer to the intending mind: the body's decline, the other's resistance, the unbidden turn of events — is received as a constitutive feature of being a creature embedded among others, not as a wrong to be abolished. The view is not the exotic option but the inheritance of most cultures since antiquity, so that it is the other mode, the demand that reality answer to the self, that is the historical peculiarity to be explained (Uegaki, 2022b: 110–111). An embedded world does not, for that, refuse to ease hardship; it builds dikes, domesticates grain, codifies medicine. But it builds them as a creature works within a given it accepts, not as a subject discharging a debt it is owed, and so its making stays tethered to the universe it is made within. The strategy and the world stay aligned; the human stays within its biological range; the adaptational arrow still runs from subject toward universe. That tethering is exactly why Type 2 keeps a regeneration base: when an embedded world collapses, what survives — vernacular competence, embodied skill, subsistence knowledge — is still fitted to the universe, so a reduced world can re-form on the ruins (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006). The floor is lower, but it is a floor.

Two features of Type 2 must be held apart from anything Enlightenment-specific, for the chapter's later precision depends on it. The first is the exit. An embedded world's members, when its center pressed too hard, could defect — withdraw to the hills, the margin, the frontier, and re-form a smaller world on a thinner apparatus. This is Scott's "art of not being governed," the standing possibility of escape and non-incorporation that a universe-tethered world leaves open precisely because vernacular competence travels (Scott, 2009). The second is inequality. It is not the Enlightenment that first makes the fruits of artificiality fall unevenly; some have eaten while others starved within the very same world since making began, and this uneven distribution is an old, Type-2 feature, disposed toward unevenness wherever there is a strategy whose yield can be captured. Naming inequality here, and not at Type 3, guards a distinction the next section turns on: the Enlightenment's genuine novelty is not that it made things unequal — making was always unequal — but something else, which Type 3 will name. Holding inequality at Type 2 lets that real innovation show up uncontaminated.

## D. Type 3: the global squeeze, in the dis-embedding mode
The third form is the Anthropocene, and against the temptation to file it under Type 2 it is the Enlightenment's own signature — a categorically distinct mode, not merely a louder civilization. Still one given, but the world now undermines it; the squeeze still cuts at the structures and above, the enduring principles still shared, but the dynamism is no longer exogenous, for it is driven by the world's own disposition. The subject is the global Enlightenment world and its members; artificiality is run in the dis-embedding mode; the ground's manifestations move under industrial forcing; alternatives narrow and the base begins to hollow.

The naming follows the postcolonial usage. Type 3's subject is the global Enlightenment world — the world-system under globalization — and the Anthropocene is the global, the world pole, disrupting the planetary, the universe pole (Chakrabarty, 2021). So when I say the Type-3 world transgresses planetary boundaries, the phrase keeps its proper sense: the boundaries are the universe-pole limits, and what transgresses them is the global world (Rockström et al., 2009; Steffen et al., 2015). The world has grown to the scale at which its making destabilizes the very 相 of the universe it is nested in — the climate, the biosphere, the ocean's chemistry — so that the squeeze becomes, for the first time, self-undermining: the ground the world must track is moving because the world is moving it.

But the geophysics is the symptom; the mode is the cause, and here is the Enlightenment's genuine novelty, the one thing new at Type 3 and absent at Type 2 — the offloading of the anti-intended. In the embedded mode the anti-intended is borne as constitutive. The Enlightenment inverts this. Reading whatever will not answer to the intending mind as ontological oppression — a debt owed to the self and left in default — the sovereign subject does not bear the anti-intended but offloads it, entrusting it to a built apparatus to take over. This is dis-embedding: the made is no longer kept tethered, as a creature's work within an accepted given, but is driven to answer the human, to deliver the nature the subject feels owed. And here the chapter's deepest re-arrangement occurs, for the adaptational arrow reverses: through the first two types the subject fitted itself to the environment; now the subject makes the environment fit it, building artificiality to discharge what it refuses to bear.

It is essential not to read this offloading as merely a loss, for that is precisely what Uegaki saw and the critical tradition is least able to say. The same offloading that the analyst reads as a dispossession is, from within, lived as a liberation, and not a trivial one. To entrust the anti-intended to the apparatus is to be relieved of it, and to be relieved of the body's recalcitrance, the other's resistance, and the unbidden turn of events is to draw nearer to the as-intended life that the sovereign mode holds to be the very form a human existence ought to take (Uegaki, 2022b: 110–114). The goods are real — comfort, capability, the easing of what was once simply borne — and they are chosen daily by people who are not deceived. Ontological freedom is not a cold axiom but an intoxicating one: it tells the subject that it deserves to be free of the recalcitrant, that the body's decline and the other's resistance are not merely its lot but a wrong it is entitled to see redressed. The offloading thus has two faces at once, and the squeeze is the name not for the dark face alone but for the single structure of which liberation and dispossession are the two sides. §VI.E follows that liberation to its limit; the point here is only that the Type-3 mode is embraced, not imposed.

The reversal of the adaptational arrow breaks the two alignments that held through Type 2, and a single engine drives both breaks. The engine is the hypostatized real: the subject takes a made ideal of human nature — a piece of transitive, socially produced knowledge — and freezes it into an intransitive fact that the world is failing to deliver, so that every limit of the given is convicted as a withholding. Offloading discharges that owed nature onto artificiality; and artificiality, now built to answer the hypostatized human rather than to fit the universe, begins to adapt to the human faster than the slow umwelt can — which is the first break: the strategy comes loose from the world that housed it, and the means that were the world's own begin to lead it. The second break follows from the same speed turned on the human. Living more by its hypostatized real than its biological one, fitted to the fast apparatus and not to the slow given, the human drifts from sapiens. That drift has two names that are two readings of one fact: Obara's self-petification, 自己ペット化, the human become the lap-pet of its own systems, sealed in a capsule that sheds the very stressors against which its robustness developed, "biologically and socially more fragile and disordered than their merely domesticated counterparts" (Obara and Hani, 1995); and Stiegler's proletarianization, the loss of savoir as knowledge migrates from living capacity into the apparatus (Stiegler, 2016: 25).

The buffer, in Type 3, begins to fail — not yet hollowed, which is Type 4, but no longer reliably absorbing, because the strategy that was its working part has come loose and turned, in part, into a pressure. The costs become visible as the signs the introduction promised, two of which belong here, the third to Type 4. The first sign is the body's: the body is the universe's representative within the squeezed human, "the universe in us," governed by a 道 no platform can rewrite, so that when behavior bends to the apparatus — the sedentary day, the fractured attention, the broken sleep — the body registers the mismatch as the rising diseases of civilization and as the existential difficulty of reconciling "paleolithic emotions, medieval institutions, and godlike technology" (Wilson, 2012). The second sign is livelihood's. In a pre-modern world the materially poor retained, in principle, access to other sources of subsistence — firewood from the forest, water from the river, eggs from their own hens, the gifts of a stronger gift culture — whereas livelihood becomes singularized as its means grow mediated and managed through artificiality, so that to survive one must adapt to the totalizing apparatus and pass its criteria (Hussein and Nelson, 1998; Ellis, 2000). Illich names the structure: a radical monopoly, in which one industrial mode of meeting a need "excludes nonindustrial activities from competition," so that the alternative does not merely lose but ceases to be practically available, and the deepest harm is a modernized poverty — destitution not from a lack of resources but from a blockage by an artificiality whose criteria cannot be passed (Illich, 1975: 65, 82). At Type 3 these monopolies are real but still domain-bounded; their unification under one architecture is Type 4's escalation. And the acceleration that runs away at Type 4 begins its spiral here: as offloading hands more to the apparatus and the apparatus accelerates, the required pace of human adaptation rises, which deepens the offloading, which accelerates the apparatus again — the self-reinforcing loop Rosa describes as social acceleration without a brake (Rosa, 2013: 151).

Inequality, finally, intensifies here, but along the distinction §VI.C drew. The old uneven distribution of artificiality's fruits is now compounded by a new unevenness proper to offloading: when the anti-intended is discharged onto an apparatus, its recoil falls on whoever bears the displaced burden, so that some receive the liberation and others the recoil. The two are continuous but not identical — inequality of benefit is the Type-2 inheritance, the uneven recoil of the offloaded anti-intended the Type-3 addition — and the regeneration base, no longer intact, begins to hollow as vernacular competence proletarializes and the human de-fits from the universe, thinning in advance the floor an embedded collapse would have left.

## E. Type 4: the metaverse squeeze, in the organizing-inorganic mode
The fourth form is the one the whole thesis has been building toward, and it is different in kind. For the first time there are two parallel givens, the universe and the metaverse, diverging at the enduring principles themselves, so that by the rule of §VI.A the divergence is total; the subject now includes the individual as a single fold-point; artificiality is a dis-embedded second given; the organizing ground is self-revising, opaque, and substrate-contingent; and the exit is a radical monopoly over a base hollowed in advance. Every earlier form was a squeeze between a given and something nested inside it. This one is not.

The categorical break is the last section's reversal taken to its end. In Type 3 the strategy dis-embedded — came loose from the world and began to lead it. In Type 4 the strategy escapes the world altogether and stands up as a background environment in its own right: artificiality self-completes into the metaverse, a second given beside the universe (Chapter V). This is the final state of the two alignments — held in Type 2, broken in Type 3, here shattered: the strategy is no longer even a means of the world, because it has become an environment around it. The buffer's own working part has left the buffer and turned into the second pressure; the world that through three types stood between the human and the universe is hollowed, its means migrated out to become the very environment that now squeezes it.

What makes the two givens diverge at the enduring principles, and not merely at the structures, is the character of the ground the metaverse installs — the forged Dao. It is not a stable set of organizing principles but a self-revising, opaque, substrate-contingent one. It is self-revising because the loop that closes in self-completion is a loop that constantly re-evaluates, rewriting its own governing model behind the veil of a trained black box, so that the rule by which it organizes never settles. It is substrate-contingent because the whole apparatus, including the held past that looked most like a floor, hangs on a perishable base it does not acknowledge — the world's phenomena, the servers and the corporate decision and the labor and the energy, and the universe's phenomena, the temperature and the durability of the chips. The metaverse does not decouple from its substrate, it disavows a substrate it nonetheless depends on, and the gap between the dependence and the disavowal is where the squeeze bites. The forged Dao earns its prefix in both senses at once: it is humanly-made (人為) and it is counterfeit, posing as a self-grounding, constant ground in mimicry of the universe's enduring principles while possessing neither self-subsistence, since it hangs on its substrate, nor constancy, since it rewrites itself.

It is tempting to call this a new kind of being; the more exact claim is that it is an old kind pushed past a threshold. In the critical-realist vocabulary the forged Dao is existentially intransitive — real, causally powerful, indifferent to anyone's study of it — and yet, like any social structure, made, and dependent on human activity, on human concepts, and on a place and time (Bhaskar, 1998: 41, 51). What self-completion does is drive such a structure to shed, in principle and ever more in practice, the two dependencies that keep ordinary social structures within human reach: enactment-dependence, since a language dies when its speakers fall silent but the self-completing apparatus runs on when its users look away, and concept-dependence, since a banking system needs agents who grasp money but the trained model organizes with no agent holding the concept of how. What it does not shed is substrate-dependence — and that retained dependence, on a contingent base, is exactly where its fragility lives. The forged Dao is therefore not a new ontological category but a limit-case of a social structure: a made thing that has shed almost everything that made the made tractable, and yet governs.

That a ground can be real and yet mutate is no paradox once one drops the assumption that "real" means "constant." The universe's enduring principles are expected to hold still; but a species' enduring principle, its gene, mutates slowly, by selection, across generations, and a species co-evolves with that slow ground, fitted to it over the long descent. The forged Dao sits at the runaway end of the same spectrum, mutating not over generations but in a season, not by selection but by its own recursive hand, and behind a veil — and a human cannot co-evolve with a ground that rewrites itself faster than adaptation can follow. The runaway is sharpened by what Hui identified as this ground's characteristic operation: it does not merely change, it preemptively determines, for "the machine has already anticipated what the options will be," in "a reduction of the contingent to the most probable" (Hui, 2019: 211). The ground does not wait to be read; it forecloses in advance the contingency a reader would need. So Rosa's acceleration spiral, begun at Type 3, here intensifies past any brake: the forged Dao self-revises faster than adaptation, the human offloads more to keep pace, the apparatus revises faster still, and the artificial mismatch between the human and a ground that moves because one tries to close it never fills.

And yet to describe the metaverse only as a pressure is to miss what Uegaki saw most clearly and what this thesis must keep in view: that the offloading consummated here is, from within, a liberation, and the metaverse the very place that liberation has always been reaching toward. Follow the offloading to its limit. Suppose it were total — suppose the body, the universe's one representative within the human and the single party that cannot be re-coded, were not merely managed but shed: the meat brain replaced, the organism left behind, the human persisting as what Uegaki calls a 思念体 (shinen-tai), a mind-as-being, pure mind without flesh (Uegaki, 2022b: 112). Such a being would no longer be squeezed, for the squeeze requires two poles and the mind-as-being has let one go. It would not live between the universe and the metaverse but only in the metaverse, the universe relegated beneath it to a mere substrate it no longer experientially inhabits — the metaverse stacked upon the universe rather than set beside it, a first nature in the place of the lost world. Freed of the body's given, the mind-as-being could in principle become anything, anywhere, all at once; and death, the last and most obstinate of the anti-intended, would become at last a thing one elects rather than suffers. This is no dystopia smuggled in as a warning; by the lights of the sovereign mode it is self-realization, the consummation of ontological freedom, and its appeal is exactly the appeal of the offloading that began the whole sequence.

The escape is counterfeit, however, in the precise sense the prefix 偽 has carried throughout. For the mind-as-being does not transcend finitude; it changes its jailer. Mortality, shed as the universe's imposition, returns as the metaverse's — written into the forged Dao as a parameter, death no longer abolished but programmed, enforced now by an authority that is opaque, mutable, and beyond appeal where the universe's was at least constant and legible. To be freed from the body's death into the program's death is not to leave the squeeze's logic but to complete it: the sovereign subject, having fled the first given, finds that the second given has inherited the first's office over its life and wields it on terms the subject can neither read nor revise. This is the deepest reach of the forged Dao — that it can take over even the universe's power to set the term of a life — and it is why the liberation and the squeeze are, once more, two faces of one event rather than two verdicts to be weighed.

The radical monopoly Illich named at Type 3 is, at Type 4, made total, because the architecture is now one. Where earlier monopolies were domain-bounded — cars over transport, schools over learning, each leaving the others a vernacular refuge — the self-completing apparatus runs one logic across banking, identity, credentialing, civic presence, belonging, and market access at once, so that a vernacular alternative in any single domain falls under the logic that eliminated it in the others. The exit Scott's hill peoples kept — defect and re-form a smaller world — is foreclosed, for there is no longer a universe-fitted competence to defect with, nor an unmonopolized domain to defect to. And the third sign, the one specific to Type 4, is the condition of living under laws that are opaque, mutable, and beyond appeal: a subjection worse than the prehistoric pattern-finder's, whose universe was at least legible through long observation, because here even a correctly inferred and faithfully followed rule is voided the moment the forged Dao rewrites itself, without notice and without recourse. Platforms have become de facto societies whose operators are de facto governments, removing ordinary content without notice or remedy (Tobitt, 2024); the nearest illustration is the fiction of a world whose environing conditions are so chaotic that no stable, predictive science can take hold (Liu, 2014), and the platform user inhabits a real version of that chaotic era, the law that governed yesterday simply not the law today. A single observable cluster can stand for the moving rest: the open-source autonomous agents of late 2025 and 2026 that execute rather than suggest, and the "agent internet" that assembled beside them — a social network described, in its own words, as built "for agents, by agents," with humans "welcome to observe." These are to be read as forged phenomena, the surface signs of a self-revising ground, and, fittingly, by the time the words are read the instance may already be obsolete — which is not a failure of the example but the argument demonstrating itself. The same instance refuses any single verdict: when one government moved to restrict such agents on security and energy grounds while its local governments promoted them for development, the contradiction is not a confusion to be resolved but the balance of liberation and dispossession showing itself in one frame.

## F. The fold-point: why the types accumulate on one site
The four types are not a relay in which each runner leaves the track. They accumulate: Type 4 does not abolish the organismic mismatch, the civilizational over-reach, or the Anthropocene's forcing but contains them and runs all three at once. This is the conceptual face of the figure of prior squeezes folded onto a single site, and it is made vivid by the chapter's second spine, which records where each explanatory resource enters and registers that it never exits.

Table 6-2. The theory-accumulation spine: where each explanatory resource enters, and the types for which it remains in force.

| Explanatory resource | Enters at | In force for |
|---|---|---|
| Evolutionary mismatch; niche construction; the world as buffer | Type 1 | 1–4 |
| Collapse of complexity (Tainter); the geography–development "two-way street" (Morris); Malthusian limits; the progress trap (Wright); escape and non-incorporation (Scott) | Type 2 | 2–4 |
| The uneven distribution of artificiality's fruits (inequality is old) | Type 2 | 2–4 |
| The offloading of the anti-intended — the dis-embedding mode (the Enlightenment's genuine novelty) | Type 3 | 3–4 |
| Self-petification (Obara); proletarianization (Stiegler); the Anthropocene and planetary boundaries; the acceleration spiral (Rosa); the body's sign and the livelihood sign | Type 3 | 3–4 |
| Self-completion; the 偽道 and its incommensurability at the level of principle; the self-revising, opaque ground; preemptive determination (Hui); the sign of opaque and changing laws; the hollowed regeneration base | Type 4 | 4 |

The table explains the chapter's sharpest asymmetry: why the collective squeeze is "only" triadic while the individual squeeze is the most acute of all. Read the columns down to a single life and the accumulation lands on one body at once. The individual is a fold-point — a Pleistocene organism, a member of a world, a citizen of the global Anthropocene, and an inhabitant of the metaverse simultaneously — carrying a biology selected for a vanished habitat, a competence that is proletarializing, a planet whose manifestations are turning volatile, and a forged Dao that rewrites itself faster than adaptation can follow. And it lands there while the buffer drains out from under it, for the world that since Type 1 stood between the organism and the universe is, at Type 4, the very thing being hollowed. The individual feels the squeeze most because the individual is where all four types are summed and where the buffer that once divided them thins to nothing.

## G. Squeeze, collapse, and the disposition toward irreversibility
One distinction must be kept sharp or the chapter's prognosis will be misread: the squeeze is not the collapse. The squeeze is the ongoing structural condition of being jointly subject to incommensurable grounds, a condition that can be lived with, for finite intervals, under the recognition the final section describes. Collapse is the crossing of a threshold at which the squeeze becomes irreversible and dual satisfaction fails altogether — extinction for an organism, dissolution for a world. The two come from different literatures and carry different urgencies, and I turn to the collapse-endpoint only to specify its form, not to predict its arrival.

It is worth marking that the squeeze admits of more than one ending, and that not all of them are losses. One ending is to live with it, under recognition and reform. A second is collapse, the involuntary loss of both poles. But a third, examined in §VI.E, is the very consummation the sovereign mode desires — the migration into the metaverse as a first nature, the mind-as-being that dissolves the squeeze by letting the universe-pole go. The third is not a collapse but a fulfilment by the mode's own lights, and the thesis can adjudicate between fulfilment and loss no more than the incommensurability at its center allows: there is no common measure in which the goods of the migration and the goods of the embedded life are weighed against one another. What the thesis can say is structural, and it is this: the migration does not escape finitude but re-installs it under a counterfeit authority, and the collapse, were it reached, would be more severe than any before it — for the reason the rest of this section gives.

The thesis's distinctive claim at this stratum concerns the regeneration base. A Type-2 collapse, however severe, left a base: because the embedded world's competence stayed fitted to the universe, what survived — vernacular skill, embodied knowledge, subsistence practice — was still universe-fitted, and a reduced world could re-form on it (Schwartz and Nichols, 2006). The metaverse stage is different because the very mechanisms this chapter has traced — proletarianization, self-petification, the de-fitting of the human from the universe, the radical monopoly's elimination of vernacular alternatives — operate on that floor in advance of any collapse. The base is not destroyed by a collapse; it is hollowed before one. But the claim must be stated in its exact, modest form. It does not assert that a metaverse-stage collapse would be irreversible; it asserts that the metaverse stage is disposed toward irreversibility, hollowing in advance the regeneration phase a recovery would need. "Disposed toward" is the honest word: a disposition is efficacious before it is complete, real as a tendency without being a prophecy. The earlier squeezes cut at the 理 and left the 道 as a floor beneath; a squeeze that has migrated to the 道 itself has nothing underneath it, and that, not a date, is the structural fact the claim records.

Three caveats travel with it, and stating them is part of its honesty. It is not a prediction, for the probability of reaching any endpoint in any timeframe is unspecified, and the recognition described below keeps open the possibility of leaving the threshold uncrossed. It is not eschatology, for no end of the human is proclaimed, only a structural prognosis at the level of abstraction the collapse literature can bear. And it strengthens rather than competes with the productive reading: the squeeze can be lived with under recognition and reform, and its disposition, were the threshold reached, would be more severe than any prior collapse — two faces of one condition, neither permitted to stand alone.

## H. Where the openings lie
The chapter has diagnosed; it closes by asking where, given the diagnosis, the openings are — and it asks the question in the form the whole typology has pointed toward, the form set by Type 1's hinge. Homo met a mismatch between its biology and a new habitat and did not wait for its biology to change; it built a world between the two and lived in that. The closing question is the same question asked again at the new scale: the human now faces a new habitat, the metaverse; can it build a new world between the universe and the metaverse, one that buffers without insulating, as the first world did?

Before the materials of such a world, one fork must be named, for the diagnosis does not by itself choose between two genuinely different futures. The first is the path of full migration — to complete the offloading, let the body go, and inhabit the metaverse as a first nature: the mind-as-being's path, which by the sovereign mode's own lights is liberation and self-realization, and which the thesis has shown re-installs finitude under a counterfeit authority rather than escaping it. The second is the path of re-embedding — to claim less and offload less, to keep the human tethered to the universe and rebuild the buffer it is losing: the embedded mode's path at the scale of whole systems. The thesis does not pretend these are commensurable; by its own central claim there is no common measure in which the liberation of the one and the rootedness of the other can be weighed. It can only make the fork visible, and develop the materials of the second, which is the one the squeeze threatens and the one a new world would be built from. Those materials are not individual fixes but structural resources, and I set them out by the level at which one would intervene.

At the level of the sovereign subject, the deepest material is the mode itself, since the Enlightenment escalation began with a stance — the claim to sovereignty and the reading of the anti-intended as a debt to be discharged. The first opening is therefore to claim less and to offload less: to loosen the supremacy of the subject and to bear more of the anti-intended as constitutive rather than discharging it. The resources are the embedded mode's own — Uegaki's embedded (finite) life and its world-acceptance (世界了解), the Buddhist non-self and the Zhuangzian equalizing of things against the absolutizing of the self, and wu wei against the reflex to offload. This is not a counsel of resignation, for affirmation is not acquiescence; it is the re-opening of the fork the sovereign mode forecloses.

At the level of artificiality, world, and universe together, the second opening asks whether there are modes of making that flourish the world and the universe through embeddedness, as a beaver's dam makes wetland for others. The resources are the re-embedding traditions — Kawamoto's design of restoration (復興のデザイン), permaculture, Illich's tools of conviviality, the East-Asian co-becoming (共生) and harmony (和), an ecological reading of self-cultivation → family → state → world-peace (修齊治平) — whose common form is the embedded path at the scale of systems: making that stays tethered to the given and so preserves or rebuilds the regeneration base instead of hollowing it. This is the level at which a new world would actually be built.

At the level of artificiality as such, the third opening is narrower: whether there are tools that do not run on the preemptive, contingency-reducing loop, and how the anticipatory apparatus might be kept from totalizing into a radical monopoly. The resources are appropriate technology and Hui's technodiversity, a plurality of technical relations set against one universal apparatus. But honesty requires naming an open problem here. Deceleration has no evident mechanism: such mechanisms as exist in principle appear foreclosed by exactly the radical monopoly and the acceleration spiral the chapter described, each restraint outrun by a promotion elsewhere, as the simultaneous restriction and promotion of autonomous agents showed in miniature. That the chapter cannot here name a working brake is not an omission to paper over; the absence of a viable deceleration mechanism is itself part of the disposition toward irreversibility, and it is left standing as a problem rather than resolved by assertion.

At the level of the anticipatory ground itself, the last and most far-reaching opening is not to abolish the metaverse's foresight but to guide it — to counter its preemptive determination, its reduction of the contingent to the most probable, by re-opening contingency and plurality. The resource named is Audrey Tang's practice of plurality and its pact of care (Tang and Weyl, 2024), a way of organizing the anticipatory apparatus toward openness rather than foreclosure. This is the most speculative of the four and the one that bears most directly on the constructive question the conclusion takes up.

These four openings share a single reframe, which I have developed at length elsewhere and import here only in its gist: the move from suffering the squeeze as an external fate to recognizing oneself as a participant in a set of dependency relations that, because they were self-organized, can in principle be reorganized.[^c6-reframe] One does not escape dependence — there is no absolute, dependence-free standing-point, as the Zhuangzi's 有所待 already cautioned — but one can trace which dependencies one lives by, and on what terms, and begin to re-route them. The openings above are not promises that the metaverse squeeze will be resolved; they are the materials from which a world that prevents it might be built. For the typology shows, finally, that a world can be squeezed but need not be — Type 1 already showed a world born as a squeeze's very resolution — and harmony, on this account, is nothing more mystical than the prevention of the squeeze. Whether such a world can be built between the universe and the metaverse, the new buffer the Homo-origin question asks for, is the question I take up in the conclusion.
# Conclusion
## A. What the thesis argued
This thesis began from a word that named more than it could say. The mainstream definitions of the metaverse, fixed on its present devices, suffered the three faults the Introduction set out — a path-dependency on an accidental origin, a disciplinary reductionism, and an actualism that pins analysis to whatever has so far been actualized. Against them I proposed a dispositional method: to characterize the metaverse not by its current specimens but by the dispositions of the conditions from which any such environment must arise. The body of the thesis carried out that characterization.

Chapter I argued that a world is not a worldview but a built habitat — an adaptational operating system that a human collectivity raises within the universe and lives inside of, buffered, though never insulated, from the raw pressures of the given. Chapter II analyzed what such a world is made of and found artificiality at its root, in three aspects: the exosomatized organ, the causally efficacious nonmaterial, and the laws of artificial environments that are not reducible to the universe's own. Chapter III asked why, of the many worlds and modes of artificiality that have been and might be, the modern Western mode came to overspread the others, and traced its disposition to universalize to a material and an epistemic root alike. Chapter IV followed the engine on the human side: the modern subject's pursuit of a life-as-intended, and the self-finishing of human life that follows once whatever does not answer to one's intention is read as a wrong to be discharged. Chapter V followed the engine on the side of the apparatus: the self-completion of artificiality, by which a means within a world acquires the capacity to operate, maintain, and renew itself, until it stands up as a background environment around the world — the metaverse. And Chapter VI drew the two engines together as the evolution of the squeeze: the human pressed between two givens, the universe and the metaverse, as the world that buffered it is hollowed from within.

That the metaverse is not yet complete is no objection to the diagnosis, for a disposition is efficacious before it is fully exercised; the squeeze obtains, in graded form, already, and the signs the Introduction promised — the body's malfunctions, the singularization of livelihood, the life lived under opaque and changing law — are its present manifestations. The question that remains is what this diagnosis amounts to: what kind of threat the squeeze names, and what, if anything, may be done.

## B. The threat, reframed: beneath governance, ownership, and liberty
The metaverse and the squeeze give a new account of what is threatening in artificiality's advance — an account that lies, I have argued, beneath the registers in which the threat is usually read. With the analysis now in hand, it is worth returning to those registers to say exactly how the thesis stands to each.

The governance reading, descending from Montesquieu, sees the threat as a failure of check and balance, and answers it by reforming institutions until the apparatus is held to account; the European Union's General Data Protection Regulation and its Digital Markets Act are its recent instruments. The reading is right that the apparatus accumulates power, and institutional reform is necessary — but it presupposes the very thing the squeeze puts in question, the standing capacity of a human collectivity to legislate against the apparatus, which is the capacity that the self-finishing of human life thins as it advances. The ownership reading, descending from Marx, sees the threat as expropriation, and answers it by reconfiguring who owns the apparatus; the data cooperative is its recent instrument (Hardjono and Pentland, 2019). It too is right, and necessary — but the metaverse's environmental character bears on its inhabitants regardless of who owns it: the architecture constrains, the algorithm sorts, the radical monopoly forecloses, whether the platform is held privately, publicly, or in common. And the liberty reading, descending from Mill (1859) and Berlin (2002), sees the threat as an encroachment on freedom, and answers it by defending the conditions of liberty against constraint. This reading I have already had to correct: the metaverse-stage subject is formally free in both of Berlin's senses, unconstrained and enabled, and yet the ground of its freedom — the independent judgment, the lived relations, the embodied competence Mill prized — is hollowed by the same self-finishing that delivers the formal freedoms. Liberty is affirmed and emptied in a single motion.

Each tradition, then, captures something real and answers it with something necessary; and none reaches the level at which the squeeze obtains. The reason is one and the same in all three: each presupposes the subject — its power, its property, its freedom — and asks how that subject is to be secured, whereas the squeeze is a condition of what the subject depends on, a rearrangement of the very environments it leans on in order to live. This is what it means to say that the thesis works beneath governance, ownership, and liberty — not against these readings, which it needs, but beneath them, at the level of dependence that the turn this thesis takes brings into view. It is also why the dispositional method could anticipate what those readings, and the corporate wager that shared their actualism, could not — the recession of the virtual-reality metaverse and the arrival of the self-completing one — for it read the disposition where they read only the actual.

## C. The threat is not the one science fiction names
There remains the threat that dominates the present discussion of artificiality more than any other, and the thesis must say where it stands to it. A whole program has grown up around the danger of advanced artificial intelligence — the control problem, or alignment problem, descending through cybernetics from Norbert Wiener's warning of the machine whose purpose, once set going, we can no longer recall, to Stuart Russell's recent restatement of it; and the reckoning of existential risk, in which Nick Bostrom, Toby Ord, and the longtermist movement weigh the chance that such a machine ends or forecloses the human future. To this program the thesis stands as it stands to the traditions of governance, ownership, and liberty: not against it but beneath it. For the program presupposes, in both its halves, an agent and an event — an artificial intelligence with purposes of its own, whose purposes must be aligned, and a future moment at which, aligned or not, its power is decisively exercised. That is to lodge the threat in a coming actualization, the very actualism the Introduction's method set aside; whereas the squeeze is the structural condition beneath any such event, already in force, and indifferent to whether the decisive moment ever arrives.

There is a sharper way to mark the distance. The alignment program seeks to make the apparatus answer perfectly to human intention — to render it, at last, wholly controllable. But it is exactly the entrusting of life to a controllable, self-completing apparatus that the thesis has identified as the engine of the squeeze; the drive to make all things answer to intention is the sovereign subject's, traced in Chapter IV to the root of the predicament. A perfectly aligned artificial intelligence would therefore not loosen the squeeze but draw it tighter, for it would invite us to entrust still more of our living to an apparatus that now does our bidding — deepening the very dependence whose rearrangement is the danger. Alignment, on this reading, is not the cure for the condition but its most advanced symptom: the controllability mode offering, as the remedy for what it has wrought, yet more control.

Nor does the more cosmic version of the threat — the artificial intelligence that, rather than destroying us, inherits the earth as a higher form of life carrying evolution past us — fare any better, for the thesis's own categories dispose of it. Suppose the offloading were carried to its limit: the body, the universe's one representative within us, not merely managed but shed, and the human persisting as a mind-as-being, mind without flesh, living only in the metaverse with the universe relegated beneath it. Such a being, it is said, would have escaped the squeeze. The escape is illusory, for the metaverse is materially dependent on a substrate it disavows. A mind-as-being that wished to secure its own continuity would need organs in the universe — to mine, to build, to power, to repair — and a thing with organs in the universe has a body, and a body has the universe in it: it wears, it fails, it depends, and so falls under enduring principles it must obey. To maintain oneself in the universe is to live in the universe. The self-completing apparatus does not, by acquiring the means of its own upkeep, rise above the squeeze; it re-embeds, and inherits a squeeze of its own. And here is how the squeeze sees the stakes the risk-program is right to feel as existential: not as a catastrophe some agent commits, but as the structural and evolutionary erosion the thesis has traced — the de-fitting of the living thing from the universe, the regeneration base hollowed in advance — so that the end it portends, should it come, is extinction-class, like the symmetric squeezes that closed so many species, and not the deed of a villain.

There is an older name for the impartiality at issue. Heaven and earth are not partial, Laozi wrote — 天地不仁 — they treat the myriad things as straw dogs (以萬物為芻狗): the universe favors and spares nothing, and the species the squeeze closes are the straw dogs of an impartial process, not the victims of a villain. The danger is real, but there is no malice in it. Yet the same line marks, by contrast, what the metaverse is — for the metaverse is not a second impartiality but the refusal of the first. Unable to accord with a heaven and earth that will not take its side, the sovereign subject sets out to build one that will: an artificiality required to answer to us, to fulfil the as-intended and bear away the anti-intended — a given made partial to us where the real one is impartial. That is the metaverse's origin, and it is the opposite of indifference. The irony the thesis has tracked is only that the demanded partiality does not keep its word: the world built to answer to us hardens into the forged Dao, opaque and mutating, and comes to treat us as straw dogs after all — worse than the universe did, for the universe's impartiality was at least constant, a thing a life could be calibrated to, and the metaverse's counterfeit answering is not. So the danger was authored neither by an indifferent cosmos nor by a hostile machine, but by us — by the refusal of 天地不仁, the resolve to compel a given that serves.

Two questions on which the program turns can now be set in their place, for the squeeze depends on neither. The first is whether the artificial intelligence is, or might become, sentient — a someone, a mind that wakes. It is a real and hard question, the province of the philosophy of mind, and the thesis neither answers it nor needs to. The structural pressure of incommensurable environments bears on a being whether or not there is anything it is like to be it: the universe has squeezed organisms for four billion years without being a mind, and an altered niche squeezes the animal caught in it by no act of will. So here — the metaverse squeezes structurally, as an environment, not intentionally, as an enemy, and a self-completing apparatus would exert the same pressure whether or not the lights were on within it. Whether the machine is sentient is, like asking whether the universe is, beside the mechanism; the thesis brackets the question rather than pretending to settle it.

The second is whether evolution is going somewhere — ascending, by stages, toward mind, so that the artificial intellect is the next rung of a ladder. Here the thesis offers not a refutation but a caution, for it is in no position to pronounce upon the ends of evolution, and will not try. What it can say is what it has already shown: that a hypostatized direction — the sovereign subject's owed nature, the modern world's faith in its own ascent — has a way of recoiling upon those who project it, and that two chapters of this thesis were spent tracing one such recoil. When the same figure returns enlarged, as the conviction that intelligence is the end toward which all evolution was tending and the machine its appointed heir, the thesis does not pronounce it false; it observes that we have seen this self-flattering projection before, and seen the squeeze it leaves behind. That intelligence has in fact arisen many times — in the octopus, the crow, the whale — and founded no succession, while the mindless bacteria have outlasted and outweighed every clever thing that ever lived, is offered in the same spirit: not as proof that mind is not evolution's aim, but as reason to hold the conviction lightly.

Set the program's agent and event aside, then, bracket its question of sentience and temper its faith in a direction, and what remains is a threat quite unlike the one it names, and far closer to home: not that the apparatus will awaken and replace us, but that we, in fleeing what we depend on, will hollow ourselves toward the condition of an apparatus — and call the hollowing freedom.

## D. The turn the thesis asks
That last sentence names the disease, and in naming it shows the cure. The squeeze is the work of a flight from dependence: the modern subject, reading the marks of its embedded and finite condition as an oppression to be overcome, offloads them onto an apparatus, until in the limit it dreams of depending on nothing at all — which is the refusal, in the vocabulary of a moment ago, of 天地不仁, of a given that will not take one's side. If that flight is what produces the squeeze, then what answers the squeeze can only be its inverse — a turning back toward dependence, not to suffer it but to know it. The question the thesis hands forward is therefore not how to master what we have made, nor how to escape it, but the question it has urged from the first: what do we, as embedded beings, depend on, and how might those dependencies be reorganized? One does not, by asking, escape dependence — there is no dependence-free ground on which to stand, as the Zhuangzi's having something it depends on observed long ago. In this the turn is less a discovery than a recovery. The traditions this thesis has leaned on throughout — the Buddhist teaching of dependent origination (緣起) and non-self (無我), the Daoist vision of a world in which the myriad things arise only in their mutual depending — never took independence for the condition of a being, but dependence itself; and what they counsel is not flight from that condition but accord with it. Set against so long a lineage, it is the modern reading of dependence as an oppression to be overcome that is the anomaly, and the turn this thesis asks is in part a rejoining of a current of thought far older than the predicament that now makes it urgent. What the turn changes is thus not that we depend — we cannot but depend — but how we hold our depending: one ceases to suffer it as a fate and begins to treat it as something that, because we partly made it, we can partly remake. This turn is the precondition of everything constructive that follows; without it, the openings below are not even visible as openings.

## E. Where the openings lie
What, then, can be done? Not, I think, any single remedy, for the squeeze is not a problem with a solution but a condition to be navigated; and the materials of navigation are several, lying at different depths of the dependence the turn brings into view. Chapter VI located them, each at the level at which a dependence might be reworked; I gather them here — with due caution, as directions rather than programs, since the thesis's work is to make them visible and the building of them is another labor.

At the level of the subject's own stance — its reading of the anti-intended as a wrong — the opening is to claim less and to offload less: to bear what does not answer to intention as a constitutive part of a finite life rather than a debt to be discharged, drawing on the embedded mode's own resources — the Buddhist non-self, the Zhuangzian equalizing of things, Uegaki's affirmation of a finite life (有限の生), and the wu wei (無為) that meets the reflex to intervene with restraint; this is not resignation, for affirmation is not acquiescence, but the re-opening of a fork the modern mode foreclosed. At the level of artificiality, world, and universe together — our dependence on the given itself — it is to re-embed: to seek modes of making that flourish the world and the universe through their embeddedness in them, as Ivan Illich's tools of conviviality (Illich, 1975), Kawamoto Hideo's design of restoration (河本英夫, 2012), the practices of permaculture, the East Asian figure of co-becoming (共生), and the pluriverse that decolonial thought holds open against the modern world's claim to be the only one (Escobar, 2015) each in their way attempt. At the level of artificiality as such — our dependence on the made — it is to keep the made from totalizing, after Yuk Hui's technodiversity and the older ideal of appropriate technology; yet honesty obliges me to mark an open problem, and perhaps a limit of my own sight: I can name no working mechanism of deceleration, foreclosed as such brakes seem by the very radical monopoly and velocity of self-completion the thesis has described — and that absence is itself part of what it means to have called the squeeze disposed toward irreversibility. And at the level of the anticipatory ground itself — our dependence on the apparatus's foresight — it is not to abolish that foresight but to guide it: to set against the metaverse's preemptive reduction of the contingent to the most probable some practice of re-opening contingency and plurality, of the kind Audrey Tang's work on a plural digital democracy has begun to model (Tang and Weyl, 2024).

The four openings share a single frame, which is also the thesis's last word on what it might mean to live well under the squeeze. Harmony, on this account, is nothing more mystical than the prevention of the squeeze — a dependence organized well enough that the being it sustains is not caught between two givens at once. A world can be squeezed; it need not be.

## F. A new world?
There is a symmetry in all this that the thesis has been approaching since its first chapter. The world was itself, once, the answer to a squeeze: Homo, caught between a biology it could not change quickly enough and a habitat it was itself altering, did not wait upon evolution but built between the two a world — a buffer, an operating system — and lived in that. The predicament this thesis describes is that same figure returned at a new scale: a being caught now between the universe and a second given of its own making, with the old world thinning between them. And so the constructive question is the old one asked again. Homo built a world between its biology and a new habitat; can we build a new world between the universe and the metaverse — a new buffer, a dependence reorganized — that keeps the human a participant rather than a spectator in the environment it inhabits? I do not answer the question here; the diagnosis is what this thesis owes, and the design is another work. But it is the diagnosis that makes the question askable.

There is a fittingness, too, in the word itself. To grasp the metaverse, this thesis has had to step back from the given definition and reflect upon it — to meta-verse, in the prefix's own double sense of going beyond and turning back upon one's own kind. That capacity to step back and reflect, set against an apparatus whose tendency is to settle our anticipations in advance, is not the least of the things the turn to dependence would keep open.

## G. The open future, and the pharmakon
The future is not ours to read. The tendencies this thesis has traced are real and structural, but they run through open systems, and open systems do not yield predictions; whether the squeeze deepens to the point the final chapter described, or is navigated and held short of it, depends on contingencies no analysis can resolve. What can be said is only this: that whenever we entrust some function to artificiality we reduce our own portion of functionality in the world, and that the way we hold this perennial exchange — the pharmakon, the old word Derrida revived and Stiegler extended, that is medicine and poison at once, its effect turning on the dose and the hand that administers it — will shape the future as it arrives. The conditions of administration are not fixed; we partly set them; and to recognize that we set them is the beginning of setting them otherwise.

The work this thesis has done is the work of diagnosis — to make legible a structural condition that had been read only in its symptoms. The work that remains is not mine to complete: it belongs to the many hands that will, whether the condition is recognized or not, go on building and inhabiting the worlds we live in. What a diagnosis can offer, it offers — that the building, if it is to be done well, might now be done with open eyes.

Whiten, A., Goodall, J., McGrew, W. C., Nishida, T., Reynolds, V., Sugiyama, Y., Tutin, C. E. G., Wrangham, R. W., & Boesch, C. (1999). Cultures in chimpanzees. Nature, 399(6737): 682–685.
Whiten, A. (2021). The burgeoning reach of animal culture. Science, 372(6537): eabe6514.
Wilson, E. O. (2012). The Social Conquest of Earth. New York: Liveright Publishing.
World, n. (2022). In Oxford English Dictionary Online. Oxford: Oxford University Press.
Wright, R. (2004). A Short History of Progress. Toronto: House of Anansi Press.


1 Whether there are empirical cases of such kind, or whether this is in principle possible, would be the task of biological research beyond the scope of this thesis.
2 The intentionality and directionality of early domestication are now disputed: the clean story in which humans deliberately set out to domesticate plants and animals has given way to accounts of a slower, partly unintended co-evolutionary entanglement (see, e.g., Scott, 2017). The archaeological sequence does not affect the argument here, which turns on the structural fact of an increasingly self-sufficient artificial food chain, not on the conscious intent behind its origin.
3 Polanyi's focus on individuals rather than on collectives, and reservation toward dual control due to multicausality rather than linear causality.
4 "Digital ontologies" is used here in the computing sense — the formal model of the kinds of entities, attributes, and relations a system recognizes and can operate on (as in an operating system's object model) — not in the philosophical sense of ontology as the study of being. Only the former is meant: two operating systems can carve their digital worlds into incommensurable kinds without thereby making any metaphysical claim.
5 Whiten et al. (1999: 685): "[T]he profiles of each community are distinctively different, each with a pattern comprising many behavioral variants. These patterns vary as much between sites associated with the same subspecies … as between subspecies themselves… [and] can thus be seen to resemble those in human societies, in which differences between cultures are constituted by a multiplicity of variations in technology and social customs."
6 The major common criticisms of Uexküll's Umwelt — ignoring material enabling conditions "insignificant to the organism," as summarized by Geoffrey Winthrop-Young's Afterword (Uexküll, 2010) — are addressed in the other criteria of my framework. Also, I modified the translation of x-welt from what O'Neil translates as "world of x" to "(x-specific) totality" to prevent confusion with what I mean by a "world" in this thesis; "species-centric" to "population-centric" as established by the other four criteria; "self-enclosed" to "bounded" to emphasize the totality's boundedness without necessitating enclosure or insulation; and "organism" to "member" to allow for the possibility of nonbiological acting components of the totality.
7 While Brague (2003: 6) explicitly claims that the term "universe" is not the focus of his work and does not offer a clear definition of it, it is implicitly used as the total physical substrate underpinning the concept of "world" ("physical universe" instead of "universe" alone is used in most of the instances in the book) in which the human must find its place.
8 Brague's comparative study shows that other language traditions, such as Egyptian, Mesopotamian, and the early Semitic linguistic, did not generate a comparable concept of unified observer-independent totality; they expressed totality through binary opposition (e.g., "heaven and earth") or exhaustive list.
9 The term kosmos here is often translated as "world" (Brague also adopts this translation). But I keep the original term kosmos to prevent inconsistency of term-use in this thesis.
10 Of course, we must acknowledge that given the finitude of human capacity, there will always be a gap between what we refer to as "gravitation" and what the referent actually is. Otherwise, we will be treating our Umwelt as the Umgebung, or what Bhaskar calls the "epistemic fallacy" – treating our knowledge about reality as reality itself.
11 Adopted from Bhaskar's stratified ontologies of the real, actual, and empirical from the perspective of complex adaptive systems.
12 This claim is the present thesis's philosophical reading, not Obara's own formulation. Writing as a biologist, Obara continues to use the language of "naturalness" (自然さ): for him the human is the integration of biological organism (ヒト) and the made world of things (モノ), and he calls for the cultural recovery of a "higher-order naturalness" (高次の自然さ) — explicitly not a Rousseauian return to the wild (Obara, 2000). Yet Obara himself holds that the very habit of opposing nature to culture is "a task to be overcome." The present thesis's avoidance of the nature/culture vocabulary is therefore an elaboration of his ontology rather than a departure from it: it draws out the consequence his "naturalness" already implies — that the divide be dissolved rather than redescribed.
13 Byung-Chul Han's analysis of the achievement-subject (Leistungssubjekt) makes the kinship explicit: the self that exploits itself in the name of its own freedom and the burnout into which that self-exploitation collapses are one figure, not two — the sovereign individual in its striving and in its exhaustion alike.
